SECTION: Section 6; Page 27; Column 2; Magazine Desk

LENGTH: 4386 words

HEADLINE: Where the Money Washes Up

BYLINE: By Steve Lohr; Steve Lohr is a financial reporter for The New York Times.

 BODY:
IN ANY FLIGHT FROM MIAMI TO the Cayman Islands, passengers can usually be divided into two groups. There are the vacationers, in their T-shirts and sneakers, on their way to a Caribbean holiday of sun and scuba diving. Then there are the suits. With Rolex watches on their wrists and leather attache cases by their feet, they barely glance up from their papers to take in the palm-fringed isles below as the plane nears its destination.
The button-down crowd has been commuting regularly to do business in a tax-and-regulatory haven that, by some measures, now rivals Switzerland. Three slivers of sand tucked south of Cuba and northwest of Jamaica, the Cayman Islands are an unlikely location for a world-class financial center. Mere flyspecks on most maps, they are so out of the way that Christopher Columbus discovered them by accident when his flotilla was blown off course on his final voyage to the New World in 1503. For centuries afterward, the Caymans were a hideaway for adventurers like Edward Teach, also known as Blackbeard the Pirate, who hid ill-gotten loot on the remote isles. 
 There are still stretches on the islands -- Grand Cayman, Cayman Brac and Little Cayman -- that are postcard-perfect vistas come to life, pristine coral sands overlooking turquoise waters, disturbed only by the occasional fisherman or diver. But this British crown colony's real claim to prosperity and distinction -- as the Geneva of the Caribbean -- lies in what its champions call regulatory flexibility: scant regulation, strict confidentiality laws and the absence of taxes.
The financial district in Georgetown, the capital, boasts the highest density of banks and fax machines in the world. The 548 banking outposts here -- a who's who of global banking -- hold assets of some $400 billion, a fivefold jump in the past decade, placing it just behind Switzerland as an international banking center. There are, however, no skyscrapers or concrete canyons in Georgetown, for most of the banks are so-called plaque banks, or booking centers -- that is, loans or deposits are recorded on the books in the Caymans. Booking centers require no vaults, no tellers, no security guards, not even a bank building. They traffic in electronic debits and credits in a computer, which is where most money is in modern finance. The banking operation in the Caymans can be little more than a brass plate, with the paper work handled by a sponsor bank with offices in Georgetown and most of the transactions done by fax and phone from elsewhere.
American banks have booking centers in the Caymans mainly to sidestep the requirement by the Federal Reserve that a small portion of deposits held in the United States be placed with the Fed each night in a so-called reserve account that does not bear interest. This regulation helps the Fed manage the nation's monetary policy, by giving it some control over the total amount of funds in the American banking system each day. But banks make money by putting their money to use, earning interest, constantly. To avoid the reserve requirement, a bank can ask its corporate customers to place deposits in an offshore office of the bank. Thus, placing deposits on the books in the Caymans reduces a bank's costs. Because a major bank does billions of dollars' worth of international transactions each year, the savings, though small in percentage terms, can run to many millions. It is entirely legal, and one of the fine points of modern global finance.
Of the Caymans' registered banks, only 68 have offices and staff in Georgetown. In fact, the financial district's office buildings are five stories or less, owing to an old statute declaring that "no building should be higher than the tallest tree." The building edict is one of the few regulatory curbs on business.
Over the past decade, offshore financial centers like the Caymans have benefited enormously from an explosion in the size and complexity of international finance, as country after country has eased curbs on money flows and technology has enabled a banker to send funds around the world with a few taps on a computer keyboard. Offshore centers are now an integral part of the global economy, with as much as half of the world's money residing in or passing through them, according to Anthony S. Ginsberg, a Los Angeles-based financial consultant and expert on tax havens.
There are roughly four dozen of these financial entrepots, ranging from venerable European centers like Switzerland and Liechtenstein to newcomers like the South Pacific islands of Nauru and Vanuatu. The Cayman Islands are a particularly appealing way station partly because the colony is politically stable, efficient and close to the United States.
For individuals, virtually anyone can still establish his or her own shell company for a few thousand dollars in legal fees, open a local bank account and, because the required disclosure is minimal and business operates behind a legal wall of strict secrecy, no one need know about the company or what funds are stashed there. The few slips of paper that constitute the company records may be held in the office of a Cayman lawyer.
According to these lawyers, a hefty portion of the 23,500 companies and trusts registered in the Cayman Islands have been established by Americans who, for whatever reason, want to place funds outside the reach of American law. Americans, of course, are supposed to report overseas accounts to the Internal Revenue Service.
Like blue-chip corporations and highly reputable banks that use the Caymans legitimately to reduce taxes and expenses on their international business, shady operators of all kinds -- Americans included -- have grown increasingly adept at exploiting these offshore havens. Brian M. Bruh, director of the Financial Crimes Enforcement Network, a 200-person unit in the United States Treasury Department, recalls that not long ago a fraud investigation was a local affair. "Now a typical fraud case is not only nationwide but worldwide. And the offshore centers are frequently used by the crooks."
When Federal agents arrested the investment banker Dennis B. Levine in 1986, no one was surprised that days before he had visited the Caymans to set up a dummy corporation and open a bank account, where he hoped to deposit $10 million in insider stock-trading profits. When Lieut. Col. Oliver L. North needed a money-collecting front company in the arms-for-hostages caper known as the Iran-contra affair, it was set up in the Cayman Islands.
These islands also played a key role in what has been called the biggest scam in financial history. Agha Hasan Abedi, the founder of the Bank of Credit and Commerce International (B.C.C.I.), channeled many of the bank's illegal transactions through its Cayman subsidiaries, including bogus loans and payoffs to the bank's front men, who secretly bought First American Bankshares Inc., the largest bank holding company in Washington. Although it had offices in 70 countries, B.C.C.I. had key operations in the money havens of the Caymans and Luxembourg, beyond the reach of any central bank.
B.C.C.I.'s collapse last year has brought financial havens like the Cayman Islands under fresh scrutiny. In testimony before the House Banking Committee last fall, E. Gerald Corrigan, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York and head of the Bank of International Settlement's committee on global banking supervision in Basel, Switzerland, said the B.C.C.I. scandal highlighted, and left unresolved, the "least common denominator aspect" of international finance -- that is, money flows to the places that regulate least.
WILLIAM S. WALKER, A trim, silver-haired 65-year-old, is the elder statesman of the Cayman Islands' banking and legal fraternity. Raised in Barbados and British Guiana and educated at Cambridge, he arrived in the Caymans in 1963 to seek his fortune.
"In the first few years, there was absolutely nothing going on here," recalls Walker, seated in his spacious Georgetown law office. "It was a fishing village."
Communications and travel were difficult, he says, but the mosquitoes were the real headache. Early visitors often fled the islands on the first boat or plane out, driven away by the insects. Caymanians used smoke pots to repel the bugs and branches to swat them. It was all a losing battle until an entomologist, Marco Giglioli, arrived from British Guiana to eradicate the mosquitoes by the diking of swamps, aerial spraying and fogging. (When Giglioli died in 1984, he was honored with a state funeral.)
For the Cayman Islands, there are two crucial dates in the post-colonial history of the Caribbean.
In 1962, the Caymans, a dependency of Jamaica since 1863, chose crown colony status after Jamaica declared its independence from Britain. British crown colonies -- like Hong Kong and Bermuda -- make their own laws and regulations. Although London appoints the governors and the Foreign Office handles the colonies' foreign policy, Britain's central bank, the Bank of England, has no direct control over financial regulation in the crown colonies.
In 1973, the Bahamas, then the main financial center in the Caribbean, was granted independence. To tighten regulations, the Prime Minister, Lynden Pindling, revoked some bank licenses and expatriate work permits. This, plus talk of nationalizing banks, scared off many financial institutions, which were welcomed in the Cayman Islands. By the 1970's, the Caymans -- already a popular tourist destination and, patterning themselves after the Bahamas and their laissez-faire capitalism, a popular tax haven -- had a new jet runway and improved communications. The territory was on its way to becoming a big-league money haven, which, by itself, is "a shortcut to prosperity," notes Walter H. Diamond, an economist and adviser to the United Nations on tax treaties and free-trade zones.
In about two decades, the islands have been transformed from a fishing-and-seafaring society to a modern service economy based on finance and tourism. (About 600,000 visitors arrive each year by cruise ships and jets.) And capitalism's spoils have trickled down.
The poverty that afflicts other parts of the Caribbean isn't much in evidence in the Caymans, which has a population of nearly 26,000, including Caymanians of mixed African and European descent, and expatriate workers from Jamaica, Britain, the United States, Central America and elsewhere. The islands boast a per-capita income of roughly $20,000, higher than that of Britain, yet the cost of living is high as well. The locals -- who take for granted such amenities as cars, air-conditioning, fine restaurants, education, international travel and satellite television -- often shuttle up to Miami for shopping trips.
William Arley Chisholm, a Caymanian banker, has experienced the economic miracle firsthand. Born in 1960, he grew up with his seven brothers and sisters in a small house with no electricity, no plumbing and no telephone. Water was drawn from a well. He walked eight miles to and from school. At night, he studied by the light of a kerosene lamp or went to bed right after sunset.
Today, Chisholm lives with his wife and two children in a condominium on Grand Cayman that is equipped with the full complement of modern conveniences: television, videocassette recorder, stereo, dishwasher and so on. He is an executive at the Bank of Nova Scotia Trust Company's operations in the Caymans. The Bank of Nova Scotia has a local consumer bank in Georgetown, as well as a sizable international operation offering a full range of financial services, including foreign-currency trades and securities transactions, to wealthy individuals who prefer a regulatory haven.
As manager of the data processing for both aspects of the bank's business, Chisholm supervises the work of 15 people. He drives a Japanese sedan and has traveled the world. His one concern at work these days seems to be where the fast track leads next. "I've kind of broken the rules at the bank in terms of the speed with which I've moved up," he says.
In the Caymans, Chisholm's story is not a one-in-a-million Horatio Alger tale, but a representative example of how the lives of many bright, young Caymanians -- who represent nearly two-thirds of the population -- have improved since the Caymans became a financial center. "By the late 1980's we created a boom the likes of which few small territories -- or large countries, for that matter -- have experienced," says Vassel Johnson, the former financial secretary and one of the principal architects of the Cayman Islands' development program.
The islands' rapid development has not, however, erased colonial class structures. Although many of the senior civil servants are Caymanian, the top bankers and lawyers tend to be white expatriates mainly from Britain or its territories but also from the United States, Canada and elsewhere. They are the ones who can afford to live in the upmarket elegance of West Bay or the northern section of Seven Mile Beach, along the west coast of Grand Cayman. This is an enclave of luxury homes, condominiums and tennis-and-yacht clubs with names like the Victoria House, the Heritage Club and the Commonwealth, which sports a sign declaring that its condominiums start "from $1.225 million" each.
The explanation for the social divisions is partly generational. The islands' upwardly mobile are young Caymanians like Chisholm who have been given a level of education and opportunity that their parents didn't have. But they have yet to break into the upper ranks of the banking and legal fraternity. They tend to live in more modest homes, some in enclaves like Bodden Town on Grand Cayman; others live closer to the stretch of Seven Mile Beach near Georgetown that resembles a Florida resort strip with gas stations, fast-food outlets, supermarkets and bars surrounding the cluster of hotels like Ramada Inn, Holiday Inn and Days Inn.
THE OFFICES OF bankers, lawyers and Government officials in the Cayman Islands all seem to have been decorated by the same person, who ordered the same pictures of Queen Elizabeth II and the same 19th-century English prints of Westminster Abbey and Big Ben. If the offices are similar, so is the message from their occupants: the unsavory reputation of the Caymans is an outdated stereotype.
"An offshore center is suspect from the word go," says Thomas Jefferson, the colony's financial secretary, "and there could have been a lot of drug money lodged in the Cayman Islands in the early 1980's. But those days are gone. Yes, we're offshore, but we're not here to collect dirty money."
In the old days, when business was scarce, banking practices were more relaxed, admits Peter A. Tomkins, chairman of the Cayman National Bank. "But there's so much legitimate business out there now," says Tomkins, a graying, craggy-featured 61-year-old who has been a banker on the islands since 1969. "To get involved in the shady stuff, running that risk, just doesn't make sense anymore."
A new customer's references, for example, are checked more closely than in the past. Tomkins says he turns away customers who don't pass muster, like people with sketchy credentials who want to open an account and quickly make multimillion-dollar deposits in the Caymans. Sometimes, he has specific information from banking colleagues that prompts misgivings about the source of a prospective client's wealth, but sometimes more subjective standards are applied. "If a guy walks in here with one, two or three gold neck chains, forget it."
For American law-enforcement officials, the Caymans began to be a problem in 1966. That year the colony, to enhance its status as a money haven, withdrew from tax treaties agreed to between Jamaica and Western industrialized countries, which required the islands to supply information on demand to other treaty nations. Soon the Caymans began to blossom as a tax haven and was a favorite of American tax dodgers. The Internal Revenue Service wasn't happy about it, and countered by serving subpoenas on Cayman-based bankers whenever they entered the United States.
The Caymans responded in 1976 by enacting the Confidential Relationships Preservation Law, which made it a crime for banks or local officials to disclose information to nosy foreign governments or investigators. The advisers to drug barons viewed the 1976 secrecy law as a money launderer's charter, and many of them hid their profits in the Caymans.
The United States stepped up pressure on the islands in the early 1980's, insisting that the Cayman Government address the issue of money laundering in drug cases. In 1984, the Caymans caved in, deciding that maintaining friendly relations with Washington and cleaning up its image outweighed the risk of losing some business because it was opening a tiny crack in its secrecy rules. The colony signed a narcotics agreement with the United States, allowing cooperation on drug cases. Two years later, the two Governments signed a broader Mutual Legal Assistance Treaty, which was ratified in 1988. The assistance treaty allows the United States Government to request information from Cayman banks on specific drug cases and white-collar crimes like bank fraud.
"But it can't just be a fishing expedition," says Alan B. Ratcliffe, the commissioner of the Royal Cayman Islands Police, a big, deep-voiced man who was formerly the deputy chief constable of the English county of Cambridgeshire. "The American investigation has to be at an advanced stage before they can make proper request for information from the Caymans. And it doesn't include tax offenses because there are no tax offenses here."
By the end of last year, there were 30 United States requests for information under the legal-assistance treaty, says Ratcliffe. Of the total, 14 were drug cases and 16 fraud cases, including the one involving B.C.C.I.
In 1990, Cayman bankers agreed to a code of conduct that calls for banks to refuse any suspicious cash deposits of more than $10,000, the amount that the United States sets as a threshold to prevent money laundering. Local businesses like supermarkets, for example, might regularly make deposits of more than $10,000, but "suitcase merchants" -- those arriving at banks with attache cases full of cash -- are another matter.
The banking code, however, does not have the force of law. A bank willing to accept large cash deposits is the white-collar criminal's best friend, because once funds are in the banking system they can be sent electronically to anywhere in the world in seconds, making it extremely difficult to trace the source or the recipient of dirty money.
Other banking standards have been tightened as well. Since 1989, individuals could no longer be granted banking licenses. The personal bank is a staple of some offshore centers and the perfect refuge for crooks seeking to hide their financial manipulations. "The person who wants a private little bank for his own purposes does not fit into what the Cayman Islands is about these days," says John Atkinson, who recently finished a two-year stint as bank inspector for the Caymans.
In fact, the Cayman Government has become much tougher in granting bank licenses. The power to issue or deny a license is the Government's main means of quality control because, although it monitors financial reports, it has no corps of inspectors to go into banks and pore over records. In 1988, for example, when American troops invaded Panama, several Panamanian banks tried to relocate in the Caymans to escape the turmoil. Their requests were rebuffed. And B.C.C.I., which set up its Georgetown operations in the mid-1970's, would not be allowed in today, says Atkinson, because it was a new bank set up to avoid comprehensive supervision by a central bank. "Today, the Caymans want to be a home only to established banks that have cut their teeth in other countries."
CITIBANK, BANK OF America, Deutsche Bank, Barclays and most every other well-known international bank are represented in the Cayman Islands for two key reasons. First, many institutions use the Caymans as a booking center. The second main attraction is the colony's private banking. Private banking is the most attentive service the banking profession can offer the world's wealthy -- individuals who typically have at least $5 million for banks to protect and nurture. The service is one-to-one, and often more than money is involved. A private banker may be hedging currencies in the morning for a Latin American industrialist, and in the afternoon arranging for the client's daughter to attend a private school in Switzerland.
Most major banks have private banking divisions, staffed by small teams of cosmopolitan specialists, whose notion of banking is a world away from tellers' windows and cash machines. Confidentiality is assured and discretion guaranteed. The rich, understandably, want to keep their funds in a tax-free jurisdiction like the Cayman Islands.
"But the main reason people put their money here is that it provides a safe haven for their nest eggs, beyond the reach of governments that may be politically unstable," explains an American private banker in Georgetown who spoke only on condition of anonymity. Many of his clients are from South America, Asia, the Far East and the Middle East. The accounts are in dollars, so if developing-country clients violated currency laws to get their money out they did it before they showed up on his doorstep. In the Caymans, his bank does not handle accounts for Americans. "It's not illegal, but there is no valid business reason for a U.S. citizen to be here."
Thousands of Americans think otherwise. Many of the companies registered in the Caymans are personal trusts set up by Americans. These trusts are another form of safe haven, insuring that creditors, spouses, business partners and other potential litigants can't get at your money.
Denis A. Kleinfeld, a Miami lawyer, has set up more than 200 offshore trusts, in the Cayman Islands and elsewhere, for doctors, dentists, lawyers, small-business owners and others in the past three years. Offshore trusts, in Kleinfeld's view, are merely a prudent hedge against the vagaries of the American legal system. "The hallmark of the American courts is chaos, havoc and distress," Kleinfeld contends. "You go in a pig and you come out a sausage. People are moving their assets offshore to protect themselves."
Lawyers in the business say the practice of setting up offshore trusts is aboveboard, as long as the offshore accounts are filed with the Internal Revenue Service. But for certain Americans, the temptation to keep their Cayman accounts from the I.R.S. is irresistible.
Ed Pankau, a Houston private investigator and a former I.R.S. special agent, has tracked down several tax-evasion cases through Georgetown, including a non-swimming accountant who smuggled cash into the Caymans in scuba tanks and a pornographer who deposited funds in a Cayman bank in the name of his 86-year-old mother-in-law.
"The Cayman Islands are the location of choice for the wise-guy businessman who wants to hide a few hundred thousand dollars," says Pankau, who is currently chasing a former Texas savings-and-loan executive who spirited funds away into Cayman bank accounts. "A lot of the S.&L. bad guys used the Caymans. It was close, and they could party while they hid their money there."
IN THEIR EFFORTS to monitor the global movement of funds through money havens, the United States has signed tax-information exchange agreements with several offshore countries and territories in the Caribbean, including Barbados, Jamaica, Bermuda and Grenada. In exchange for their cooperation, Washington has said it will encourage American investment in those places.
Under these agreements, the signatories would share information and assistance on matters like tax fraud. However, says Bruce Zagaris, a Washington lawyer who is an expert on international criminal law, "the key secrecy jurisdictions like the Cayman Islands and the Bahamas refused to lift the veil."
Potentially the most significant piece of legislation to combat money laundering through offshore havens is the so-called Kerry amendment, a provision of the Anti-Drug Abuse Act of 1988. It instructs the Federal Government to reach enforcement agreements with offshore havens, requiring them to record any United States dollar transactions exceeding $10,000. Such records would be made available to American law-enforcement officials.
Under the Kerry amendment, harsh penalties may be imposed against jurisdictions that do not comply, like cutting off their banks from the United States dollar-clearing and electronic-transfer system. For a dollar-based center like the Cayman Islands, that could amount to a financial death threat. But Washington has been slow to negotiate enforcement arrangements that might upset foreign governments and drive business away from American banks at a time when there are worries about the financial strength of the banking industry.
John Kerry, the Democratic Senator from Massachusetts who wrote the amendment, contends that the United States has the right to track the funds from the Caymans, a dollar haven, because that money eventually flows through the American payments system.
"Our Government has to decide whether it is serious about protecting the integrity of the American financial system," says Kerry, "and there should be penalties for these unregulated offshore centers that won't play by the rules." His approach is the most draconian, but in the wake of the B.C.C.I. scandal, the central banks of the major industrialized countries could well see it as their duty to prohibit their commercial banks from having branches in places that do not meet certain international regulatory standards.
In Georgetown, however, there seems to be little concern about such moves. Thomas Jefferson, the financial secretary, for one, doesn't believe that either the United States or Britain would ever take the kind of steps that would endanger the livelihood of the Cayman Islands. "I doubt you could impose any regulation that would really hurt us, short of a worldwide and permanent tax repeal."

HEADLINE: HOBOKEN JOURNAL;
How a 'Bunch of Amateurs' Learned to Fight City Hall

BYLINE: By JAY ROMANO

DATELINE: HOBOKEN

 BODY:
IN a church basement here on Washington Street is a scale model of the City of Hoboken -- its churches, stores, streets and houses reproduced in painstaking detail. Depicted along the eastern boundary, where the city meets the Hudson River, is a tree-lined waterfront park that stretches from one end of town to the other.
The model represents the collective vision of an eclectic mix of Hoboken residents -- artists, writers, homemakers, truck drivers, lawyers, business people -- that calls itself the Coalition for a Better Waterfront. It also represents the group's alternative to an official city plan to develop 32 acres of the city's most precious real estate. 
 For more than two years, the group has been a thorn in the side of city officials who favor the plan. With little more than spunk, dedication and seemingly boundless love for this mile-square city, the 150-member group has managed to thwart the officials' efforts at nearly every turn.
On Tuesday, voters will make their third trip to the polls to decide their waterfront's fate. This time, they will be asked to accept or reject the city's scaled-down version of an earlier plan. The revised plan, officials say, represents the last chance to recapture the economic potential of the waterfront.
 
'I Got Tired of Hearing That'
 "When I first moved here, people would say that you can't fight City Hall, and I just got tired of hearing that," said Augusta Przygoda, a Hoboken resident since 1976 and a founding member of the coalition.
The grass-roots movement began in 1989 when Patrick Pasculli ran for Mayor on a platform that included a plan to lease city-owned waterfront land to the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey for development. The plan called for construction of two public parks and 3.2 million square feet of commercial and residential space -- including a 33-story office tower and hotel -- on two 5.5-acre piers and four city blocks at the southern tip of Hoboken.
In that election, the citizens' group sponsored a referendum that would designate nearly all the area a parkland that could never be developed. Mr. Pasculli won the election, and the referendum was defeated. After that election, the newly formed coalition, which favored some development, started collecting signatures to block the Mayor from signing the agreement with the Port Authority. In the meantime, the authority made a $7 million payment to the city in anticipation of proceeding with the plan. The city quickly spent the $7 million.
"That money was used to bridge a severe budget deficit that had been created by a previous administration," Mayor Pasculli said. "We also used it to lower taxes and to improve municipal services."
Meanwhile, the coalition had managed to gather enough signatures to place the issue on the ballot. The city fought the effort, citing its belief that state law reserved for municipal officials the power to make land-use decisions.
The coalition filed a lawsuit to force the city to put the issue before the voters. The suit made it to the State Supreme Court, and the group won.
"We were fighting them in the Supreme Court and at the same time preparing for the election," Ms. Przygoda said. "We were all a bunch of amateurs. I still don't know how we pulled it off."
The court ordered that the referendum take place, and in July 1990, the coalition defeated the city plan by a scant 12 votes out of about 9,000 cast.
Officials were in a quandary. With the plan dead, the city would have to pay back the Port Authority the $7 million, which had been spent. So the officials tried again. They scaled back the original plan, retained the two parks and proposed development for just one of the two piers and two of the four blocks. The revised plan was placed on Tuesday's ballot.
The strategy angered the coalition.
Cassandra Wilday, a landscape architect who moved to Hoboken three years ago, said the city's revised plan appeared to be different from the original plan because it cut out half of the development area. "But it's really the same plan because we expect them to just paste the other half back on," she said.
In fact, the revised plan gives the Port Authority an option to develop the property that was taken out of the original plan. Mayor Pasculli said, however, that any construction on that portion must be in keeping with a plan to be developed by the Hoboken Waterfront Corporation, a community planning agency composed of city officials, planners and residents.
Ron Hine, the coalition's spokesman, said that rather than just oppose the city's revised plan, which addresses only a small portion of the waterfront, the coalition had decided to develop a citywide plan of its own.
"We wanted to put forth a positive vision for what could happen on the waterfront," said Mr. Hine, a potter who has lived in Hoboken since 1969.
The result of that vision now sits in the basement of the First Presbyterian Church on Washington Street and is etched in the minds of the 150 volunteers who stand on street corners passing out leaflets and registering voters for Tuesday's election.
"This represents 2,000 hours of volunteer time," Mr. Hine said, referring to the 12-foot-long scale model of the group's plan, designed by Craig Whitaker, an architect. The model depicts a tree-lined waterfront esplanade that runs the length of the city. Inland of the esplanade, the coalition plan calls for four million square feet of residential and commercial development and 24 acres of parks. The two 5.5-acre piers remain vacant.
Ms. Wilday, the landscape architect, said that Frank Sinatra Drive, the road along the waterfront, provided a clear delineation between the public area and private development in the coalition's plan.
The city plan, she said, which calls for high-rise buildings on the southernmost pier with a public park at the very tip, will send mixed signals to the public. "The buildings will take possession of the pier, and it won't be inviting to the public," Ms. Wilday said. "It will, in essence, privatize that piece of waterfront."
City officials, however, say the plan has one major shortcoming: the recession has made private-development money increasingly scarce. On the other hand, officials say, the Port Authority is prepared to commit $100 million to the city's plan if it is approved.
Peggy E. Thomas, director of the city's Department of Planning and Community Development, said the Port Authority would spend up to $25 million for building a park, rehabilitating a baseball field, demolishing vacant buildings on the waterfront and making other infrastructure improvements.
 
Jobs Expected for Residents
 The remaining $75 million, she said, will be invested in the development itself, which will help stabilize the tax rates. She also estimated that 10 to 15 percent of the permanent jobs generated at the office tower and hotel would go to Hoboken residents.
"People have to make hard economic choices in this day and age," she said. "You can't make those choices just based upon somebody's view out their window."
In an effort to avoid repeating the mistake made in the 1990 referendum, officials have mounted an aggressive campaign to bring the issue before the public. The coalition has countered by stepping up its campaign.
But all the political hoopla has apparently left many residents confused and anxious for something other than plans and models to take shape on the waterfront.
"I don't like a 'yes' or a 'no' plan," said Ian Gilmore, a resident for 10 years, referring to the choice voters will have to make on Tuesday. "This is the best piece of commercial property that New Jersey has to offer. I think there should be some compromise."

CORRECTION-DATE: March 30, 1992, Monday

 CORRECTION:
An article in the New Jersey Weekly yesterday about a waterfront development plan for Hoboken omitted an element of the plan advocated by the city. It includes an "up front" payment of $3.25 million from the Port Authority.

HEADLINE: All About/Sewing;
In the Gray 90's, Women Are Heading Back to the Bobbin

BYLINE: By STEPHANIE STROM

 BODY:
IN the early 1970's, the $4 billion home sewing industry unraveled. As women left home for offices and careers, sales of patterns, fabrics, sewing machines and notions plunged, and many pundits of popular behavior began predicting the demise of a longstanding American tradition.
But while more women than ever work, home sewing is enjoying something of a renaissance. The Simplicity Pattern Company, one of the three American pattern makers that control 95 percent of the $50 million or so market, sold 15 percent more patterns last year than in 1990, and the Singer Sewing Company, the world's largest seller of sewing machines, estimates that its sales in the United States grew 30 percent last year. 
 "As the employment numbers go, so goes home sewing," said Alan G. Millstein, publisher of the Fashion Network Report, which tracks the garment industry. "There are probably a lot of women pulling their sewing machines out of the closet where they've been for the last 20 years."
Most people in the industry credit the recession with the resurgence in home sewing. But many are hopeful that a more fundamental change in the American psyche will keep the industry buoyant long after the economy recovers.
"While the uptick is certainly reflective of the economy, I really feel this is part of the whole reaction to the self-indulgent 80's," said J. Nicholas Hahn, president and chief executive of Cotton Inc., the umbrella organization representing American cotton growers whose goods account for 59 percent of the fabric sold to home sewers. "People aren't throwing money around like they did five or six years ago. They've re-embraced basic values and aren't likely to give them up for awhile."
The biggest growth categories for pattern makers has been home decorating and children's clothing, testament to the aging baby boomer's new interest in home and family.
And styles of accepted office attire have relaxed. Women are no longer expected to wear a severely tailored suit adorned only with a demure strand of pearls or a scarf.Uniforms are out, individuality is in -- and a dress is far easier to sew than a jacket.
"Everybody has the same Liz Claiborne stuff, and who wants to go to the office and see everyone wearing the same thing?" said Pamela Hastings, director of consumer education for Singer.

 
 
Smarter Machines
They Can Memorize A Buttonhole
 Innovations in sewing machines have given the home sewing market a boost. What was once manual -- threading the needle, snipping thread -- is now electronic. "Machines are getting smarter so that the people operating them don't have to be," said Linda Griepentrog, editor of Sew News, a sewing magazine with about 230,000 subscribers. "If you tell them what kind of fabric you're using and what you're sewing, they make all the decisions about stitching for you."
The Quantum CXL -- at about $2,500, the Cadillac of Singer's line -- has a 300-stitch-plus built-in memory, allowing it to spew out anything from a bit of programmed embroidery to a buttonhole. Not enough? It will also, on command, recreate a pattern in mirror image.
Think the Izod signature embroidery has pizazz? New Home, a highly regarded Japanese-owned sewing machine manufacturer based in Teterboro, N.J., is about to introduce a device that will allow the sewer to scan a design in, say, a newspaper, magazine or family photo; commit it to the sewing-machine's memory; accept orders on within-the-design color changes and produce it on a garment. The device, named Memory Craft 8000, is so smart it knows when to stop and wait for you to change colors of thread.
"People are interested in embellishing garments to give them a unique, one-of-a-kind sort of look," said JoAnn Pugh, marketing manager of software at Bernina of America, a sewing machine maker.
The new technology also gives home sewers the ability to put a professional finish on garments. Electronic sewing machines can memorize a buttonhole, then produce identical copies, and can make various types of stitches without mechanical attachments.
Innovations in today's sewing machines have enticed new entrants into the market. Singer reports that five years ago, 30 percent of its North American customers were first-time purchasers, compared with 50 percent today. "It's not chic to spend $5,000 on your wardrobe any more," Ms. Hastings of Singer said. "It's almost like it's cool to say you made your own clothing."
 
Pattern Makers
Stressing How Little Time Is Needed
 The pattern industry is emerging from a decade of upheaval, when takeover artists like Carl Icahn, who made a bid for Simplicity, and Reginald Lewis, who bought McCall's for $1 million in cash and $19 million in high-yield debt, were attracted to the high pre-tax profits pattern companies generated.
All three of the major pattern companies -- Simplicity, McCall's and Butterick -- were acquired in leveraged buyouts. The ownership of Simplicity and McCall's changed hands multiple times, loading them with debt. McCall's is just emerging from bankruptcy, and Simplicity, which started the 80's with $100 million in spare cash, entered the 90's with $100 million in debt.
Butterick, once the nation's smallest major pattern manufacturer, was bought by its management in 1983, and now controls 32 percent of the domestic pattern market, according to the National Purchase Diary, a market research firm.
Butterick was the first to attempt to decrease its dependence on apparel patterns. Capitalizing on its sophistication as a printer, the company acquired small greeting card companies and has increased its presence on newsstands with seven magazines that generate income as well as pattern sales. Simplicity and McCall's each publish a similar magazine.
"Five years ago, we started programming ourselves to go down in pattern sales and planned a strategy to offset that with new businesses," said John E. Lehmann, chairman, president and chief executive of Butterick. "But we got luck and pattern sales climbed slightly, and we still have the other businesses as insurance."
The pattern companies have simplified and streamlined their patterns to save their customers time. McCall's sells a pattern for a blazer that can be constructed in four hours, and Simplicity has what it calls a 2 Hour Express Collection.
"The perception of sewing is that it's time-consuming, but that isn't always the case," said Judy Raymond, senior vice president of marketing and new business at Simplicity. "So in many cases, the only difference in patterns today is that we're telling the consumer precisely what kind of time investment it's going to be."

 
 
More Than Clothes
Capturing the Home Spruce-It-Up Craze
 Home decorating is hot. For example, Butterick's best-selling pattern in January was for a baseball cap ('tis the season), but second, third and fourth places were taken up by curtains.
People in the industry note that the straight-line sewing of curtains and pillows is far easier and faster than, for example, a dress's curves, tucks -- and complicated fittings. "Windows don't gain weight," explained Simplicity's Ms. Raymond.
Moreover, people have been moving far less in the last five or six years than in the past, and new curtains, slipcovers and other decorative touches spruce up an old house.
Pattern makers are also dipping into the crafts business with patterns and plans that don't require sewing. Simplicity offers a Halloween costume, for example, that can be assembled with fusable tape and an iron in less than two hours.
McCall's Creates, a new series of $4 leaflets, shows how to make, among other things, woven placemats and dolls from handkerchiefs. Simplicity even offers instructions on how to build a birdhouse.
 
 
 
EVERYONE LOVES A SERGER
 While manufacturers have wrought a revolution in sewing machines, it is the serger, or overlock, machine that has truly revolutionized home sewing. Called the microwave of the business, a serger is used to give seams and other parts of a garment the same neat finish as commercially produced clothes.
The sewing machine makers introduced sergers at retail -- with price tags of $400 to $1,000 -- about seven years ago. Complements to the basic machines, sergers weave stitches around an edge of fabric that might otherwise ravel. As they go along, they also trim away excess material. In addition to giving homemade clothing a more finished look, overlock machines hit speeds of 1,500 stitches a minute, compared to between 800 and 1,000 stitches regular sewing machines are capable of. A pair of leggings can be completed in less than an hour.
Almost half of Sew News's 230,000 subscribers own a serger, according to Linda Turner Griepentrog, the magazine's editor. In fact, PJS Publications Inc., which publishes her magazine, decided to launch Serger Update, a newsletter about the machine and its capabilities.

CORRECTION-DATE: April 5, 1992, Sunday

 CORRECTION:
The "All About" column last Sunday, about home sewing products, misstated the American market share in patterns, as reported by the National Purchase Diary, a market research group. The group said that in 1991, McCall's held 32.4 percent of the market, Simplicity had 32 percent and Butterick's share was 27.7 percent.

HEADLINE: ALBANY'S LEADERS ANNOUNCE ACCORD ON STATE'S BUDGET

BYLINE: By SAM HOWE VERHOVEK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ALBANY, March 28

 BODY:
Gov. Mario M. Cuomo and the leaders of the New York State Legislature announced tonight that they had reached an accord on a roughly $31 billion spending plan for the fiscal year that begins on Wednesday.
The agreement appeared to end the colossal five-month fight over the budget, which Governor Cuomo said was the obstacle that kept him from running for President. 
 At a joint news conference in the Governor's office, Mr. Cuomo and the leaders, Assembly Speaker Saul Weprin and Senate Majority Leader Ralph J. Marino, said the agreement meant they were "virtually assured" of having all budget bills passed by Wednesday, April 1, the start of the 1992-93 fiscal year.
 
No New Aid to New York City
 It would mark the first time in eight years that the Legislature managed to produce a budget before the legal deadline. For the last seven years, the budget was passed days and sometimes weeks after April 1. Last year it was a record nine weeks late.
Aides to Mr. Cuomo and the leaders said that much like the budget plans proposed by the Governor in November and January, the agreement reached tonight includes virtually no increases for most local-aid programs, including aid to New York City. It also puts new limits on some services in the Medicaid program and imposes hundreds of millions of dollars in new fees on hospitals and other health-care providers to keep the budget in balance.
Unlike last year, when Mr. Cuomo and the leaders made a last-minute deal to raise the state income tax to produce the revenue they needed to close the budget, this time, aides said, there were no major surprises in this plan.
 
What About Candidate Cuomo?
 But the Governor and the leaders repeatedly refused to provide any details of the plan in their joint appearance, about 9:30 P.M. And Mr. Cuomo cut short the news conference, joking that he and the leaders had to watch the overtime period of the N.C.A.A. East Regional final between Duke and Kentucky. Duke won, 104-103.
Asked later in a telephone interview whether, with the budget settled, he might now consider joining Gov. Bill Clinton of Arkansas and former Gov. Edmund G. Brown Jr. of California in the race for the Democratic Presidential nomination, Mr. Cuomo said he would be "thinking exclusively" about the budget until the Senate and Assembly passed it.
Asked if he would think about running for President after the Legislature passed a budget, the Governor said he could not answer. "It's like saying, 'If I had a little bread and I had a little cheese, then I'd have some bread and cheese.' "
Although the plan includes no increases in any of the state's broad-based taxes -- the sales tax and personal and corporate incomes taxes -- it does include increases of hundreds of millions of dollars in fees that hospitals, nursing homes and insurers would be obligated to pay to the state.
Lobbyists for those groups are likely to wage a ferocious last-minute attempt to defeat those fees, and if unsuccessful, are likely to pass them on to consumers.
Similarly, while most of the other new revenue sources agreed to will be felt indirectly, some, like an accelerated collection schedule for sales taxes on petroleum products, will be more apparent. Business groups estimated the new schedule would force them to add a penny or more to the retail price of a gallon of gasoline.
 
How Talks Began
 The legislative leaders dropped plans to allow video lottery games in bars and clubs.
Mayor David N. Dinkins may campaign strongly against passage of the budget plan agreed to tonight. He has urged lawmakers to "just vote no" on any budget that did not restore many of what he said totaled $100 million in aid cuts.
Mr. Cuomo said that Mr. Dinkins was unlikely to be satisfied by the agreement, but that its timely passage was critical. And the Governor suggested that part of the Mayor's unhappiness was politically expendient.
"No mayor who wants to please his or her constituency will admit to any satisfaction with this budget," Mr. Cuomo said.
Mr. Dinkins's press secretary, Leland T. Jones, said that the city had not received full details of the budget agreement, but that he was skeptical about what it might contain.
"What we've heard is that the agreement in Albany is tentative, tenuous and somewhat troublesome," he said. "As the saying goes in football, you don't get credit for the touchdown if you have the ball at the 20-yard line with third and goal to go."
Mr. Jones also said that having taken $300 million in cuts in state aid, New York City could not bear any more. "We're not sure if this agreement holds us harmless for any more cuts," he said. "If we have to face additional cuts, we're obviously going to have to look at some reductions in services and increased costs to our residents."
The struggle to reach this particular budget accord dates back to early November, when Mr. Cuomo's budget director, Patrick J. Bulgaro, revealed that the state was facing a deficit of at least $500 million in the existing $30 billion budget, a shortfall that eventually grew to $875 million.
At the same time, the Cuomo administration announced that even more severe problems were looming for the 1992-93 fiscal year, which begins on Wednesday, because of declines in projected tax revenues and growing demands on the budgets for Medicaid and welfare in the face of the recession.
That led Mr. Cuomo to propose that he and the legislative leaders undertake a singular approach to solving the budget woes, essentially tackling both years' budget problems at once. His plan involved drafting a 17-month budget that would carry the state through April 1993.
 
Political Baggage
 But whatever the fiscal merits, the plan became weighed down with heavy political baggage: Had it worked, it would have given Mr. Cuomo the clear advantage of freeing him to hit the Presidential campaign trail. Republicans initially said they were skeptical of the proposal, even though later -- after Mr. Cuomo had pulled out of the Presidential race -- they became its chief proponents.
In any event, negotiations ultimately collapsed. And even more modest attempts to solve the smaller $875 million deficit failed, which forced the state to borrow more than $500 million to tide it through the year and which caused two major Wall Street bond-rating agencies to lower the state's credit rating to the second-lowest of any state in the nation.
Mr. Cuomo, meanwhile, grew increasingly agitated with what he depicted as the Legislature's inertia, and he frequently lashed out at both parties, saying they would pay the price if they failed to come together to produce a budget.
"If they don't do it, they're dead politically," he told reporters a few days after announcing on Dec. 20 that he would not run for President. "They're going down. Why? Because they're going to have such trouble doing the budget in daylight, in April, May, June, July, they're going to get killed when they are revealed to the world."
Still, once he reverted to a traditional 12-month budget proposal, Mr. Cuomo said it was imperative that the budget be completed by the legal deadline, as opposed to last year, when he and the Legislature were nine weeks late. Delay, the Governor said, would run the risk of a further decline in the state's credit rating. A late budget would be "disastrous" for New Yorkers, he said, because it could demote some state borrowing to "junk bond status."

HEADLINE: Employers Winning Wide Leeway to Cut Medical Insurance Benefits

BYLINE: By MILT FREUDENHEIM

 BODY:
A rapidly growing number of victims of cancer, AIDS and other serious illnesses are discovering that under recent court interpretations of a law that was originally intended to protect employees' benefits, their insurance coverage can evaporate when they need it most.
The recent Federal court rulings have given employers that now act as their own insurers wide leeway to cut back on existing coverage -- or to skimp on coverage in the first place. These "self-insured" employers, a large majority of companies from giant corporations to an increasing number of smaller businesses, have been exempted from state insurance laws governing what ailments insurance companies must cover. For example, many states require coverage for alcoholism, mental health problems and intensive care for infants with birth defects. 
 At the same time, a Supreme Court decision has made it much harder for patients under all kinds of health insurance plans to sue to get benefits they say have been unfairly denied.
 
AIDS Coverage Slashed
 Among the most striking cases in which employees found they had insurance in name only were those of Richard Owens in Atlanta and John McGann in Houston. After Mr. Owens began filing thousands of dollars in AIDS claims with his employer, a furniture chain called Storehouse, the company cut its maximum lifetime benefit for AIDS to $25,000 from $1 million. Similarly, Mr. McGann's employer, H & H Music, a Texas retail group, reduced its $1 million maximum to $5,000 for AIDS cases.
A Federal district court in Atlanta and an appeals court in New Orleans ruled last year that the companies had every right to do so. If they had bought their coverage from an insurance company, state laws in both Georgia and Texas would have required that the original AIDS coverage be continued.
Under a string of earlier court rulings, self-insured employers have been exempted from hundreds of state health-care laws. Massachusetts, for example, requires insurers to provide up to 30 days of hospitalization a year for alcoholism treatment. But A.T.& T. employees are eligible for only five to seven days of detoxification in a hospital and a lifetime limit of two 30-day hospital stays, when approved by company medical experts. A.T.& T. also pays for outpatient alcoholism treatment.
 
Separation of Sick From Well
 Although six states now require coverage of in-vitro fertilization to help women become pregnant, General Electric, like many self-insured employers, does not pay for that treatment.
In effect, the court rulings and the health plans that take advantage of them are another manifestation of a system of private health insurance in which the sick are increasingly separated from the well. That lowers the insurance costs of the healthy but leaves more of the sick with little or no protection.
"We're very concerned," said John Rother, public policy director for the American Association of Retired Persons, which had supported Mr. McGann. "It's not just AIDS. This kind of ruling could be applied to any chronic disease, like Alzheimer's, which strikes some people in their 50's. We seem to just have insurance for those people who don't need it."
 
Avoiding State Rules
 The number of Americans affected by the rulings is rising as more employers, concerned about upwardly spiraling medical costs, choose to act as their own insurer.
To be sure, many of these employers continue to offer a comprehensive benefits package that often exceeds what most states require. They say they act as their own insurer not to cut back on coverage but to economize by paying less to insurance companies and averting the costs of complying with varying state laws. They are also exempted from state insurance taxes and assessments, and they say they can slow the rise in costs by supervising the quality and duration of care.
But others, desperate to lower medical-cost increases, have cut back. Many self-insured employers are eliminating or reducing coverage for expensive treatments like care for infertility and alcoholism, which insurance companies are required to offer in many states, said Judith Mazo, research director with the Segal Company, a benefits consulting firm.
Nearly two-thirds of the nation's employers are now providing their own coverage, up from fewer than half only four years ago, according to a survey of 2,000 employers last year by the A. Foster Higgins consulting firm. The proportion of self-insured smaller employers, with 100 or fewer workers, grew even faster, more than doubling to 23 percent last year from only 9 percent in 1988.
Many self-insured employers have been able to reduce their coverage under the courts' interpretations of the Employee Retirement Income Security Act, known as Erisa. Enacted in 1974, the law was intended to guarantee employee pension rights by establishing Federal control over benefits.
But the courts have interpreted a broad but little-noticed provision of the law to limit state authority over health benefits as well.
Former Government officials who helped draft the law said health plans were not even discussed when a sweeping Federal pre-emption of state benefit laws was added. Erisa did not spell out Federal rules to replace the state laws.
The American Medical Association, the American Association of Retired Persons, the state attorneys general, and several state insurance commissioners support proposals in Congress to plug the exemptions from state laws. As James S. Todd, executive vice president of the American Medical Association, put it, "The sad part is that somebody who thinks they are covered by their employer suddenly discovers that they are not."
But the Bush Administration and many employers strongly oppose changing Erisa, and the insurance industry even supports extending the Federal exemption to all employers. Without Erisa, they say, insurance costs would rise even faster as insurers would be forced to comply with varying state requirements, and even more employers would give up health coverage.
Even more small businesses would be exempted from state insurance laws under an Administration proposal last month, which would reward companies that join new purchasing groups to buy coverage.
Worries about the dependability of health insurance, which Mr. Bush cited in his State of the Union speech, were hardly assuaged by the McGann and Owens rulings. Citing a 1985 appeals court ruling that employer decisions on what benefits to offer should be determined by "forces in the marketplace" unless contradicted by a Federal law, the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit said in November that under the Erisa law a self-insured employer has "an absolute right to alter the terms of medical coverage available to plan beneficiaries."
Mark A. Huvard, a lawyer for H & H, Mr. McGann's employer, said the company had to "limit its exposure on things that are exotic" or else drop basic health coverage for several hundred other employees. Lawyers for the estate of Mr. McGann, who died last June, recently petitioned for a Supreme Court review.
Judith O'Brien, a lawyer for Mr. Owens's employer, said Storehouse could not find an insurance company willing to provide reinsurance for costs above $25,000 for AIDS cases. Self-insured employers often hedge their risks by buying reinsurance, which covers costs above an agreed level. A Federal appeals court in Atlanta heard arguments in the case last month.
Bob Griss, a United Cerebral Palsy Associations researcher, cited Government surveys indicating that 81 million Americans under age 65 have the kinds of chronic ailments, including cancer, multiple sclerosis and diabetes, that he said could put them at risk of losing coverage.
 
Loses Case Against Dow
 James C. Cathey Jr. was a purchasing agent in Houston with the Dow Chemical Company when his wife, Bette Lou, contracted muscular sclerosis and had to use a wheelchair. He said that Dow, which was self-insured, forced him into early retirement in 1983 and two years later stopped paying for nursing care ordered by Mrs. Cathey's doctor. He sued Dow unsuccessfully in Federal and state courts, which decided that Erisa protects the company's plan from such challenges.
Janet Van Alsten, Dow's benefits planning manager, said that Mrs. Cathey was eligible only for skilled nursing that would monitor her disease and give medication, but not for such daily nursing needs as helping her bathe and eat. "Even if Erisa didn't apply, we wouldn't have to pay a dime," she said.
But Mark Kincaid, Mrs. Cathey's lawyer, said that her health plan specifically covered nursing prescribed by a physician. "If the Erisa exemption had not existed," he said, "we would have won in state court, where insurance policy language that is unclear is construed in favor of the patient."
State officials want to remove the regulatory exemptions. "Erisa has become a tool for employers who wish to avoid their obligations under state law," the National Association of Attorneys General said last year.
Complicating the problems of people complaining that coverage has been unfairly curtailed, the Supreme Court has discouraged lawsuits against insurers by deciding that patients cannot collect punitive damages allowed under state laws. They may collect only the price of the treatments found to have been unfairly denied.
That decision came in a precedent-setting Erisa case involving the Pilot Life Insurance Company of Greensboro, N.C. The Supreme Court held in 1987 that health insurers are exempt from the state laws that permit juries to assess punitive damages for improperly denied medical claims.
 
'Take a Hike'
 Because of the Pilot ruling, John Garamendi, the California insurance commissioner, said his department could do little about the 30,000 health insurance complaints it receives each year.
"The insurance companies tell us to take a hike," he said. "The self-insured employers don't even bother to answer our phone calls."
Critics of the Erisa law say the Pilot Life ruling makes it very difficult for a family that had spent its savings on medical bills to sue an insurer. Without the possibility of punitive damages, they said, most lawyers will not take these cases.
Nick J. Canata's 12-year-old daughter, Corinne, is blind and has to take growth hormones and other expensive drugs for medical problems she has had since birth. Mr. Canata, a farmer in Earlimart, Calif., near Fresno, said an insurance broker persuaded him to buy coverage from a farmer insurance trust without telling him that the trust was self-insured. When Corinne's expenses snowballed, Mr. Canata said, the trust canceled his coverage and would not provide a conversion policy to continue her benefits.
William M. Shernoff, a Claremont lawyer who took Corinne's case despite the limitations on his potential compensation, said the trust was able to claim exemption, under Erisa, from state laws that would require an insurer to offer her an individual policy. He took the case to a Federal court, where the potential recovery was limited, and the family would be left without insurance for her heavy continuing needs.
Representative Howard L. Berman, a California Democrat, and Senator Howard M. Metzenbaum, Democrat of Ohio, are sponsoring bills to overturn the Pilot Life ruling. Business groups say the bills would primarily benefit plaintiffs' lawyers, and the Bush Administration has threatened to veto them.

HEADLINE: Networking;
Keeping the Costs Down When Office Hopping

BYLINE: By STEPHEN C. MILLER

 BODY:
Connecting a local area network in small branch office to the home office or another branch office can be expensive if it isn't planned carefully. A major problem with this process, called internetworking, is controlling the volume -- and cost -- of traffic over expensive long distance phone lines.
In most LAN operating systems, packets of data are sent along the network until they reach their intended destinations. Much of that data involves requests for access to expensive shared resources, such tasks as producing something on a color laser printer. 
 In some cases, the data might travel quite a distance before getting to the right place. A print request might originate with a user in Chicago, but the only color printer is in New York. If there are three other offices on the network between Chicago and New York, each would get the print request, reject it and send it on. A solution is a device called a router attached to the network. It acts as a traffic cop and sends data to the proper destination via the most efficient route.
Even with a traditional router, the amount of network traffic can still swamp a remote LAN connected via a regular phone line, since the phone line can handle only a small amount of data at any one moment. A T1 line, a dedicated high-speed phone service, eases the problem, but it can add significantly to the cost of servicing the remote LAN. One solution is to put additional routing power within the LAN itself by installing a router circuit board and software on the network's server computer. The local router decides what to do with outward bound data before it leaves the local network.
"For those companies committed to internetworking and running a single network operating system, like Novell's Netware, router cards are the way to go," said Paul Callahan of Forrester Research Inc., a market research firm in Cambridge, Mass.
Eicon Technology of Montreal goes beyond the traditional with Router for Netware and its accompanying software and circuit board, known as the Eiconcard, to speed data to and from the remote LAN. The system acts as a mini-router by figuring out which router on the network would handle a particular user request. It then passes the request to the appropriate router, using the most efficient, or cheapest, connection on the network.
Router for Netware comes in two versions. One is for packet switched commercial data networks that use a communications protocol known as X.25. (Packet switched networks are akin to long distance lines for computer data.) The other version is for point-to-point connections using private phone lines. Both models retail for $995. The Eiconcard for each PC retails for $1,095.

HEADLINE: Lighting Russia's Entrepreneurial Spirit

BYLINE: By PENNY SINGER

 BODY:
ARE the citizens of Moscow and St. Petersburg ready to be transformed into entrepreneurs? The wives of two Russian diplomats say the answer is yes and are helping lay the groundwork for entrepreneurial centers across Russia that would be patterned on one in Purchase.
The wives, Natalia B. Lozinsky and Elena V. Ilitchev, know something about what it takes to be an entrepreneur because they studied how to start a business in a 10-week course at the Entrepreneurial Center at Manhattanville College in Purchase. 
 One recipient of their help now is the director of the Entrepreneurial Center, Marge Lovero, who is leaving Friday on a two-week fact-finding trip to Russia. She will be accompanied by Clifford Schorer, the volunteer president of the Entrepreneurial Council, a nonprofit group affiliated with the United Nations that was begun in 1990 to foster development.
Mrs. Lozinsky's husband, Valentin, is Russia's First Deputy Permanent Representative to the United Nations. Mrs. Ilitchev's husband, Alexander, is the political counselor at the Russian Federation Mission in Manhattan.
"What is happening is very exciting," Mrs. Lozinsky said in a meeting earlier this month at the Russian Mission in Manhattan. "It started simply, with a few women."
This week's trip is under the auspices of the Volunteer Development Council for United Nations Delegations, a group whose goal is fostering volunteerism to stimulate business worldwide.
Mrs. Lovero and Mr. Schorer will be accompanied by Russian volunteers selected by Mrs. Lozinsky and Mrs. Ilitchev, who will assist in setting up meetings with people in business and government.
In addition to Moscow and St. Petersburg, the group will visit Kaluga, an hour and a half by car from Moscow; Barnaul in Siberia, and Ulyanovsk, 1,000 east of Moscow. In each city, they will explore the possibilities of setting up centers for entrepreneurs similar to the one in Purchase.
"Elena and I met Marge Lovero through Toby Anthony, who is the acting president of the Volunteer Development Council," Mrs. Lozinsky said. "We started talking about the problems that ordinary citizens have; not those higher-ups running steel plants, but average people who are finally making the adjustment from working in a centrally planned economy to a free economy. We had no guidelines."
Mrs. Lozinsky, a political analyst who has observed capitalism in America for 15 years, said she was especially intrigued with the entrepreneurial businesses she has seen here.
"We are on our fourth assignment to the United States," she said, speaking of herself and her husband. "We lived in Rye for several years and liked it very much; our son was born there." The couple now live in Manhattan.
"Now, when Russian society is being reshaped," she said, "I thought it was time for people like myself to make a grass-roots effort, to help able, mainstream Russian people to get into what is, for us, a new economic world."
It was to better understand the skills needed to become a successful entrepreneur that Mrs. Lozinsky and Mrs. Ilitchev, who is a medical doctor, decided to enroll in the course at Manhattanville.
 
'You Call It Hands-On'
 "It is not like any course I have taken," Mrs. Ilitchev said. "We got so much practical information -- here you call it hands-on experience -- from the lecturers, who were all entrepreneurs themselves."
Mrs. Ilitchev lives in the Russian compound in Riverdale and sends her two sons to P.S. 81 in the Bronx. "We also got to know the other students in the course well," she said. "At the end of each session, we would stay and talk and learn from each other."
What was especially interesting, Mrs. Lozinsky said, was the spark of creativity she observed in everyone in the program. "It convinced me of something I already knew: that universal human qualities are all the same," she said.
"A creative spark is what makes people come alive. As a people, Russians are very creative. And understanding that a business is also a creation is a concept that is very much attuned to the Russian soul."
 
'The Time Is Right'
 The idea of opening entrepreneurial centers across Russia is very pragmatic, Mr. Schorer said. "The time is right simply because the Russian Government is aware that the institutions they now have in place are not adequate for the future as a platform of business. I believe they are going to nurture entrepreneurs in every possible way."
Mrs. Lovero described this week's trip as an initial effort.
"Cliff and I are making this first exploratory trip in order to find out how we can best help small business people in Russia," she said. "The plan is for us to go back to Russia in September with Mrs. Lozinsky and some of the instructors at the center, plus a volunteer group of entrepreneurs."
The plan at that time, she added, is to hold a two-day conference in each of the five cities, "with the hope of starting Russian-staffed entrepreneurial centers in all of them."
The project is receiving only private financing, not government funds. "We hope our efforts will be like the pebbles thrown in the water, expanding in ever-widening circles," Mrs. Lozinsky said.
"We know there is a lot of desire to learn how to be your own boss in Russia," she added. "Skilled people there, including professionals, know that they have an opportunity now to implement their skills. But they want to know the right and the best way to operate in a market-driven economy, which is new to us."
The conferences in September, Mrs. Lozinsky said, are expected to attract "an audience made up of 'brokers,' who might be manufacturers, professionals such as accountants or dentists, and people interested in starting service businesses."
Even former black marketeers would be welcome, she said. "They are the one group that already knows the basics of entrepreneurship."
She sees the conferences attracting "an eclectic group -- what people have in common is that they are eager to adapt to new ideas."
This interest has already been recognized by the new Russian Government, Mrs. Lozinsky said. "There has been an Association of Entrepreneurs formed recently in Moscow. It is headed by Arkadiy Volsky, who is a high Government official and a former representative of Parliament.
"But our Entrepreneurial Centers are designed for the people who are, so far, left out. We will deal with them on a one-to-one basis and they will learn such practical things as I did, such as how to get financing, promote sales, price products and services."
Mr. Schorer, who heads his own company, Express Office Products in Atlanta, said he wants to attract American corporations to participate in the entrepreneurial project.
"What better time for American corporations with vision to establish an identity for themselves for future business dealings in Russia?" he asked. "Russia has 260 million educated consumers, and that's a very big potential market for American goods and services."
Mrs. Lozinsky's enthusiasm for the entrepreneurial program has been contagious, Mrs. Lovero said.
"So much so," she said, that the wife of the Danish Under Secretary of the United Nations, Barbara Hansen, "who is Polish by birth, has now begun to take our course on Wednesday evenings."
"Mrs. Hansen will be going back to Europe in late June," Mrs. Lovero added, "and says she intends to go to Poland and discuss what she has learned about entrepreneurship."

CORRECTION-DATE: April 5, 1992, Sunday

 CORRECTION:
An article last Sunday about plans for helping entrepreneurs in Russia referred incorrectly to the Entrepreneurial Council. It is affiliated with the Entrepreneurial Center at Manhattanville College in Purchase, not with the United Nations.

SECTION: Section 7; Page 7; Column 1; Book Review Desk
