HEADLINE: 'In Trouble' in Black and White

BYLINE: By Andrew Hacker; Andrew Hacker teaches political science at Queens College and is the author of "Two Nations," a book on race in America.

 BODY:

WAKE UP LITTLE SUSIE
Single Pregnancy and Race Before Roe v. Wade.
By Rickie Solinger.
324 pp. New York: Routledge. $25. 
 WE have come a long way, we like to think, since Hester Prynne had to wear a scarlet A. Yet much closer to our time, children born outside marriage were openly called "bastards," with "ILLEGITIMATE" stamped on their birth certificates.
But how far have we progressed? Even in our more enlightened era, most Americans feel that procreation should be confined to married couples. Single motherhood is still a flashpoint for social and sexual morality, as well as a means of exacerbating tensions of race and class and gender. Extramarital pregnancies and births raise an array of vexing issues. Whether abortion should remain an option often leads the list. But there are also worrisome aspects to adoption, and passions rise over having to pay taxes for public assistance.
With the dismantling of Roe v. Wade certain to continue, and reversal a possibility, states will be free to rescind the abortion alternative. What will be the consequence? During the 1980's, an average of 1.57 million pregnancies were terminated each year, or some 424 abortions for every 1,000 live births. Approximately 1.3 million abortions, about 82 percent, were performed on single women. These figures seem fated to change as access to abortion gets restricted. Yet quite apart from policies on abortion, out-of-wedlock births have risen without remission for the past 40 years.
"Wake Up Little Susie: Single Pregnancy and Race Before Roe v. Wade" details the treatment accorded unmarried girls and women who became pregnant from approximately 1945 to the middle 1960's, which brought both the Great Society and the sexual revolution. Rickie Solinger, now a visiting scholar at the University of Colorado, Boulder, tells a grim and compelling story. It is an avowedly feminist analysis, showing how their possession of "the reproductive capacity has contributed to the oppression of women in the United States in variable ways." The major variable Ms. Solinger has chosen to stress is race. She deals only briefly with contrasting class reactions to premarital pregnancies and births, and the book does not distinguish the experiences of teen-agers from those of more mature women. Still, she has a persuasive case for making race her key division. The result is a stimulating study that should have a wide audience.
Whether or not beliefs about race have really changed, people spoke more candidly in those postwar years. Most members of the white race saw themselves as a superior strain, which meant more was expected of them, particularly if they were single women. Those who became pregnant were held morally culpable for having got themselves into "trouble," as if their seducers or traducers were in no way responsible. They were deemed a disgrace to their race, with redemption only possible by displaying remorse. The expected penance was to give the baby up for adoption, since sex prior to marriage was sufficient evidence of unfitness for motherhood. These strictures were especially stern in maternity homes for white girls, to which Ms. Solinger devotes several doleful chapters. Some were plainly places of incarceration, with high fences and locked gates. As many as 20 girls could be crowded into dormitory rooms, with a single drawer for personal possessions. Mail was routinely opened and read; and at most homes, only two afternoons were allocated for trips into town, always "under supervision."
Ms. Solinger also shows how experts in the 1950's and 60's held the view that "every unmarried mother is to some degree a psychiatric problem." A New York University study found such women to be "overtly dominant, aggressive, narcissistic and bitterly hostile." To want to raise children on their own showed they were "conflicted about their masculine and feminine drives" or were acting out a "parthenogenic, puberal fantasy." Unwed mothers should also be monitored for neglecting their babies, it was believed, with authorities empowered to take the children away and arrange for their adoption.
Attitudes toward black women were more ambiguous. To start, Ms. Solinger tells us, black unwed pregnancies and births were seen as "the product of uncontrolled sexual indulgence." Some Southern legislators called for compulsory sterilization after a specified number of children, although no state actually passed such a law. Yet even as late as 1964, Theodore H. White could allude to the "pattern of mating and breeding" in "zoological tenements." But despite condescension and worse, black women were seen as naturally suited for motherhood, so no efforts were made to remove their children. On the contrary, in one graphic case cited by Ms. Solinger, when a black woman in Chicago sought to give her baby up for adoption she was charged with desertion. On the whole, though, the social costs were low, since most black out-of-wedlock children were absorbed by extended families, and their mothers were expected to take jobs as chambermaids or domestic servants.
These attitudes began to change with the expansion of the Aid to Families With Dependent Children program. In the Great Society ideology, single women were as entitled as married women to remain at home with their children, even if at public expense. But that outlook did not last. The "welfare mother" soon became a target, even though stipends were -- and are -- below the poverty line. One criticism was that in the past, women usually applied for public assistance because of desertion or divorce; however, in recent years most of those joining the aid program have been women who embarked upon motherhood without benefit of marriage. The Family Security Act of 1987, a pet project of Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan of New York, aims at forcing women on welfare to find work. Little is heard any longer about how preschoolers might benefit from having a mother at home, and states like New Jersey are considering banning welfare payments for subsequent children.
AT what may be the end of the Roe era, is Ms. Solinger's racial emphasis still valid? Where abortion is concerned, black women have used that option as much as their white peers. According to the most recent Public Health Service figures, of unmarried white women who become pregnant, 1,130 choose abortion for every 1,000 who give birth; among their black counterparts, the ratio is 777 abortions for each 1,000 births. However, when economic levels are factored in, black women actually use abortion at a greater rate. With births there is also an increasing convergence. When unwed mothers of both races have babies, it is usually a conscious choice. Few have been forced to do so by lack of money or clinics in their areas. Moreover, almost all now opt to keep their children. The latest study by Planned Parenthood shows that only 3.2 percent of white women and 1.1 percent of black women give up their out-of-wedlock infants. Indeed, even as early as the 1960's, Ms. Solinger found, "white girls were keeping their babies as part of a rational life plan."
In 1950, only 1.7 percent of white babies were born to unmarried women, compared with 16.8 percent of black babies, or 10 times the figure. But by 1989, the most recent reporting year, the respective rates for the races had risen to 16.1 percent and 66 percent. While two-thirds of black births now occur outside of wedlock, the accompanying fact is that the proportion of black births has declined from 10 to 4 times that for whites. Indeed, the white rate is now where the black figure was 40 years ago. So even in an age of racial division, we may be coming closer to a consensus where sexual behavior is concerned.
And what about the book's title? At no place in the text does Ms. Solinger allude to this Everly Brothers song, let alone explain why she took it for her title. True, it was released in 1957, about midway through the period she is covering. The song, which I have played several dozen times, seems simply to be about two teen-agers who are getting home very late from a movie date. Sex is hardly hinted at; pregnancy certainly isn't. But perhaps I have not deconstructed it properly. Readers who want to try their hand can find the record in the "oldies" section.
 
 
 
THE POWER OF GUILT
 "Wake Up Little Susie" takes its title from a song by the Everly Brothers about a young couple in a quandary about how to explain Susie's tardy return home to her parents after a date.
"The words of that song are about the imputation of guilt, which was a key component in unwed pregnancy at the time," Rickie Solinger, the book's author, said in a telephone interview from her home in Boulder, Colo.
"This young girl thinks her association with imagined sexuality will doom her, and this book is really about the ways society and culture stigmatize girls in very damaging ways."
Although women's reproductive rights today seem more imperiled than ever, with the Supreme Court poised to review Roe v. Wade and a growing number of states taking steps to impede access to abortion, Ms. Solinger does not believe society will ever again treat unwed mothers as it once did.
"You can't reconfigure the social structure of a culture simply by changing laws," she said, "and unwed pregnancy today is regarded much differently than it was before 1965."
Instead, she said, she hopes her book demonstrates the ways in which society uses women's bodies to satisfy political ends.
Ms. Solinger says she is "on a mission to reveal the history of women in the pre-Roe-v.-Wade era, because I think it's very important to understand the horrors unwed, fertile women faced when their lives and their children's were used to fulfill society's agenda." -- STEPHANIE STROM

PUB-DATE: March 29, 1992

SECTION: Section 12CN; Page 1; Column 5; Connecticut Weekly Desk

LENGTH: 1373 words

HEADLINE: As Money Gets Tighter, Tax Collectors Get Tougher

BYLINE: By ROBERT A. HAMILTON

 BODY:
IN the first two weeks of March, Bridgeport issued almost 60 tax warrants, which allow it to attach the salaries and bank accounts of people who owe municipal taxes. By contrast, the city issued only about 15 such warrants in the previous five years. The city's finance director, Richard V. Robinson, said the accelerated collection efforts would continue, and the number of warrants "could be in the thousands by the time we're done."
As the recession continues to squeeze their finances, municipalities throughout the state are getting tough with taxpayers. In addition to issuing warrants, towns and cities are expediting foreclosures and sales of tax liens, even turning delinquent accounts over to private collection agencies. 
 "When you're desperate, you look for new ways to do things," Mr. Robinson said.
Every town has had problems with collections this year, said the executive director of the Connecticut Conference of Municipalities, Joel Cogen. Towns that historically had a 99 percent collection rate have seen it drop to 97 percent, he said, while those that have collected 95 percent in a good year might be down to 92 percent today. The tax collector in Brookfield, Theresa A. York, said she had moved up by seven months the schedule for foreclosures. "This is what we need to do, because the town needs the money," she said. "Cash flow is becoming a serious problem." Hamden's tax collector, Barbara J. Tito, said, "What we're seeing is people who for 10 or 20 years have never paid their taxes late, they can't afford them this year, and they're embarrassed."
Local tax collection is based on the calendar year, but city budgets correspond to a fiscal year that runs from July 1 to June 30. The scramble is now on to collect as much as possible of the money that was due on Jan. 1 before the current fiscal year ends, so the towns can wind up in the black.
Municipal officials say they are sympathetic to people who have lost their jobs or faced other economic hardships that have forced them to fall behind on their bills. But the officials add that it would be unfair to let taxes go unpaid.
"It's a lot better to collect all the taxes owed from the people who are supposed to pay than to raise the tax rate on the people who do pay," Mr. Cogen said.
 
'There's No Money Left'
 The Conference of Municipalities has just started a new program that uses two private collection agencies to track down recalcitrant taxpayers. Fees range from 20 to 40 percent of collections, depending on the type, balance and age of the account. There is no fee if there is no collection. More than a dozen towns have expressed interest in the program, Mr. Cogen said.
"People are financially strained these days," he said. "And when you're in that situation, you pay those bills you can't get away without paying. You pay the telephone company, because they threaten to cut off your service. Or you pay the electric company, because they threaten your lights. And by the time the tax collector comes around, there's no money left."
Many people have found it profitable to delay tax payments, Mr. Cogen said. "There's an 18 percent annual fee for overdue tax bills," he noted. "But if you're paying 21 percent on your Mastercard, 18 percent sounds pretty good, and it's zero percent until you actually pay it."
Mr. Cogen said the conference has been advised by its lawyers and by the State Office of Policy and Management that the new program is legal. But some tax collectors said they had their doubts. Mrs. York in Brookfield said her town's counsel warned her that the authority to turn delinquent accounts over to collection agencies was not specifically spelled out in state statutes.
"I think they could do a great job," Mrs. York said. "But I'm not about to make myself, or the town, liable."
She said the local newspaper agreed to publish the names of tax delinquents. "I told them I hope they had enough newsprint and typists," Mrs. York said with a laugh. "I expected that would get a lot of action. But it didn't. It must mean people don't have the money."
 
Selling Tax Liens
 In Bridgeport Mr. Robinson is using audits to aid in tax collection, something he had not done in several years, he said. In an audit the tax collector reviews the personal property statements a company files with state and Federal agencies to find out if there are any discrepancies.
Another tactic proposed by Bridgeport this year is the sale of tax liens. Rather than foreclose on a property, the city would sell the tax bill to a company or individual. The new owner can foreclose on the property and get it at a fraction of its value. The owner of the property can, at any time in the first 12 months, pay the tax bill and regain title, Mr. Robinson said.
"But in the meantime you're being paid 18 percent interest, under the statute," Mr. Robinson said. "It's a lot better than the five and a half percent interest you're going to get on a certificate of deposit. A lot of investors are very interested in the concept."
Mr. Robinson said that while he had not heard of another Connecticut town selling tax liens, the practice was used in Florida.
"We're trying to catch up on the ground we've lost over the last few years," Mr. Robinson said. Bridgeport's tax collections have consistently been among the lowest in the state, he said, just over 92 percent in recent years.
Some towns are finding the mere threat of enforcement is enough to get the job done. The tax collector in Guilford, Barbara A. Kohls, prepared a list of 34 properties proposed for a tax auction this month. The bills were paid on all but six by the day of the auction, she said.
 
Missed Mortgage Payments
 Mrs. Kohls already has nine other properties that have fallen behind, she said, and she will recommend another tax auction before June 30.
"This is the first time I've ever had two in a year," she said. "And the way things are going, I could have one again before the end of the calendar year."
In Hamden, where collections are about $1.1 million behind officials' expectations, Mrs. Tito said that many people were not making their mortgage payments, so banks were not paying the taxes on those properties.
"Once the banks foreclose on the properties, they'll pay the back taxes," she said. "But not in this fiscal year, which is creating the problem."
Mrs. Tito said most of her efforts involved trying to contact delinquent taxpayers earlier. Letters that normally would have gone out in January, warning people that their taxes were overdue, went out in October.
"People are picking and choosing what they're going to pay," Mrs. Tito said. "If I get them before another bill collector and tell them their tax is overdue, I may get the payment that week instead of someone else."
But she is also working with other town agencies to strengthen collection efforts, she said. For instance, she makes sure the Health Department does not issue a new license to a restaurant that is in arrears.
 
'The Worst Year'
 Middletown has established a task force that sends out collection letters to delinquent accounts, said the tax collector, Joseph S. Colonghi. A year ago it was sending out 60 letters at a time, he said. The most recent round of letters, in February, totaled 200.
"This has definitely been the worst year in my tenure, and I've been here 22 years," Mr. Colonghi said. He estimated more than 250 people are on payment schedules to catch up on overdue taxes.
"We've got about 50 properties in foreclosure proceedings," he said. "Two years ago maybe we had five or six."
Not everyone is enthusiastic about the results of more aggressive collection efforts in a recession, though. In New Britain the tax collector, Fred J. Menditto, said the city had collected just over $65 million, leaving about $3 million to be collected before June 30.
"We've gotten a little more forceful about foreclosures," Mr. Menditto said. But he added that taking errant taxpayers to court had its drawbacks.
"The city doesn't want to be the property owner," he said, because we don't want to have to sell it, or insure it. And the courts don't want to hear foreclosure cases. They've got a lot more important business."

LENGTH: 1172 words

HEADLINE: ARCHITECTURE VIEW;
A Logo of the Past On the Screen Of the Present

BYLINE: By Robert Campbell; Robert Campbell is the architecture critic for The Boston Globe.

DATELINE: BOSTON

 BODY:
Everyone says Robert A. M. Stern's new office tower here "looks like Boston." The phrase is subtler than it sounds. It defines a paradoxical, very contemporary kind of architectural success.
The tower, 222 Berkeley Street, is the first tall building ever designed by this New York architect, long known for lavish houses. Twenty floors of office space ascend above two floors of shops and lobbies on a prominent site in the Back Bay neighborhood. An instant landmark is a 10-foot-tall bronze teddy bear -- not Stern's -- that sits on the sidewalk like a huge embalmed panhandler, signaling the presence of a toy store inside. 
 Out-of-town designers who build in Boston often surpass the locals in architectural chauvinism, and 222 practically pops its studs with traditional Boston motifs -- red brick walls, limestone trim, bay windows, stuff like that. It all feels spookily hollow -- partly because, in a recession, there are hardly any tenants, but also because the building is, in every way, a simulation.
This is a papier-mache mask of architecture. Except at the lowest floors, the limestone isn't God's own limestone but precast concrete, just pretending. The red brick doesn't feel like thick clay that has been dug and shaped and cooked and piled up by calloused hands. Instead, it arrived in flat manufactured panels that were slipped into place like giant jokers in a house of cards. Palm fronds rustle in a breeze not of nature but of the air-handling system. Porticoes look like tiny Renaissance temples but lack the detail of the real, seeming to have been miniaturized in porcelain.
The building can best be thought of as a light sketch of the solid architecture of the past, a mirage of the iconic red-brick Boston. There's a kind of authenticity in its very inauthenticity. Today, when we build, we build tents. We erect a frame of steel and stretch a skin over it. The skin may be glass or stone or brick or metal, but it's still only a skin. Willy-nilly, a building becomes a wrapped package. By looking like what it really is -- a paper-thin fake -- 222 negotiates the trickiest shoal in architecture today. It "looks like Boston," but renders the resemblance as an unmistakable simulation and, therefore, as a work of our own time. In an era dominated by media imagery, 222 is a logo of the past projected on the screen of the present, taking care at every turn to remind us that it is a fiction. It could hardly be more contemporary.
Mr. Stern was out to please -- not an unreasonable goal for so public an art as architecture. In this case, it was a necessary goal. Mr. Stern got the job only when another firm was fired under pressure from community activists who hadn't liked his last building. A partner in that firm was the famous Philip Johnson, Mr. Stern's nearly lifelong mentor.
So Mr. Stern moved with caution, but also with considerable skill. Besides adopting a watercolor-of-old-Boston esthetic, he displayed a commendable sense of urbanism. He found ways to unpack his building, so it would look more like a pile of architectural blocks and less like the box they came in. He turned it sideways, reducing its bulk further. He avoided useless plazas, those patches of urban mange so common in new developments, by pulling his building's shopfronts to the edge of the sidewalk, where they would engage the pedestrian. He succeeded in creating something far more palatable than Philip Johnson's proposed building. Nobody took Stern to court, a circumstance that for an architect in Boston is tantamount to a standing ovation.
Robert Stern is a scholar as well as a designer, and his scholarship shows at 222 in the motifs drawn from architectural history. A skylit atrium recalls those of the turn-of-the-century Chicago architect Daniel Burnham. Lobby details learn from the "stripped classicism" of public buildings of the 1930's -- the last era in which Americans achieved an appropriate civic style.
But the dominant source for 222 remains the neo-Georgian (often called neo-Colonial) architecture of the early 20th century. As Mr. Stern himself has argued, those were decades in which a WASP establishment, feeling itself to be drowning in a tide of immigrant groups, struck back by reviving the architecture of the 18th century, the golden age of WASP hegemony. The Rockefellers restored Williamsburg in Virginia, and Harvard erected a virtual theme park of pseudo-Colonial red brick, buff limestone, green doors and white cupolas. As has often happened, good architecture was created in a hopeless attempt to stabilize a culture in decline.
There's a big difference, though, between Harvard's fake-Colonial and Robert Stern's. Harvard's buildings embodied a political message, if a bigoted one. And they were inhabited by a society that believed the message and dressed the part. That has changed. At 222, the population fails to cooperate in the architect's ambitions.
A long-haired messenger in bicycle tights, for instance, loping across the marble floor of Mr. Stern's grandiose lobby, transforms the building at once, by his mere presence, into a stage set in which his own role is that of grip. An attorney in running shoes and earmuffs simply by being here alchemizes 222 into a museum representation of a dead culture, becoming, herself, a tourist in that museum. A disquieting background babble from the walkie-talkies of the security guards is another clue that we must be in a museum.
Such bizarre disruptions of time and place pull the plug on what seems to be the intended political and social message of the WASP-revival architecture. At 222, we don't really experience nostalgia for a WASP past. What is unintentionally dramatized is its very opposite: the diverse, plural, conflicted, imagistic and mosaic city of today.
The architecture of 222 recalls a great sentence from Milan Kundera's novel "The Unbearable Lightness of Being": "Kitsch is taking pleasure in being moved by a mask of beauty."
The truth is that, kitsch or no, most of us probably do take pleasure in 222 as a mask of beauty. What we seem most to love, at the end of the century of modernism, is the simulation that isn't too perfect, that remains obviously a simulation. So we flock by the millions to, say, the MGM theme park at Walt Disney World, where we can move from front stage to backstage among the Hollywood sets, and thus experience them as simulations. We gravitate to the slice of space that lies between reality and its representation.
 At 222 Berkeley, we're offered that guilty delight. No doubt it's a shallow pleasure. Mr. Stern's architecture could be a lot riskier, grittier and fresher (as it is, for example, in his recent library at St. Paul's School in Concord, N.H.). But if it were, it would be that much less interesting an expression of its own moment in cultural history. Mr. Stern is now a member of the board of the Walt Disney Company. The fact arrives with the satisfying click of a shutting box. This is architecture for an age of simulation.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Robert Stern's office tower at 222 Berkeley Street--Out-of-town designers who build in Boston often surpass the locals in architectural chauvinism. (Peter Aaron/Esto)

The New York Times

 

PUB-DATE: March 29, 1992

SECTION: Section 6; Page 34; Column 1; Magazine Desk

LENGTH: 4097 words

HEADLINE: Really Cool Cars

BYLINE: By Lesley Hazleton; Lesley Hazleton is the auto columnist for Lear's magazine. Her book "Confessions of a Fast Woman" will be published by Addison-Wesley in September.

 BODY:
IT LOOKS LIKE A FUTURISTIC SHOW CAR: SILVER, with swooping aerodynamic lines. Just another sporty "concept" car, you think, until you get behind the wheel.
Then comes a moment of disorientation. There's no gearshift, you learn, because there's no clutch and no transmission. Just two small electric motors, one for each of the front wheels. A long hump down the center floor of the car looks rather like the one in old sports cars; but instead of a driveshaft there's a line of 32 lead-acid batteries under it. 
 Now you see the dials, which are centered just below the steeply raked windshield. Motor temperature, check. Miles per hour, fine. But then there's a dial for battery charge, marked in percentages. And another -- the ammeter -- marked in amps. Driving this car, you realize, requires a different frame of mind.
Turn the key, and nothing seems to happen. No sound, no vibration. But press the button marked F for forward and step on what you can't help thinking of as the gas pedal, and the force of acceleration suddenly has you pushed back against the seat.
You didn't expect this. You realize you're smiling. Surely a sexy electric car is an oxymoron.
Until this car's debut in 1990, it was. The car is the Impact, a General Motors prototype designed to break the conceptual mold of electric cars as glorified golf carts. Though there's some way to go before it will be fit for the market -- the suspension is rock hard and the motors whine like an animal in pain -- G.M. has scheduled it for production in the mid-1990's, at a price reportedly about $25,000.
The Impact is a major precursor of what appears to be an impending new era in cars: a radical change from gasoline to electricity. Even as auto makers struggle through the recession, they have been forced into an expensive, high-stakes race to determine the future.
Fueling the race, as it were, are new environmental regulations introduced by California and now under consideration in 10 Northeastern states, including New York -- potentially, a total of just over a third of the United States new-car market.
The aim of the regulations is to clean up cars, which by most accounts produce up to two-thirds of all urban smog and a quarter of the greenhouse gas carbon dioxide. The California program mandates progressively lower emissions standards, starting two years from now. At first, these can be met with alternative fuels -- reformulated gasoline, compressed natural gas, alcohols like ethanol and methanol -- and with improved combustion technologies.
But these measures will probably only produce a holding pattern on smog and greenhouse gases. As James J. MacKenzie of the World Resources Institute notes, "with auto ownership worldwide forecast to rise by up to 50 percent in the next 20 years, we simply cannot conserve our way out of the problem." Thus, the ultimate requirement of the new regulations: By 1998, 2 percent of all new cars sold by auto makers with California sales exceeding 3,000 a year will have to be Z.E.V.'s, or zero-emission vehicles; by 2001, 5 percent; and by 2003, 10 percent will have to be Z.E.V.'s.
"The die has been cast," MacKenzie says. "The only way to do this is an absolutely fundamental change in the character of the auto: its propulsion system." The hot technology of the internal-combustion engine -- known in electro-speak without any apparent irony as ICE -- will have to give way to the cool one of electricity, the mechanical engine to the electrochemical one.
As developments in major industrial markets go, all this has happened very fast, and most auto makers are now scrambling to catch up, investing heavily in R.&D. for what they still consider an uncertain market. Much of the research is directed at batteries, because at this stage no purely electric car can go more than about 100 miles before stopping for several hours to recharge. As a result, it looks as though many of the first electrics will be "hybrids" -- electric-drive cars using batteries together with small gasoline engines to recharge the batteries and extend the car's range. Some researchers say that a battery powerful enough to drive a pure electric as fast and as far as a gasoline car may well be on the market by 1998. And in the longer run, researchers are looking to hydrogen-powered fuel cells to replace batteries.
"THIS DECADE IS GOING TO BE BY FAR the most exciting ever in cars," says Paul MacCready, head of Aerovironment, the company that led the Impact development team. "It's going to be somewhat like the 30's in aviation, when you went from canvas and sticks to airliners and fighters in just a few years."
MacCready is not the kind of man you would expect G.M. to trust farther than it can see: too iconoclastic, you would think, for a major American corporation. A three-time United States national soaring champion, he designed a series of solar-powered planes in the late 70's. Then, in what seemed the height of eccentricity, he led a team that built a huge radio-controlled, wing-flapping, flying replica of the largest animal ever to have flown -- the dinosaur-age Quetzal coatlus northropi, or pterodactyl, with a 36-foot wingspan. That, he said, was to show the connection between the evolution of natural flight and technological flight.
Far from a mad scientist, though, MacCready is a restrained, cerebral, intensely practical engineer with a Ph.D. in aeronautics from Caltech and a long string of awards and honors. No fewer than five Aerovironment inventions have been acquired by the Smithsonian, including the Gossamer Condor plane -- the first plane powered solely by a human to achieve sustained, controlled flight -- and the Sunraycer car, G.M.'s record-breaking entry to the first World Solar Challenge Race in Australia in 1987.
After the Sunraycer's success, G.M. bought 15 percent of MacCready's company and gave him and his team the go-ahead to develop an electric car. They were apparently thinking of it primarily as a show car, but MacCready had other ideas. In just under a year, his team developed the Impact, with its top speed of more than 100 m.p.h. and acceleration from 0 to 60 m.p.h. in eight seconds.
Most electric cars developed in the previous 20 years had been conversions of gasoline cars. They worked, but not very well. By redesigning the car from the ground up, MacCready's team transformed the electric car from a technological oddity into a stylish practicality.
They literally reinvented the wheels: made out of aluminum, with tires specially designed to reduce drag, they help make the Impact so energy-efficient that if it were to run on gasoline, it would use only one-third the amount required by a typical internal-combustion car. And in a process known as regenerative braking, the brakes themselves act as small generators, feeding energy back into the batteries and recharging them.
"We had to rethink the whole question of automotive design from the point of view of efficiency the way it's pursued in the aerospace industry," MacCready says, sitting in his sparse office in Monrovia, Calif. "And the odd thing is that nobody had ever looked at a car from that point of view before. There'd never been any need to."
MacCready is no car enthusiast. He puts down the American obsession with high-powered, energy-intensive cars in his low-key, slightly sardonic manner: "If you look at birds like the albatross, you'll see they can spend days soaring over the ocean, with magnificent maneuverability. Then at the other end of the spectrum, there's the peacock, stuck on the ground, flying at most up to a low branch. The mechanical part of a peacock's flying function is minimal, so much effort goes into other things like the large tail, which hampers flying but boy, does it make the females quiver."
The Impact promptly made a lot of auto journalists quiver, a thing they normally do only for peacock cars. The Impact's team had created the automotive equivalent of a peacock that could fly like an albatross, proving, as MacCready says, that "styling and efficiency don't have to be incompatible." And the very fact of its existence undoubtedly influenced California's decision to go ahead with electric-vehicle regulations. MacCready had demonstrated that the technology already existed.
WITH SEVERAL MORE STATES looking closely at the California program, support for electric car production seems to have already "passed critical mass," as one advocate puts it. The European Community will probably adopt a similar measure in the next few years, as will Japan, which already aims to have 200,000 electric cars on the road by the year 2000. Meanwhile, in February the Senate overwhelming passed a bipartisan energy bill mandating the use of alternative fuels or electricity for all government fleets and most commercial ones by 2000. A similar if somewhat weaker House bill is expected before the Easter recess.
But some critics call this unseemly haste. Rushing a new technology onto the market may be counterproductive, they say. David E. Cole, director of the University of Michigan Office for the Study of Automotive Transportation, argues that scientific research on pollution and the greenhouse effect is still contradictory. He warns that demanding that Detroit right now invest in a whole new technology could further jeopardize the position of the American auto industry.
James P. Womack, a principal research scientist in technological policy at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, cautions that Detroit's problems could yet undermine the new regulations. "There's always the possibility that laws can get changed," he says. "It's true there hasn't been much inclination to believe Detroit when they've said they couldn't do something, and then done it under the regulatory gun, as with catalytic converters. But if you get to the late 90's and Detroit says they can't make the deadlines for financial reasons, it's quite possible there'll be delays in the mandates. So I don't know how much voltage there is, so to speak, behind the regulatory drive for electronics. There's a lot of room for poker-playing here."
Now that the Japanese have entered the poker game, however, nobody can afford to drop out. "A whole electric-vehicle industry has suddenly come into being," one auto executive says. "Our Rolodexes have been transformed in the past year." And even though the regulations call only for a gradual conversion to Z.E.V.'s, the major producers know they will have to be there when the laws kick in.
Some companies, including Ford and Chrysler, are working on electric vans rather than cars. Their assumption is that Federal legislation and market signals virtually guarantee that the initial demand for electrics is more likely to be for service and delivery vehicles than for private cars. But many others are going straight for the far larger private market, and their cars are attracting a lot of attention, both from potential buyers and from other companies.
The first modern electric car to come onto the American market is scheduled to start production a year from now. Code-named the LA301, it was commissioned by the city of Los Angeles and will be built by a small British-Swedish consortium called Clean Air Transport. A four-passenger hybrid commuter car, it runs on lead-acid batteries plus a small gasoline engine that cuts in above 30 m.p.h. or when peak power is needed, extending the car's 60-mile electric range to over 150 miles. It doesn't have the flair or technical sophistication of the Impact. And because of its small production run -- 1,000 in 1993 and 5,000 in each of the two succeeding years -- its projected cost is $25,000.
A prime contender for the mid-90's is the Volkswagen Chico, a small city runabout. Another hybrid, it uses a two-cylinder gasoline engine to recharge nickel-hydride batteries. The batteries alone give it a range of 12 to 13 miles for nonpolluting city driving, while the gasoline range is about 300 miles, with a top speed of 81 m.p.h. Some early reports price it at a very attractive $7,000, but initially it is likely to cost far more.
Volkswagen is also working on a joint venture with Swatch to produce electric cars, capitalizing on the Swiss watchmaker's reputation for inexpensive quality.
In Japan, radical prototypes have pointed the way to a more powerful electric future. Nissan introduced its F.E.V. (future electric vehicle) last year, with nickel-cadmium batteries fully rechargeable in just 15 minutes, compared with a minimum of three hours for most other batteries. That, however, is at 440 volts, which is far beyond home capacity and therefore impractical right now.
Another Japanese prototype is setting an interesting precedent: it was developed not by an auto maker but by Tokyo Power and Electric, the largest privately owned utility in the world. Its Iza is a sleek four-seater with nickel-cadmium batteries, a top speed of 109 m.p.h. and a record-breaking range of 340 miles. The Iza is an expensive proposition, however, and is not scheduled for production.
BMW intends to produce two sophisticated electrics -- the E1, promptly dubbed "the electric egg" by the automotive press because of its shape, and the larger E2 sedan unveiled in January at the Los Angeles Auto Show, with sodium-sulfur batteries, rear-wheel drive, a top speed of 75 m.p.h. and a maximum range of 267 miles. To Americans, one of the most striking features of the E1 and E2 is that they are the first BMW's to be powered by American-made motors. And these come not from a major supplier, but from a small company called Unique Mobility.
AT FIRST BLUSH, RAY A. GEDDES, THE mustachioed chairman of Unique Mobility, is an unlikely proponent of electric technology. A lawyer with an M.B.A. from the University of Michigan, he spent 12 years as Ford's GT and sports-car manager, supervising Shelby Cobras, high-performance Mustangs and the like. In 1981, he got a call from his cousin, a radiologist who had invested in Unique Mobility. "He was worried about the way the company was going," Geddes says. "He wanted me to come help protect his investment." Like all good Detroit people at the time, Geddes scorned the very idea of electric cars. "I had to be dragged in," he says, grinning. "Definitely under duress." Yet what he saw was so interesting that by 1983 he was working full time at the firm's headquarters in Englewood, Colo.
Unique had built a novel direct-current motor that looked so promising that Geddes decided to sell off the company's real estate -- including a race track -- to concentrate on its development.
Standard electric motors pose a difficult trade-off for engineers. They can be designed for torque, which provides acceleration, or for power, which allows steady operation at high speed. But getting both characteristics -- which is what most American drivers expect in a car -- in a motor that is sufficiently small, light and inexpensive for a mass-marketed automobile is a vexing problem.
The patented Unique motor uses less iron and copper than regular electric motors, making it lighter. More important, the motor's copper wire is wrapped in a special configuration and the current is controlled electronically to provide acceleration or high-speed cruising power as needed, and without a transmission. It is thus extraordinarily efficient, producing up to two horsepower per pound of motor weight -- almost twice as much as the Impact's variable-speed AC motors and more than five times that of conventional DC motors.
Though Geddes may have gotten out of racing, the racing hasn't gotten out of him. Pinned to his office wall are engineering drawings for an electric Grand Prix race car. "Racing can accelerate this technology," he says, "just as it did for gasoline technology. Eventually, electric-drive race cars with a motor in each wheel will run circles around other kinds."
With the legendary racing figure Carroll Shelby a former board member of Unique Mobility and still a shareholder, it may not be that long until the drawings move from Geddes's wall to the production studio. And another future indicator is the fact that the company's largest shareholder, Alcan, is working on an aluminum-air battery with 10 times the energy density of lead-acid, and far longer life due to an exchangeable aluminum cassette that can simply be inserted into the battery casing.
The battery connection is important. Electric motors are well advanced, but batteries have a long way to go. In fact the phrase most used about batteries is "the Achilles' heel of the electric car." Right now, they provide too limited a range, take too long to recharge and have too short a life to make electrics fully competitive in cost and performance with gasoline cars.
The range problem is more psychological than practical, but none the less real for that. The average daily trip of an American car is 22 miles, well within the range of even the most modest electrics. But drivers don't like to think of themselves as average. And the limitations challenge a basic piece of American mythology: the freedom to range.
The energy and durability of batteries present more serious problems. Right now, new batteries mean an expenditure of some $1,500 every two years or so. And no single kind of battery seems to do everything that's needed. Sodium sulfur, for instance, holds about three times the energy per pound weight as lead acid but typically delivers that energy to the motors less than half as fast.
The lead-acid batteries in the Impact produce nearly twice the energy per pound weight of current car batteries -- "we think our battery people sneaked some kryptonite in there," jokes one of the engineers working on the production model -- and take only three hours to recharge. But three hours is about 2 hours and 57 minutes too long when compared with the "recharge" time for gasoline motors.
Detroit's Big Three have joined with major utilities and the Department of Energy to form the Advanced Battery Consortium, a billion-dollar effort to develop the ideal battery. The group's goal is to extend battery life to 5 years by 1994 and to 10 years by the end of the decade, while simultaneously bringing down costs and extending range.
Meanwhile, auto makers and utilities are on the verge of agreement on a standard charging system, while the National Highway Traffic Safety Administration has begun setting safety standards for electric cars. For a change, auto makers are hoping the agency acts swiftly; nobody wants to put a car on the market that suddenly has to meet new safety standards.
The standards are likely to include disabling mechanisms to disconnect the batteries from the rest of the car automatically during servicing and in crashes, thus preventing electric shock. They may also require that batteries be sealed and packed behind flame barriers to prevent explosions and keep toxic and often highly corrosive acids from spraying the cars' inhabitants in an accident.
Despite the intensive investment of time and money, batteries may be only an interim means of powering the electric car. The ultimate problem is that the electricity to charge them still has to be produced somewhere. In the first few years, as electric cars are phased onto the market, the present power capacity will suffice. But eventually, more will be required, and that means either more nuclear plants, which seem highly unlikely in the United States, more coal and gas-fired plants, or solar generation.
In the long run, though, most experts predict that this whole issue will be resolved by the use of a far more promising energy source: hydrogen. In fact, it could be the ultimate clean fuel.
HYDROGEN IS THE space-age fuel par excellence. The space shuttle uses hydrogen fuel cells to provide on-board electricity and drinking water. Yet one of the leading firms working on hydrogen-powered cars developed its technology not hundreds of miles above the earth, but below it: specifically, underwater.
Energy Partners, based in West Palm Beach, Fla., was founded by John H. Perry Jr., a former newspaper and cable television owner who introduced computerized typesetting into the newsroom. In the 60's, Perry began producing small manned submarines for the offshore oil industry, eventually cornering 90 percent of the market. He also built the hydrogen fuel-cell-powered Hydrolab, in which astronauts trained underwater for the weightlessness of space, and began to experiment with fuel cells in submarines.
Now 74 years old and looking more like a retired naval captain than a captain of industry, Perry is high on hydrogen, and curiously, considering that the source of a considerable part of his fortune is in the oil-drilling business, on the idea of clean energy. "The fuel cell," he says, "is the silicon chip of the hydrogen age."
Fuel cells were bulky and very expensive until the development some years ago of proton exchange membranes, or P.E.M.'s for short. A P.E.M. is a variety of Teflon that looks like a regular sheet of transparent plastic. When treated with platinum as a catalyst, it splits hydrogen and separates out its electrons to form electricity. A series of P.E.M.'s stacked one on top of another like layers of meat in a sandwich produces a fuel cell that is light, small and potentially cheap enough to use in a car.
Hydrogen can be burned in an internal-combustion engine -- BMW, Mercedes-Benz and Mazda all have prototype internal-combustion cars working on hydrogen fuel -- but when used instead to produce electricity in a fuel cell, it will take that same car twice as far. A fuel cell in an electric car as aerodynamic as the Impact could increase its range to about 400 miles, and reduce its recharge time to two or three minutes.
Energy Partners plans to build a "proof of concept" car, rather than a production prototype, running on two hydrogen fuel cells.With it, Perry hopes to demonstrate that such cars can be ready for the mass market by the end of the decade, rather than 20 years from now, as most experts predict.
Though the car will use gaseous hydrogen as its fuel for now, a better and safer option for the future would be to store the hydrogen in an on-board tank containing a granular metal alloy that holds hydrogen gas in nonvolatile form, releasing it to the fuel cell as required.
One of the many advantages of hydrogen as an energy carrier is fuel flexibility: hydrogen can be made from just about anything. It could be reformed either aboard the car or at the service station from methanol, ethanol or natural gas. It could even be produced by using solar power to electrolyze water.
It seems the perfect fantasy: a car running, basically, on sun and water. But outside Munich, Germany, an experimental power plant is already producing hydrogen from solar power and water. Solar technology may be nowhere near the stage where it could power a family car directly, but its potential to power the car indirectly, by producing hydrogen, has now been established.
THE TECHNOLOGY for a new era in cars already exists. The problem now is building a market large enough to justify the large production runs that will make electric cars more economical. Some of the options include regulation, as is being done right now, and incentives, like higher gas taxes or tax breaks for buyers of electric cars.
"Economics underlies this whole issue," MacCready says. "Cars are not airplanes. You have to make them affordable. The challenge is how to get some of the efficiency of the culture of aviation into the mass-market car culture, which is all the trickier when gasoline is cheaper than bottled water.
"If some fearless politician with enough charisma to carry it off could introduce a slowly rising tax on gasoline to, say, $4 a gallon by the end of the decade, the national debt would be pretty much taken care of, the economy would be revitalized, everyone's health would be better, and we'd be free of independence on foreign oil," he says. "But it's not going to happen."
With a clear Federal policy still missing, the coming decade will be one of rapid development and high risk for auto makers. State regulations have set the pace. Now the question is whether consumers are ready to make the leap. If they are, it seems certain that within the next 10 years, cars will finally emerge from the century-old technology of gasoline and begin to catch up with the level of technology we use in the rest of our lives. Within 20 years, they may even catch up with the space age.

PUB-DATE: March 29, 1992

SECTION: Section 3; Page 13; Column 2; Financial Desk

LENGTH: 746 words

HEADLINE: FORUM;
Dick Darman's Spending Ways

BYLINE: By DANIEL J. MITCHELL; Daniel J. Mitchell is a senior fellow at the Heritage Foundation, a public policy research group in Washington.

 BODY:
According to Washington budget lore, Richard Darman's extraordinary talents and pragmatic approach have restored the Office of Management and Budget's credibility, which was allegedly bruised under the ideological tenure of Jim Miller, its director during President Reagan's second term. But if the numbers in O.M.B.'s recently released historical tables are accurate, Mr. Darman's reign has been an unmitigated disaster. By every conceivable measure -- Federal spending, budget deficits, economic assumptions -- he has presided over fiscal calamity. It appears that his energies have instead gone toward preserving his inflated reputation.
Self-proclaimed Washington budget experts still dismiss Mr. Miller as a lightweight, yet he accomplished what he set out to do: control the growth of domestic spending and reduce the deficit. The budget deficit declined by $59 billion between 1985 and 1989 under Mr. Miller (years are fiscal years, starting Oct. 1 of the previous calendar year). That compares with an jump of $246.2 billion in just three years under Mr. Darman. And the deposit insurance bailout cannot be blamed for the discrepancy. Excluding deposit insurance spending, the deficit fell by $83 billion under Mr. Miller, but climbed $188.1 billion under Mr. Darman. 
 Perhaps more importantly, the deficit as a share of the nation's output declined every year under Mr. Miller, falling from 5.3 percent of gross domestic product in 1985 to 3.0 percent in 1989. By comparison, the deficit has climbed every year under Mr. Darman and is expected to consume 6.8 percent of G.D.P. this year. Once again, the exclusion of deposit insurance has no effect.
The reason for the climb under Mr. Darman is the explosion of Federal domestic spending. While Jim Miller held spending growth to an average of $1.08 billion annually (in constant 1987 dollars), spending has jumped an astounding $43.5 billion annually under Mr. Darman. The figures, moreover, exclude not only deposit insurance, but also interest on the national debt.
No matter how it is measured, domestic spending is expanding at a phenomenal clip. After adjusting for inflation, domestic spending climbed by an average of only two-tenths of 1 percent under Mr. Miller, compared with an average of 7.0 percent annually under Mr. Darman.
Mr. Darman's supporters correctly argue that much of the higher spending is due to entitlements like Social Security and Medicare that are mandated by existing law. But the same entitlement programs existed when Mr. Miller was O.M.B. director. The difference? While Mr. Miller sought to reform entitlement programs to restrict the growth of spending, Mr. Darman has watched Congress expand the programs and increase taxpayer liability. As a result, inflation-adjusted entitlement spending under Mr. Darman is increasing by an average of 7.8 percent annually, compared with only 0.1 percent in the Miller years.
Knowledgeable budget observers may point out that such comparisons are skewed since Mr. Miller's tenure coincided with the enactment of the Gramm-Rudman Deficit Reduction Act. Jim Miller's fine record is indeed due in part to Gramm-Rudman, but Gramm-Rudman is also due in part to Jim Miller, whose early project as budget director in 1985 was to work with his friend Senator Phil Gramm, Republican of Texas, to push through Gramm-Rudman.
Mr. Darman, in contrast, agreed as part of the catastrophic budget agreement of 1990 to eviscerate Gramm-Rudman, eliminating the fixed annual deficit targets that were the heart and soul of the law. In its place he created the Budget Enforcement Act, which is supposed to cap domestic discretionary spending but has actually allowed it to grow nearly five times faster than it did under Gramm-Rudman.
Finally, while Jim Miller was a staunch opponent of tax increases, Dick Darman is perhaps most infamous for engineering the President's reversal on his no-new-taxes pledge, which has slowed economic growth, thereby reducing tax revenues. Revenues for the 1991-1995 time period are now projected to be $497 billion lower than estimated back in the summer of 1990 -- before the tax increase was enacted.
Dick Darman may be adept at generating good press, but his talents do not include managing the budget of the United States Government. If the President wishes to extract himself from the fiscal mess Mr. Darman created, he should fire Mr. Darman and ask someone with a proven record to take the job.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 6; Metropolitan Desk

HEADLINE: THE BUDGET AGREEMENT;
DINKINS DENOUNCES DETAILS OF ACCORD ON ALBANY BUDGET

BYLINE: By SAM HOWE VERHOVEK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ALBANY, March 29

 BODY:
As details emerged today about local-aid cuts and new fees in the budget agreement reached by state leaders late Saturday, Mayor David N. Dinkins denounced the plan as too harmful to New York City and urged lawmakers to revise it.
The Mayor's protests came as Senate and Assembly leaders of both major parties began drafting the bill intended to be New York State's first on-time budget in eight years. The 1992-93 fiscal year starts on Wednesday, with April 1 the legal -- if seldom observed -- deadline for the budget. 
 Dinkins administration officials said today that the agreement would cost the city as much as $375 million -- $100 million more than the Mayor had anticipated when he drafted his own budget proposal in January. [Page B7.]
The Mayor, through a spokesman, described the $30.8 billion spending plan as "a hurtful" budget. "At the current moment we are asking lawmakers to vote no," Mr. Dinkins said.
 
'Going to Be on Time'
 But Gov. Mario M. Cuomo and the legislative leaders predicted that the budget from the agreement, which they reached after five months of negotiations, would be quickly ratified by both houses. "We're going to have a budget," Mr. Cuomo told reporters today. "It's going to be on time."
Rank-and-file lawmakers returned to Albany this evening and were briefed on details of the agreement during party conferences in each house. While many legislators voiced displeasure over spending cuts, there was a general consensus that the pact would survive essentially intact.
Mr. Cuomo, who cited the state's fiscal morass as his only obstacle when he announced last Dec. 20 that he would not run for President, flatly ruled out the possibility that he would enter the race now. He repeated his view that Gov. Bill Clinton of Arkanasas would "probably" be his party's nominee.
The budget pact seems to have been reached now in large measure because lawmakers feared that another late budget would only deepen the election-year wrath of voters who say they are fed up with politicians to begin with. It would cut many local-aid programs and freeze others, including school aid, at the present year's levels.
It would also impose hundreds of millions of dollars in new state fees on insurers, hospitals and nursing homes, all of which assert that they will have to pass these costs on to the consumers. [Page B7.]
And, as expected, it would defer about $1 billion in scheduled income tax cuts for New Yorkers. The Legislature passed the cuts several years ago during much rosier fiscal times.
Cuomo administration officials said privately this weekend that the Governor was furious with Mayor Dinkins's last-minute objections, arguing that they could make the budget late and risk another painful lowering of the state's already abysmal credit rating.
Publicly, the Governor chalked up much of the Mayor's dissatisfaction to political expediency. "If I were Mayor, I think I'd be doing about what he's doing," Mr. Cuomo said. "I might be wrong, but it's what I'd be doing."
When asked about a possible Cuomo draft at the Democratic National Convention, the Governor replied, "To answer that question is to encourage its consideration, which I do not wish to do."
City officials, meanwhile, said they would target their lobbying efforts at the Democratic-led Assembly and its new Speaker, Saul Weprin. While Mr. Weprin seems to have had a major influence on crafting an on-time budget, city officials depicted him as having caved in on several issues vital to the city in his haste to realize what would be, by Albany standards, a stunning achievement.
But Mr. Weprin angrily rejected that notion today, saying his chamber had done as well as it possibly could for the city. "I had to make a determination -- if the budget were late, the city would be hurt worse," he said in an interview.
 
Shift in Program Cost
 But Dinkins administration officials expressed anger because the agreement would shift the burden of paying for tens of millions of dollars in mandated programs from the state to the city. These included a program intended to help poor people with their heating bills, which was expected to cost $47 million. A new cap on state payments for foster care and day care would cost another $40 million.
The budget deal will apparently spare bus and subway riders in New York City from any further fare increase this year -- the fare already rose from $1.15 to $1.25 on Jan. 1 -- but makes no guarantees for 1993. And after a successful squawk by suburban Republicans, it insures that city officials will be prevented from using any of a $90 million surplus in the commuter railroads' budget that they had aggressively sought.
To no one's surprise, the budget accord quickly generated condemnation from many quarters, including the insurance companies, health-care providers and petroleum dealers who would bear the brunt of the new fees. The dealers said a new plan to accelerate their sales-tax payments would force them to add a penny or more to the retail price of a gallon of gasoline.
 
'Callous and Insensitive'
 Dennis Rivera, the head of Local 1199 of the health-care workers' union, said all three of the state's top leaders -- Mr. Cuomo, Mr. Weprin and Ralph J. Marino, the leader of the Republican-controlled State Senate -- had been "callous and insensitive" in putting together the new budget.
But Mr. Cuomo sought to co-opt much of the disgruntlement, arguing that the recession and his desire to avoid any major tax increases had left the state little choice but to adopt a painful budget. "There's a great deal here that I'm not happy about," he said.
One thing Mr. Cuomo and the legislative leaders said they were happy about was what they described as the unusually small amount of "one shots," or one-time revenues in the budget. In past years, credit raters have expressed alarm about the use of one-shots as a budget-balancing tool since they represent revenue that the state cannot be assured of replacing in subsequent years.
 
Significant Decline
 Although the relative secrecy of the budget deal prevented reporters from verifying the claim today, Cuomo administration officials said there would be less than $500 million of one-shots in the 1992-93 plan, down significantly from $1.3 billion in this year's budget and $2.7 billion the year before.
The credit rating agencies were not officially apprised of the plan, so they were unable to pronounce any opinions on it. But the state leaders said they were confident it would head off any possibility of a further decline in the state's credit rating, which was reduced earlier this year to the second-lowest of any state in the nation, behind Massachusetts.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 1; Financial Desk

LENGTH: 2501 words

HEADLINE: BANKS FEAR LOSSES AS BUILDER REELS

BYLINE: By RICHARD D. HYLTON

 BODY:
Many of the largest banks in North America, Europe and Asia lent billions of dollars to Olympia & York Developments Ltd., the world's largest private real estate company, even though it refused to disclose all its financial obligations and would not let the banks review its books.
Now the company, owned by the Reichmann family of Toronto, is being pummeled by $18.5 billion in debt, depressed property markets and a series of disastrous investments, and the banks are nervously lining up to see what their losses are likely to be. 
 And after decades of closely guarding its finances, Olympia & York has been forced to agree to open its books next Monday and show its creditor banks who is owed what, and on what terms.
 
A 3-Legged Colossus
 Olympia & York is the largest owner of office space in New York City, where its properties include the World Financial Center in Manhattan and more than a dozen other office towers. It is also building the immense and troubled Canary Wharf office complex in London.
With the veil now slowly being lifted from Olympia & York, a picture is emerging of a three-legged colossus with debts of Byzantine complexity.
The two most troubled legs of the company are its New York and London real estate holdings; the Canadian assets are believed to have the most value. No one but the Reichmanns and their lawyers are sure of the value of the empire after the billions of dollars of debt are subtracted. Very possibly, it is a negative number.
Some loans secured by the first mortgage on valuable assets, like Scotia Plaza in Toronto, are likely to be secure, but some banks may wind up writing off as losses a portion of the billions lent against the company's general credit.
"Unsecured lending without knowing the full financial picture is very dangerous," said H. Rodgin Cohen, a partner with the law firm of Sullivan & Cromwell in New York and a specialist on banking law. "But secured lending based on the value of specific projects is perfectly reasonable without knowing the full financial picture."
On Friday, about 20 of the largest bank lenders were introduced to the new management and advisers at Olympia & York, in particular Thomas S. Johnson, the former president of the Manufacturers Hanover Trust Company, and Robert S. Miller, former vice chairman of the Chrysler Corporation and now a partner of James D. Wolfensohn Inc., an advisory firm.
The banks were told that the advisers were still combing through the company's books, trying to determine what the company's short-term cash needs would be.
Olympia & York is carrying about $15 billion in real estate debt and more than $3 billion more on its oil and gas subsidiaries. With the company facing a string of large debt payments that it cannot meet, it will be pressing its banks for even more money in short-term loans and for more lenient terms on its existing debt.
Some of the world's major banks, including Citibank and the Chemical Banking Corporation, lent the company at least $10 billion for real estate, and Olympia & York borrowed billions more by selling bonds.
 
Hard-to-Measure Signs of Trouble
 The signs of trouble at Olympia & York have been emerging for at least two years but could not be measured because the company's finances were so closely guarded. And the company always vehemently denied it was having difficulty, even when in 1990 it sought to raise cash by trying to sell 20 percent of its United States property portfolio and then trying to sell its oil and forestry businesses. Both sale attempts were unsuccessful.
Last June, it borrowed $160 million from a group of banks, sold property in Florida, sold assets of its pipeline business and got a Hong Kong billionaire, Li Kai-Shing, to buy a stake in one of its troubled buildings.
"To the extent that borrowers approached the banks and the banks did not complete due diligence, it is partly the banks' fault," said Thomas Hanley, a managing director and bank analyst at the First Boston Corporation. "If you combine the lax underwriting standards with some of the most radical changes in the external environment during the past 50 years, then financial shocks should be expected."
 
The Leading Lenders
 Citibank is believed to have lent roughly half a billion dollars to Olympia & York, but it may have sold pieces of those loans to other banks. Chemical Bank, which recently merged with Manufacturers Hanover, is believed to hold about $250 million of loans to the company, one banker said.
Five large Canadian banks are believed to have the largest loans to the company, but European banks are also owed billions, as are a host of Japanese lenders.
Some of Olympia & York's creditors are the same banks that lent Donald J. Trump nearly $2 billion in the 1980's without fully reviewing his financial records or his property appraisals. Those banks were stunned in 1990 to find that Mr. Trump had overborrowed, was short of cash, and that property values had fallen so drastically.
And last year, many of those same banks again expressed horror when Peter S. Kalikow, one of New York's largest developers -- now under Chapter 11 bankruptcy protection -- produced financial records that their accountants called Dickensian. Loans totaling hundreds of millions had been commingled in a single account with little regard for which projects the monies were used for.
Several bankers have said they did not fully understand how volatile real estate values could be and often loosened underwriting standards because real estate loans were highly profitable before the market collapsed.
 
Many New York Buildings
 In New York City, Olympia & York owns about 22 million square feet of space. In addition to the World Financial Center at Battery Park City in lower Manhattan, it owns many low-profile but first-rate buildings.
But the problem Olympia & York faces in New York is that it borrowed heavily against its properties at the height of the real estate market. Now that values are down sharply, the properties are worth much less than the more than $5.5 billion of debt against them.
Many of New York's top real estate analysts and executives say privately that there is little or no remaining equity value in the company's New York portfolio. Although some properties provide more than enough cash to service the debt on them, vacancies are growing as leases mature and rents in New York -- especially in lower Manhattan, where most of the buildings are situated -- are also down drastically.
The company's North American property portfolio consists of almost 40 million square feet of mainly office space: 54 percent in New York City, 17 percent in Toronto, 10 percent in Calgary, 5 percent in Ottawa and the balance scattered among smaller American cities, according to Dominion Bond Rating Service Ltd. Olympia & York has an overall vacancy rate of 10 percent in its North American portfolio; but that rate is rising.
Figures supplied by Olympia & York indicate that leases representing 5 percent of the space will end this year and about 7 percent next year.
Olympia & York financed its $3 billion equity contribution to Canary Wharf largely by remortgaging some older New York buildings. As the debt on some of those mortgages mature, the company must find a way of refinancing hundreds of millions of dollars in mortgages.
 
A Close Call
 It had a close call last year when the $450 million mortgage on One Liberty Plaza that was financed by Japan's Sanwa Bank came due. Olympia & York could not get refinancing and it could not pay off the debt. It avoided a crisis only by persuading Sanwa to reduce the mortgage by about $100 million and to grant a one-year extension for repayment.
The core of the New York portfolio consists of several towers acquired from the Uris division of the National Kinney Corporation in 1977, during a severe property recession. Olympia & York held onto these 20- to 30-year-old buildings and saw their market value rise with the upturn in the mid-1980's, then mortgaged the buildings for billions and continued its expansion.
Among the more troubled of those properties are 60 Broad Street, which is almost 70 percent empty; 2 Broadway, a behemoth that is 40 percent vacant, and 320 Park Avenue, from which the ITT Corporation moved its headquarters.
Another headache is 55 Water Street, on which Olympia & York put a $548 million Eurobond mortgage in 1986. This large office center is no longer worth the roughly $150 a square foot value that the mortgage put on it, judging by the decline in New York office properties.
Other buildings now generating enough cash to cover debt costs will see their cash flow shrink as leases mature and tenants demand rent cuts or move out. In addition, Olympia & York is short on the cash it needs to renovate some of its older properties and make improvements necessary to lure potential renters.
 
A Risky Wager On London's Docks
 The London picture is as gloomy. The Reichmanns' 71-acre Canary Wharf project was from the start regarded as an immense bet on the market for modern office space in London. A $7 billion project that is Europe's biggest office development, Canary Wharf is scheduled to have 10 million square feet of offices, 500,000 square feet of stores, a 400-room hotel, restaurants and other amenities.
According to Dominion Bond Rating Service, the company needs about $720 million more in financing to complete the first phase of the project and the first two buildings of the second phase.
Strongly supported by the Conservative Government of Prime Minister Margaret Thatcher because it promised to revive London's derelict Docklands area and insure that city's future as Europe's international business center, Canary Wharf now seems to many a symbol of the Tories' coziness with wealthy developers. And with British elections scheduled for April 9 and Labor leading in the polls, there is no expectation that the Government will spring forward to help the Reichmanns.
 
Enormous Cash Drain
 So far, Canary Wharf has proved little more than an enormous cash drain. The cash being generated by the first phase of the project, which is largely complete, is far from enough to cover its expenses, partly because tenants were lured with incentives like several years of free rent.
With London property in a severe slump caused by overbuilding, rents are depressed and falling still, and British and European banks are reluctant to extend new real estate loans. That is making it difficult for Olympia & York to lease the 40 percent of the completed space still vacant at Canary Wharf. And with the company acknowledging that it is unable to meet its near-term obligations, it is likely to be more difficult to attract first-class corporate tenants.
The greatest threat to Canary Wharf is the strong possibility that Olympia & York will not be able to provide the 400 million ($697.3 million) it promised the British Government to help pay for the completion of the Jubilee subway line.
The line, scheduled to be completed in 1996 at a cost of $2.27 billion, would connect Canary Wharf, which is in an out-of-the-way site, to the rest of London. A contract that was scheduled to be signed between the Government and Olympia & York at the end of the month has been delayed, in part because the company would be hard pressed to come up with the first 40 million payment it agreed to provide.
Without the completion of the line on schedule, Canary Wharf's value will be further damaged. Indeed, its value as collateral is already questionable because the banks are unlikely to be able to find a buyer should they ever foreclose.
 
Valuable Canadian Assets
 Banks have lent Olympia & York about 700 million ($1.22 billion) on Canary Wharf, and the company to reduce its $3 billion in the project. Olympia & York now has about $2 billion of its own money in Canary Wharf. . A meeting had been scheduled in London today between the company and and some British and European banks that helped finance Canary Wharf. It was put off to await the results of the April 6 meeting at which Olympia & York will open its books.
On Saturday, Michael Dennis, chief executive of Olympia & York/Canary Wharf Ltd., said that Canary Wharf's lenders had agreed on March 20 to provide an emergency loan of 52 million ($90.6 million) that would keep work on the project going "through the next month."
It is in Olympia & York's Canadian assets that the banks are likely to find additional collateral worth holding. The portfolio of oil, gas, forestry, real estate and financial company stocks is worth more than $3 billion.
The holdings include a 75 percent controlling interest in Gulf Canada Resources Ltd., a major oil company; an 82 percent interest in Abitibi-Price Inc., a pulp and paper company, and a 89.3 percent GW Utilities Ltd., an oil and gas concern. Olympia & York also has smaller takes in some of Canada's largest property and financial companies.
The company's stock portfolio has dropped 44 percent in value in the last two years and 26 percent last year alone.
 
Squeezing Out Cash In Stock Dividends
 To some extent, the Reichmanns have been able to squeeze cash from the companies they control by paying out large dividends and liquidating assets, but efforts to sell Gulf Canada and Abitibi-Price, the two most valuable holdings, have been unsuccessful.
The company's Toronto real estate holdings have fallen in value over the past two years, but not as dramatically as the office space in New York. The company has 5.4 million square feet of prime Toronto office space and the value of some of those buildings is widely believed to be in excess of the debt several Canadian banks have against them. Partly because of this, the Canadian banks control of some of Olympia & York's most valuable assets.
 
Leaders Among the Lenders
 Already emerging as leaders among the banks are the Canadian Imperial Bank of Commerce, Citibank and the Hongkong Shanghai Banking Corporation. The three banks will direct the creditors' committee.
In all, Olympia & York currently has about 15 outstanding bond issues and most are backed by mortgages on specific buildings, although some are also backed by Olympia and York's shares in its energy and forestry companies.
Several rating agencies have downgraded some of Olympia & York's bonds because of the deterioration of the company's credit position and the individual buildings backing the bonds.
Most of the bonds trade in London on the Euro market, and Europeans hold large chunks of these securities. Since news of Olympia & York's problems, the prices of many of these bonds have fallen, but there has been little trading.
Some bonds, however, have maintained their high quality ratings and are expected to trade near face value because the properties on which they have a first-mortgage benefit from long-term leases with solid tenants and generate a lot of cash.
