HEADLINE: '88 Welfare Act Is Falling Short, Researchers Say

BYLINE: By JASON DePARLE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
When Congress passed a much-celebrated welfare law in 1988, its supporters, including Senator Daniel Patrick Moynihan, said it would bring revolutionary changes to the system.
But the first major study of its implementation says the program's accomplishments fall far short of radical change. While the law has succeeded in expanding services, the report found, it is failing to convert welfare from a system that permits long-term dependency to one that stresses skills, jobs and financial independence. 
 The report, to be released Monday at a Senate hearing, attributes the problems to a lack of political commitment and a recession-driven shortage of money.
 
Caseloads at Record High
 "The potential of the program hasn't been fully realized," said Irene Lurie, a professor of public affairs at the State University of New York in Albany, who co-wrote the report. "There have been significant changes, but there hasn't been what was advertised in Washington: the most sweeping reform in 50 years."
The findings come as welfare caseloads have reached record highs and elected officials from President Bush on down are making sharp attacks on the system and the 13 million people who rely on it.
In a telephone interview Friday, Senator Moynihan said that the problems were predictable and that he remained "very encouraged" about the program's prospects. "We always said when we put the statute in place that we wouldn't really know if it would work until the year 2000," he said.
The newly antagonistic political climate has arrived only several years after Mr. Moynihan and others were celebrating what they called an era of new political consensus on welfare. Mr. Moynihan, the New York Democrat who will preside over Monday's hearing, has expressed the fear that the current attacks may undermine the program.
The program is called JOBS -- for Job Opportunities and Basic Skills -- and it was created as part of the 1988 Family Support Act. It requires every state to run an education, training or work program for parents on welfare, and provides up to $1 billion a year in Federal matching money to help pay for it.
When it passed, Senator Moynihan and others said the law amounted to a new social contract, one that emphasized the mutual obligations of Government and poor people. The law expands training opportunities for those on welfare but also imposes new obligations on them to participate. States are supposed to meet enrollment quotas and reduce the grants of those who refuse to join.
 
Some Successes
 The report, which examined 10 states including New York, was not entirely negative. It found that states were meeting their enrollment goals and that Government agencies that provided related services were learning to work together, a social policy goal often pursued but difficult to achieve.
As a result, it said, the program was offering "enhanced educational and training opportunities to welfare recipients."
The report looked only at the states' experiences in setting up the program and said it was too early to tell whether the programs were actually getting people off welfare.
Its biggest criticisms centered not on the letter of the law but on the states' approach toward its spirit. The program's architects hoped it "would signal a change in the mission of welfare systems or redefine the social contract," the report said, but that "has not been realized."
In none of the states studied, it found, had the program spurred leaders to "make a strong personal commitment to reform their welfare programs."
 
Implications for Public
 Richard Nathan, director of the Nelson A. Rockefeller Institute of Government at SUNY, which conducted the study, said the lack of fanfare had implications for the public: amid all the current talk about changing welfare, he said, many are unaware that large-scale changes are already supposed to be under way.
Mr. Nathan had hoped the program would serve to spur social workers to think more creatively about the range of a family's needs, and design services for children as well as their mothers.
But the report found that states had not used the new money "in any significant manner to provide new or expanded services to the children."
The report found that states, unable to come up with their portion of the matching funds, were only spending about 48 percent of the $1 billion in available Federal money. Figures from the Department of Health and Human Services, which is among the groups paying for the study, have since updated that number to about 60 percent.
 
Priority on Volunteers
 Mr. Moynihan said he was most disturbed by the finding that states, in meeting their enrollment goals, were giving priorities to mothers who volunteer. He said this practice could undermine the notion that the reluctant had an obligation to participate and raised the concern that those who most need the training may not get it.
"Most welfare administrators don't believe in telling people they have to do something or else," he said.
By 1995, every state will be required to enroll 20 percent of their welfare recipients in a work or training program, but about half the mothers are exempt because their children are too young.
Representative Thomas J. Downey, the Long Island Democrat who sponsored the law in the House of Representatives, blamed the Bush Administration for part of the program's problems.
Among the targets of his criticism is the so-called "20-hour rule," which requires states to design programs that involve at least 20 hours of activity each week. Many state officials have complained that this makes it hard to put welfare mothers in college classes, since they usually meet for just 10 or 12 hours.
Administration officials have said the 20-hour rule is necessary to insure that programs are long enough to be meaningful. Jo Anne B. Barnhart, the assistant secretary for health and human services who oversees welfare, could not be reached for comment on the report.
In addition to New York, the report examined Maryland, Michigan, Minnesota, Mississippi, Oklahoma, Oregon, Pennsylvania, Tennessee and Texas. It was co-written by Jan L. Hagen, also a professor at SUNY.
The fragility of the so-called welfare consensus can be seen in the experience of New York. Although the law was shaped in Washington by two New Yorkers -- Mr. Moynihan and Mr. Downey -- the State Legislature took two years to get its program up and running, as it bogged down in a contentious debate about the kind of training to offer and other issues.
Ms. Lurie said that while the program is now in operation, it "has not been sold to the public at all." She said New York put up enough matching funds to spend 47 percent of $87 million available in Federal money.
 
Link to Recession
 After showing little growth for almost two decades, the number of people receiving Aid to Families with Dependent Children, the official name for the welfare program for households with children, has skyrocketed. There are currently about 4.6 million families on welfare, or about 13.2 million people, an increase of 21 percent in little more than two years.
Experts have said the rise is being driven by the recession but also reflects a long-term increase in the percentage of families that are headed by single mothers. Many states have responded by cutting grants or tightening eligibility standards.
Nationwide, a mother with two children and no outside income received an average of $623 a month in A.F.D.C. and food stamps last year, according to the House Ways and Means Committee. That represents a two-decade decline of 27 percent in inflation-adjusted terms.
 
Attacks on the System
 In December, Gov. Pete Wilson of California, a Republican, gained national notice with a proposed ballot initiative that would cut grants by up to 25 percent. Vice President Dan Quayle traveled to New York City last month and said it showed the failure of a "liberal vision of a happy, productive and content welfare state."
President Bush -- previously silent on welfare issues -- used his State of the Union Message in January to attack the system and then began promising in televised campaign commercials to "make the able-bodied work." But he has offered no concrete proposals.
The actions by the White House drew an unusually sharp response from Mr. Moynihan, who said the President was playing "the race card." Mr. Moynihan has introduced a bill that would add $4.5 billion a year to the JOBS program, and enroll every eligible mother.
In a political attack of his own, he has called on President Bush to support the proposal, asking him to "put up or please to shut up."

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Candidate's Record;
Brown Firm on What He Believes, But What He Believes Often Shifts

BYLINE: By JEFFREY SCHMALZ

 BODY:
Listen to the voices of Edmund G. Brown Jr. over the years: Limits on campaign contributions are "a violation of free speech." National health insurance is out of the question, an invitation to "runaway inflation." Economic growth cannot be maintained forever; Americans must accept "an era of limits."
If those positions sound the opposite of what the former California Governor is pitching on the campaign trail these days, it is because they are. The Jerry Brown running for President in 1992 is at odds on crucial issues and campaign themes with the Jerry Brown who ran for President in 1976, the Jerry Brown who tried again in 1980 and even the Jerry Brown of just a year ago. 
 "You grow. You learn from mistakes. Positions evolve," the candidate said yesterday in an interview, pointing out that the country had changed greatly since he first ran for President at the age of 37.
Many political figures shift their stands over the years, of course, sometimes out of expediency, sometimes out of a genuine change in circumstances and convictions, sometimes because the responsibilities of governing overtake the promises of campaigning. But even Mr. Brown's supporters acknowledge that his shifts have come more often and been more fundamental than those of most other politicians.
Mr. Brown has become a champion of limiting campaign contributions to $100; it has been a hallmark of his campaign, as he says that to take more money is to be corrupted. But in years before, he sought much larger contributions, both for the Democratic Party in California, of which he was chairman, and for his own political action committee. A leading contributor to his PAC was Milan Panic, who ran a drug company of which Mr. Brown was a director; that relationship created a new controversy for the Brown campaign yesterday. [Page A14.]
In Michigan this month, Mr. Brown achieved second place in the Democratic primary largely because of his opposition to free trade with Mexico, which automobile workers say will cost them jobs. But as a Senate candidate in California in 1982, with the state's economy tight and Mexican immigrants pouring in, he called for close economic ties with Mexico and forming "a North American Common Market."
Mr. Brown now favors limits on the length of terms of elected officials, but in 1990 he opposed them. This year he supports gun controls, as he did in the 1976 Presidential campaign, but in the 1980 campaign, at a meeting of gun owners, he dismissed controls as the beginning of government restrictions on private lives. In 1979, Mr. Brown rejected national health care as too expensive, but this month, with health care one of the big issues in the campaign, he embraced the idea.
"You have to remember that Jerry Brown is a work in progress," said Bill Carrick, a Democratic poll taker in California. "He's a very shrewd politician, very shrewd. I wouldn't dismiss political motives for his changes in position. On the other hand, don't forget he started very young. He's constantly re-evaluating who he is, what he thinks about the issues. To some that's positive; to some it's negative."
In addition to the shifts on individual issues has come a shift in the main themes of Mr. Brown's campaigns. In 1976, he was in some ways playing the Paul Tsongas role, bearing a message of austerity. "We are entering an era of limits," he said. "And we all need to get used to it."
By 1980, having lost with that message, Mr. Brown's somber campaign theme had changed to "protect the earth, serve the people and explore the universe." After he wound up without any primary victories that year, he announced he was going to rethink his message and bring it closer to earth.
What emerged is this year's campaign: an appeal to the public anger at government and an attack on "a ruling elite of insiders who serve only themselves." The balanced-budget amendment that he advocated in 1980 has been replaced as the campaign's main economic theme by the flat-rate income tax. Exploration of the universe has turned into proposals to build high-speed trains.
"Jerry has no political or ideological anchor," said Mickey Kantor, who is running the Clinton campaign this year and had run Mr. Brown's 1976 campaign for President and 1982 race for Senator. "I'm saddened, frankly, by his kind of politics. I think he once had something to offer. Now he's a groping politician looking for the next thing he can exploit."
Mr. Brown sees it differently. "You experiment," he said in the interview. "You have to learn. You can't keep repeating."
 
Consistancy Cited
 Throughout it all, he argued, are underlying threads to which he has been consistent. "My writing and my speeches have had a real consistency. I've always been for trade unions; I've always been strong on the environment; I've always been strong on minorities."
But what happened, just to cite one example, to his fixation on space exploration? "You guys beat my brains out," he said, meaning that newspaper articles had ridiculed him for it. "But I still think it's important."
To be sure, Mr. Brown has been consistent on some of the same issues, like concern for the environment, opposition to nuclear power and cuts in military spending. And he has always run as the outsider, "David versus Goliath" he said just the other day, exactly what he said in 1980. The only difference is that for this performance Gov. Bill Clinton is substituting in the role of Goliath, played previously by Senator Edward M. Kennedy.
Still, the shifts in Mr. Brown's stands are there, and they come both fast and deep. It is sometimes possible to watch things shift -- or at least blur -- before one's very eyes.
Here is Mr. Brown early this month, saying America needs to help Americans: "I wouldn't give a penny in foreign aid until every small farmer, businessman and family are taken care of."
Does that mean he would withhold aid to Israel, he was asked a few days later, on March 8, on the NBC News program "Meet the Press."
"You know, that Israel thing is a hot-button issue," he replied.
Asked if he did not take accountability for what he originally said, Mr. Brown said: "I take accountability, precisely. Precisely. I wouldn't give one penny as long as the injustice is going on in this country. But I've also said that I believe this country is rich enough to be a leader in the world, that we can afford foreign aid."
Mr. Brown's shift on issues is most apparent on campaign contributions. Here he is on March 21: "I challenge Bill Clinton to limit his contributions to $100, as I have done. I challenge him to do away with the big money that is corrupting our Government, eating away at it. Government is being run for the big contributors, not for the people."
But in November 1989, Mr. Brown, then chairman of the Democratic Party in California, fought in Federal court against a state law that would have limited individual contributions to $1,000. Asked in court if the state should place any limits at all on campaign contributions, he responded: "When I had the chance to impose that, I decided that it would not be neither effective or good policy."
At another point Mr. Brown said, "I don't think large contributions per se have any different impact than contributions raised under these limits."
Question: "In the course of the days and weeks preceding the special election that occurred Tuesday, did you telephone and solicit any registered lobbyist in the State of California for contributions?"
Answer: Yes.
Q. How many?
A. As many as I could.
Mr. Brown's former law firm, which took on the case pro bono, has since applied for Federal money for its handling of the suit, which was brought as a civil-rights freedom-of-speech case. It was awarded $170,000 but no money has been paid, pending an appeal of the whole case. The lower court ruled in favor of Mr. Brown's opposition to the contribution limits.
Mr. Brown says that as party chairman he was obliged to fight the campaign limits, which would have treated the party as a political PAC and limited its donations to candidates to $2,500. In any case, he says, he has now seen the light because of his experience soliciting big contributions. "I have had my nose rubbed in the stench," he said. "I know about it first hand."
 
Stand on Proposition 13
 Another area where Mr. Brown seemed to shift was on Proposition 13, a voter-initiated measure that cut property taxes by 60 percent. As Governor, he strongly opposed it as "a consumer fraud, expensive, unworkable and crazy."
But once it passed overwhelmingly, he jumped on the bandwagon, cutting spending and talking about "a tax revolt that is being heard and has inspired the hopes of many."
Asked about that, Mr. Brown said yesterday that he had no choice as Governor but to enforce the new law. As for the enthusiasm of his enforcement, he indicated that perhaps he should have curbed it a bit. "But we had to make a move," he said.
One of the keys to Mr. Brown's doing well in Michigan and winning second place was his opposition to free trade with Mexico. "I opposed the fast track to free trade," he said to the delight of Detroit auto workers, to whom he portrayed Mexico as the enemy. "That means jobs that should be yours will end up in Mexico."
But in 1982, running for the United States Senate in a race he eventually lost, Mr. Brown said: "I would work in Washington to achieve closer economic cooperation with Mexico and Canada, whose economic destiny is linked with our own. I'd work for a unified economic policy for the Western Hemisphere to create the richest and most powerful trading region in the world."
Mr. Brown said yesterday that the statements were not inconsistent, that he simply opposed giving President Bush "a blank check" to open trade with Mexico.
On the issue of national health insurance, Mr. Brown changed stands recently. In 1979, at a Harvard meeting of doctors, he opposed it, saying: "If you have one individual who is dedicated to curing disease at all costs and another person understandably interested in alleviating his own suffering, and if you have a third party called Government that is willing to pay the cost, you have brought runaway inflation."
But this month, in Miami, he backed national health insurance, saying, "We can pay for it."
Even Mr. Brown's beloved flat tax is a reversal of an earlier stand. In an Op-Ed piece in The Los Angeles Times on Nov. 18, 1990, he spoke in favor of the progressive income tax. Asked about that yesterday, he said his flat tax was progressive, but admitted that was not what he was talking about in 1990. "If you are asking did I understand the implications of the flat tax then," he said, "the answer is no."

HEADLINE: A Final Clear-Cut, and Goodbye to Logging

BYLINE: By TIMOTHY EGAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: QUILCENE, Wash., March 23

 BODY:
Loggers don't say "timmmmmmber," when a 200-foot Douglas fir comes crashing down, severed by chain saw from its centuries-old anchor to the ground. They never did, as far as anyone on the Olympic Peninsula can remember.
Instead, after the pop and snap of wood fiber crumbling into the arms of gravity, Gary Phillips takes a deep breath. "Smell that?" says Mr. Phillips, a third-generation logger who is cutting his last stand of timber. "That's the smell of dying trees." 
 It wasn't so long ago that the Olympic Peninsula, home of the only temperate rain forest in the contiguous United States, called itself the logging capital of the world.
A big man and a chain saw could cut enough trees in a day to build two average-sized houses. Of course, those trees may have lived five centuries and harbored untold biological secrets, and therein lies the conflict. The public owns most of the big trees left in the country. More and more, they are valued for something other than building materials.
Because of environmental restrictions, concern about the shrinking habitat of endangered wildlife like the northern spotted owl, automation and over-cutting on some lands, the timber industry here is but a ghost of what it once was.
This spring, Mr. Phillips, who owns one of the last of the small timber operations on the peninsula, is closing his business. For him and his crew, this is the final clear-cut.
Increasingly, wood for new American homes comes from Canada, the South or the big Northwest tree farms owned by companies like Weyerhauser. Less than 15 percent of the nation's wood comes from the national forests, and some critics would like to see all logging halted on Federal lands.
 
Farmers, Not Villains
 These men do not see themselves as land rapists or villains. They say they are more like farmers, harvesting a crop. Nature, Mr. Phillips said, has downed more trees through fire and wind than any logger.
"A salmon fisherman once asked me if I felt bad, cutting down all these big trees," Mr. Phillips said. "I said: 'No, sir. Have you ever felt bad taking in a big salmon?' "
But these men concede that most of the really big trees are gone, and in many ways, the loggers still working in the last years of the 20th century are paying for the sins of others.
"I cut a tree once, 18 feet in diameter, but have not seen anything like that for many years," said Bob French, who has spent most of his 57 years in the woods.
They gather on this day in darkness, an hour before dawn, at a restaurant along Highway 101 called The Logger's Landing. Everyone wears spiked boots, and suspenders because belts don't hold the pants up just right. They consume enough eggs and bacon to shock a heart surgeon into palpitations. On the walls are pictures of the glory years, a dozen or more loggers standing proudly atop a single tree stump.
 
70 Acres of Timber
 Mr. Phillips, who has a bearskin rug over the couch at home, elk trophies on his walls and antique rifles displayed in a glass-topped coffee table, started H & P Logging with his partner, Pat Handly. They are contract loggers -- meaning they cut trees on contract for the purchaser of the timber. Their final timber harvest is a 70-acre piece of land owned by the state. Logging on Federal land on the peninsula has come to a virtual standstill because of restrictions to protect the northern spotted owl.
In all the years that Mr. Phillips's crew has been logging, not a man can remember seeing a spotted owl. Although a few dead owls have been found nailed to posts here in the Olympics -- a sign of the intense bitterness some men feel -- the loggers on Mr. Phillips's crew say they respect wildlife more than the average city dweller.
The Olympic Peninsula, a Massachusetts-sized shank of land in the far northwest corner of Washington State, can get heavy rains -- up to 180 inches a year -- on its western side, while the eastern side basks in sun. The interior of the peninsula is the Olympic National Park; it is bordered by the Olympic National Forest, two-thirds of which has been logged. The remaining land is private or owned by the state of Washington. Much of the private land has been severely cut, leaving vast stretches of stumps and brown earth.
Clear-cutting, considered the most economical way to cut trees in the thick forests of the Pacific Northwest, is the only way most of the men on the H & P crew have ever logged. In a typical clear-cut, the land is cleared of every tree, the logs gathered into neat piles, the brush burned and the ground replanted.
On this cut, the loggers start at the bottom of the slope and work their way up.
Deer, black bears, mice, coyotes, and numerous birds may live in this particular 70 acres, just above a saltwater inlet of Puget Sound. After the chain saws start up, the prime concern is wood. A single large tree can yield lumber worth as much as $30,000. When all the trees are felled from this section, they will provide enough wood to build about 230 houses. Much of this wood, after being cut into framing timber, will go to Japan.
Lee Olson uses a chain saw with a 36-inch blade to bring down the trees. He is massively built, with thick upper arms and bulging chest inside a Mickey Mouse T-shirt. It takes all of about five minutes to cut a century-old Douglas fir. The hardest part, he said, is keeping his footing while holding the saw steady.
Mr. Olson has scars all over his legs and arms from where the saw has bitten into his skin. Three of his uncles have been killed on logging jobs, and he has lost several good friends as well. On average, one of every three loggers is killed or seriously injured on the job during the length of a career. But even with the danger, Mr. Olson, who is 33 years old, said there was no job he would rather have than being a lumberjack.
 
Vanishing Vocation
 "I like the satisfaction of looking back at the end of the day and seeing what you've accomplished," Mr. Olson said. "Every tree is a different challenge; no two trees are the same." He can determine the way a tree is going to fall by cutting at an angle on the opposite side of the downslope. When he is done, he steps back and watches the crash.
Mr. Olson is something of an anachronism. The big companies that provide most of the nation's wood from their tree farms cut timber with a $300,000 machine that can pinch off a tree trunk like pruning shears on a plant. In the last 15 years, even before a single acre was set aside for the spotted owl, the Northwest lost 30,000 timber jobs, while gaining more than 800,000 jobs in other fields. Wood products, which once drove the economy, now accounts for only 2 percent of all jobs in western Washington.
And in the next 25 years, an additional 30,000 jobs are expected to be lost throughout the Northwest if proposals to set aside six million acres for the spotted owl are approved. Some economists have said that tourism will replace logging as the economic mainstay of timber communities. But many loggers say they will never become waiters or tour guides. Mr. Olson said he wanted to build houses. Mr. Phillips may work with plants. Mr. Handly is not sure what he will do. None of them seemed particularly bitter.
"The environmentalists have done their homework," Mr. Phillips said in a sort of back-handed compliment. "They got out ahead of us, and then before we knew it, it was basically over. We never knew what hit us."
As the jobs and towns built around logging have disappeared, some people have romanticized it, talking about a logger's culture of pickup trucks and brawny work. But the men on this final clear-cut scoff at such notions.
"What we do is all about one thing and one thing only -- money!" Mr. French said. Timber workers here make about $14 an hour.
When Mr. Olson has felled enough trees, he cuts them into sections, about 40-feet each, sized for fitting on a truck. Then a yarding crew comes along, hooking up the logs to massive steel cables connected to a hydraulic pulley. From there, they are loaded on trucks and sent to a mill in Tacoma.
 
'Just Plain Ugly'
 The forest floor, stripped to the bare earth in parts, covered with limbs and brush in others after the loggers have finished, looks like it has been hit by a hurricane.
"There's no way around it, a clear-cut is just plain ugly," Mr. Phillips said. But he sees life where others see death. "We've had lots of clear-cuts where I thought we did a great job," he said. "But then your average person comes along and says it's a catastrophe. People just don't understand what it is we do."
As part of what is known as the new forestry, not all the trees is this clear-cut were cut. A small section bordering a stream was left standing, as were several old trees used by birds. But the loggers complained that it caused more environmental damage to leave some standing trees, because the crew had to build twice as much road to get around the trees left near the stream. The road, gouged into the side of the hill, is already causing erosion and runoff of sediment.
"We're supposed to make a clear-cut that's not really a clear-cut," said Mr. Handly. "It's ridiculous."
Mr. Phillips's grandfather was a logger who came to the Northwest from Maine, via Wisconsin -- a fairly typical path for workers who followed the declining timber harvests from one coast to the other. Mr. Phillips's father was a logger as well, and he advised Gary not to get into it.
Although Mr. Phillips is not sure what he'll do when he hangs up his saw this spring, he is thinking about starting a nursery.
He does have another project in mind, a museum depicting the history of logging on the Olympic Peninsula. For artifacts, he said, he need go no farther than his own front yard, where he keeps the big tools of the tree-cutting trade.

HEADLINE: Budget Plan Will Raise Costs for Health Coverage, Private Insurers Say

BYLINE: By SARAH LYALL

 BODY:
Health insurance executives said yesterday that millions of New Yorkers would ultimately have to pay more for coverage from commercial carriers if the Legislature approved its leaders' budget pact.
At the same time, state officials said the budget agreement alone would not be enough to prevent a rate increase for 1.5 million customers of Empire Blue Cross and Blue Shield, the nonprofit insurer of last resort around New York City, Albany and the mid-Hudson Valley. 
 The tentative agreement would force the commercial insurance companies to pay hospitals more for customers' care, an increase the companies said they would have to pass along to their customers.
"This will cause premiums to increase signficantly," Philip J. Harrington Jr., vice president of government relations for the Prudential Health Insurance Company of America, said yesterday. "This is a tax that we would have to pass on to our policy holders."
But state officials dispute that assessment, saying that the commercial insurers are profitable enough to absorb the increase. They say the change will help make the Blue Cross and Blue Shield plans -- which would not be subject to the higher rates -- more competitive.
The state is considering a request by the ailing Empire Blue Cross and Blue Shield to raise rates by as much as 42 percent for individual subscribers and those who work in companies with fewer than 50 people.
The State Insurance Superintendent, Salvatore R. Curiale, has warned that unless the Legislature comes up with a comprehensive package of reforms for the industry, in addition to the hospital-fee increase, he will be forced to grant Empire's rate request. He has set April 1 as the deadline for his ruling.
While the increase in premiums under the higher rates would vary considerably depending on a subscriber's individual circumstances, some people could find themselves priced out of the market.
Daniel Margulis, who is 62 years old and lives in Whitestone, Queens, now pays $1,872 quarterly for an individual Empire policy for himself and his wife. If the rate increase is approved, his premium would rise to $2,470 quarterly, which Mr. Margulis said he simply could not afford.
Mr. Curiale has proposed a list of changes that would help bolster Empire and the other Blue Cross plans, which provide insurance for some 12 million New Yorkers but which are hemorrhaging money and subscribers. In the last few years, Empire -- which takes on everyone, no matter how old or sick -- has been increasingly challenged by commercial competitors.
This has meant disaster for Empire, which has raised rates four times in as many years, even as it lost some 400,000 customers. Its chairman, Albert A. Cardone, has said that without an increase, Empire will lose $372 million this year, making it insolvent by summer.
Mr. Curiale's proposals would force the commercial companies to accept any applicant who could afford to pay, as Empire does. And they would also change the way the commercial insurers set their rates for small groups and individuals.
Currently, the commercial insurers set rates depending on a subscriber's risk of illness, considering factors like age and whether or not the subscriber smokes. If the subscriber works for a small company, the other workers' risks are also considered in setting rates for the company. This keeps the rates down for healthy customers, while raising them for sicker customers, who are often forced into the Blue Cross plans.
Mr. Curiale, and the Assembly Democrats, want to force the commercial companies to spread the risk by considering all small-group subscribers as part of one pool, as the Blue Cross plans do.
But Senate Republicans have balked at those proposals, saying they would put an unfair burden on the commercial insurers. And now Mr. Curiale is saying that if his plan is not passed, not only will Empire's rates go up, but the whole system will begin to unravel.
It is unclear what will happen with the rest of the revisions Mr. Curiale is proposing. After yielding on the hospital-fee issue, the Senate Republican leadership may be in no mood to compromise on the other proposals.
 
Managing Competition
 Currently, all hospitals charge insurance companies the same base fee for the same procedure. The Blue Cross plans reimburse the hospitals at cost, and the commercial insurers pay an extra 13 percent. Under the agreement, the hospital fees for the commercial companies would increase to 24 percent.
The Blue Cross plans maintain, and the state agrees, that the different fees are necessary if Blue Cross is to compete with commercial carriers.
"This is an economic device that the state uses to help manage the competition between nonprofit carriers and commercial carriers," said Geoffrey Taylor, a spokesman for the New York State Conference of Blue Cross and Blue Shield Plans, which takes in Empire.
But the commercial insurers say the increased fees will just raise rates for their customers, without addressing the underlying problems.
"We feel sorry for anyone who feels that a tax on commercial insurance policy holders will save Blue Cross and solve the monumental problems facing health care in New York State," Mr. Harrington said. "This is like putting a Band-Aid on a cancer."
An odd twist in the agreement is that the extra money that would be raised by the fee increase -- about $130 million -- would go to the state's general fund to help offset $1 billion or so in cuts from the Medicaid program. This means that hospitals would pass the money from the increase along to the state.
"We haven't begun to estimate how much it will cost," said Kenneth E. Raske, president of the Greater New York Hospital Association, which represents 131 hospitals and nursing homes in New York City and nearby. "We'll be used to channel funds to the state, which is an activity we're not particularly familiar with."

HEADLINE: Levy Says He Is Resigning As Israel's Foreign Minister

BYLINE: By CLYDE HABERMAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: JERUSALEM, March 29

 BODY:
Foreign Minister David Levy, brooding for weeks about his faction's decline within the governing Likud Party and under pressure from angry supporters to do something about it, announced tonight that he was resigning.
Mr. Levy, the most openly enthusiastic supporter of the Arab-Israeli peace process in the hard-line Israeli Government, did not say he would also leave Likud, which he said he still loved and considered his home. 
 But even if he stays with the party, a decision that some political commentators said was far from assured, he has left it divided and perhaps seriously hobbled at the threshold of a campaign for national elections in June.
"I think the most satisfied person today is Yitzhak Rabin," a senior Government official said tonight. He was referring to the new leader of the opposition Labor Party, which recent opinion polls have given the edge against a Likud stumbling over an economic slowdown, difficulty in absorbing a tide of new immigrants and stagnation in the peace process.
It was not clear if Mr. Levy meant business about resigning as Foreign Minister, an announcement that came as a surprise in that his followers had been urging him to quit Likud, not necessarily the Government, and form his own party.
His resignation does not take effect until two days after he submits it to the Cabinet, and that is not scheduled to take place until it meets next Sunday. So there is a probable nine-day waiting period, and some Government officials and neutral commentators predicted that Mr. Levy would use it to wring concessions from his main enemy of the moment, Prime Minister Yitzhak Shamir.
But Aliza Goren, a Levy spokesman and longtime aide, said she thought the decision to quit was irreversible. Still, she said, "he keeps his cards very close to his chin, and he's the only one who knows what he'll do."
Mr. Levy, who also has the largely titular position of Deputy Prime Minister, has staked out a centrist position in the Shamir Cabinet, the most right-wing Government in Israeli history. Among ranking ministers, he has been not only the one most favorably disposed toward the peace process but also the most consistent exponent of restraint in the present tensions between Israel and the United States.
Before announcing his resignation at the end of a long speech to cheering followers gathered in Herzliya, near Tel Aviv, he rebuked Mr. Shamir and other Cabinet ministers for severely attacking Washington after the collapse of Israel's request for United States loan guarantees.
"Sometimes everyone is Foreign Minister," he said. "They are all experts. Everyone is declaring war on America. This certainly was a fundamental mistake."
It seemed unlikely, however, that Mr. Levy's departure would have a material effect on either the peace process or relations with the Bush Administration. Peace negotiations are expected to resume in late April after a hiatus of nearly two months. But many participants assume that nothing will happen and that the talks will be suspended until well after the Israel elections on June 23.
 
Rebuffed by Party
 In any event, the announcement tonight had less to do with public policy than with the moodiness that enveloped Mr. Levy and his Likud supporters after internal elections several weeks ago that shut them out of prominent spots in the party's lineup of parliamentary candidates.
Mr. Levy, who is 54 years old and was born in Morocco, is a proud man, the most successful Israeli politician among Sephardic Jews, who immigrated here in the 1950's from North Africa and elsewhere in the Middle East. But many feel that he is also sensitive to the point of touchy, especially about his origins, his meager formal education and the endless jokes told about him.
After his candidates fared poorly, the Foreign Minister accused Mr. Shamir of conspiring against him. Many of his followers saw an anti-Sephardic conspiracy by Ashkenzim -- those who, like Mr. Shamir, came here from Europe.
The dispute, some commentators caution, threatens to rekindle old rivalries between Ashkenazim and Sephardim that many Israelis thought they had put behind them long ago, especially in the Likud.
 
'Genie Out of the Bottle'
 "The genie is out of the bottle, and it's going to be a big one," said Akiva Eldar, a senior political writer for the newspaper Haaretz.
In Herzliya tonight, Mr. Levy invoked some of the Sephardic resentments in charging that he was the victim of discrimination.
"In the mouths of some I was a monkey that just came down from the trees," he said, blaming "the weak minds of little people" for his situation.
Nonetheless, he insisted that he still loved Likud, saying, "The party is my home, and a man cannot leave his home." But Ms. Goren, among others, said it was still possible that he would walk away and form his own party.

TYPE: Biography

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Vermont;
'Uncommitted' in Unpredictable State

BYLINE: By B. DRUMMOND AYRES Jr., Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BURLINGTON, Vt., March 29

 BODY:
This was once the nation's staid state taciturn, thrifty, rural, Yankee, Republican, a place where cows and sugar maples far outnumbered people and life was, above all else, predictable.
That was before the hordes of refugees from the sprawling, mauling urban combat zones of New York, Boston and Philadelphia discovered Vermont, bringing with them liberal, progressive ideas that have muddled much of what used to be a sure thing in the Green Mountain State. 
 Now hardly anything here is predictable. Especially politics.
So when Vermont Democrats caucus on Tuesday night to choose 14 delegates to the Presidential nominating convention in July, former Gov. Edmund G. Brown Jr. of California and Gov. Bill Clinton of Arkansas may find themselves in a surprising three-way contest instead of the simple one-on-one contest they had anticipated.
 
'Uncommitted' Running Strong
 In this, the year of the angry voter, "uncommitted" appears to be running strong in Vermont, at least in the final hours before the caucuses.
Mr. Brown and Mr. Clinton both hunger to go into next week's New York primary with a fresh victory, even one from a tiny state that seldom has played much of a role in Presidential nominations. What they may get, instead, is political muddle.
"The 'uncommitteds' are a big factor out there," said Jennifer Wallace-Brodeur, the executive director of the Vermont Democratic Party. "Neither Clinton nor Brown seems to be stirring much enthusiasm. This should be Brown's state, given he's so strong on the environment and women's issues and the like, but it's not inconceivable that Vermont could really confuse things and give 'uncommitted' its first 1992 win. I wouldn't bet on a thing."
Republicans will caucus, too, on Tuesday, and there a bet on President Bush is as safe as anything in politics. His conservative rival, Patrick J. Buchanan, has made no significant effort here, where 19 delegates are at stake.
There are no public opinion polls indicating who is out front here in the Democratic contest. But Clinton and Brown campaign workers have begun to warn the party faithful not to vote for "uncommitted."
Both campaign camps are driven, in part, by the results of a Democratic Party straw vote a few days ago in Norwich. "Uncommitted" captured 40 percent, with Mr. Brown getting slightly more than 30 percent and Mr. Clinton just more than 20 percent.
The nature of caucuses adds another element of unpredictability to Tuesday's results.
Caucuses are typically small neighborhood gatherings in which only a small, strongly committed percentage of voters turn out. A well-organized campaign can often pack caucuses with a candidate's supporters, thereby creating a voting result that does not actually mirror the preference of the electorate at large.
Mr. Brown appears to be better organized in Vermont than Mr. Clinton. But neither candidate has poured in major resources and both campaigns are relying mainly on the help of volunteers, who are working the phones and handing out pamphlets.
Mr. Clinton has not visited the state, though a solid finish here would help wipe away some of the tarnish his campaign picked up when he lost to Mr. Brown last week in the Connecticut primary.
Mr. Brown made his first appearance in the state this afternoon, appearing at a rally here and later at a rally at Montpelier, the capital.
"They can say all they want about me and my record," he told a gathering of about 2,000 people here. "But they can't stop the moral force of you, the people. We have to wake them up. Take our country back."
Mr. Brown called the rally "the biggest of my campaign" and questioned whether Mr. Clinton, troubled by questions about his personal and public behavior, could be elected should he win the nomination.
Before Mr. Brown's arrival here, campaign workers distributed leaflets that defended his proposal to set a flat tax of 13 percent on all earnings, a proposal that Mr. Clinton is criticizing at every opportunity.
Ken Dean, the director of Mr. Brown's Vermont campaign, said Mr. Brown hoped to win Tuesday's caucuses by picking up the support of voters who helped the Rev. Jesse Jackson win here in 1988.
Craig Fuller, a former Clinton aide in Arkansas who now lives in Vermont and heads the Governor's campaign effort here, is playing down expectations of success, perhaps as much out of frankness as political expediency.
"This is a tough state for Bill Clinton and, for that matter, for Jerry Brown and any other candidate," he said. "I confess, I don't detect much enthusiasm for anybody."

	--------------------	 	
 
Clinton Wins One Delegate
 CHRISTIANSTED, St. Croix, March 29 (AP) -- Mr. Clinton won one delegate Saturday and two went to "uncommitted" at the Virgin Islands' caucus.
The caucus coordinator, St. Claire Williams, said Mr. Clinton won 42 percent of the vote, earning him one of the three Virgin Islands delegates at stake. He said "uncommitted" got 58 percent and Mr. Brown got no votes.

HEADLINE: Bush Inches Toward Announcing How Much Help to Give to Russia

BYLINE: By ANDREW ROSENTHAL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
President Bush is expected to announce his much-debated program of aid for the former Soviet republics this week, perhaps as early as Tuesday, Administration officials said today.
The officials said that the program, which has become embroiled in the 1992 election campaign, was virtually finished but that White House advisers were debating the political merits of presenting it at a news conference or in a televised address. 
 Mr. Bush, who has come under bipartisan criticism from members of Congress and others, including former President Richard M. Nixon, for the delay in announcing the program, told reporters after attending church today that the package "will be good."
But he declined to discuss specifics. "I will have something to say on that this week," Mr. Bush said as he walked across Lafayette Park.
Mr. Bush's proposal has already been widely reported. It is expected to include an American contribution of about $1 billion to a fund to stabilize the ruble as it becomes a convertible currency, officials said. That fund is expected to total $5 billion or $6 billion from several countries.
Mr. Bush is also expected to propose about $1 billion in loan credits for Russia and about $200 million for Ukraine, with the money to be used to purchase farm goods from the United States.
The package is said to include an end to restrictions on lending that were imposed on Moscow during the cold war, and an increase of $12 billion in United States loan guarantees through the International Monetary Fund so the fund can make loans to the former Soviet republics.
Among details still being worked out this weekend was how to trim the Federal budget to come up with the $645 million in direct assistance to the republics that was pledged by Washington last month.
Some lawmakers have suggested taking the money from the Pentagon budget, which is being cut sharply. But Mr. Bush has refused to do that, saying he did not want to violate the 1990 budget agreement, which set limits on spending in individual areas and stipulated that any money cut from the military budget must be used to reduce the size of the deficit.
Administration officials were also debating how to present the package.
As Mr. Bush tries to bring the power of his incumbency to bear on his bid for re-election, some officials have argued that the President should make the announcement as "Presidential" as possible by doing it in a prime-time address from the Oval Office.
Once the the program is announced, a senior official said, Mr. Bush is likely to start an intensive lobbying campaign on Capitol Hill, both because of the need to show that he is seizing control of the issue for political reasons, and because of the complicated politics of finding the money.

 	 	 		--------------------	 	
 
The Hurdles That Remain
MOSCOW, March 29 (Special to The New York Times) -- While economic reform in Russia is showing "some good results," a great deal remains to be done, a senior American official said here today.
The official, David C. Mulford, Undersecretary of the Treasury, said that while he expected Russia to be able to join the International Monetary Fund by early May, the current reforms were not as comprehensive as the fund would require before agreeing to help.
"On the budget deficit, they have a lot more to do, and they know it," Mr. Mulford said to reporters after meeting the Deputy Prime Minister in charge of the reform, Yegor T. Gaidar. "They have not necessarily reached the goals they originally set. I think the whole process has gone a bit slower and has been more difficult than they first imagined, but that is not unusual."
Mr. Gaidar has already relaxed monetary and fiscal policy to try to keep inefficient industry going during this difficult transition, when more than 40 percent of the country's industrial capacity is idle and output is continuing to decline by about 10 percent a month. Tax revenues are consequently below expectations, and tax collection is still haphazard.
The Russian Government is expected to face considerable criticism over its economic policies, which have raised prices for most commodities, when the Congress of People's Deputies convenes on April 6. Apparently concerned about the political reaction, President Boris N. Yeltsin said last week that he had decided to postpone freeing oil and gasoline prices from April until May or June.

HEADLINE: U.S. Reaping Zairians' Anger Toward Mobutu

BYLINE: By KENNETH B. NOBLE

DATELINE: KINSHASA, Zaire

 BODY:
Ever since Mobutu Sese Seko came to power nearly 30 years ago, Zaire has been regarded as one of Washington's best friends in Africa. But now, with the country facing economic ruin and increasing violence, there has been a marked surge in hostility toward Washington for its longtime support of President Mobutu's autocratic rule.
Students and intellectuals have long denounced the United States as a repressive force in Zairian politics, accusing Washington of pressing a cold-war ideology that has helped prop up the Mobutu dictatorship. But amid the growing political turmoil, even Zairians who reject such views are taking a more critical view of the United States. 
 "There's no doubt that many people here blame the United States for keeping Mobutu in power for so long," said Etienne Tshisekedi, leader of Zaire's biggest opposition party, who served briefly as Prime Minister in October. "The anti-American sentiment has recently become a lot worse because many people can't understand why the U.S. is still supporting his regime."
Zairians say they believe that the United States has the power to remove the President, but is unwilling to.
President Mobutu has received hundreds of millions of dollars from the United States since he came to power in 1965. Over time, public and private American voices were raised against that aid.
Last November, allegations of a massacre of university students in a major southern city, Lubumbashi, and persistent reports that he amassed a fortune prompted Congress to end all military and economic help.
Nonetheless, the United States is perceived as President Mobutu's Western backer, which has made life uncomfortable for Americans living in this Central African country. This hostility is directed not just at Americans but at Zairians who are friendly with Americans.
A Zairian who studied at Stanford University said, "Even people who have been educated in the U.S. like me have been told things like we're brainwashed, or probably C.I.A. agents, or on the C.I.A.'s payroll."
A poll taken by the Zaire Institute of Public Opinion in February found that 73.3 percent of those asked had a poor opinion of American policy toward this country. In contrast, 78.9 percent said they had a good opinion of Belgium, the former colonial ruler.
American officials said they were hard pressed to explain this, since in recent months, at least publicly, the United States and Belgium have taken virtually the same positions toward the Zairian leader, criticizing him for being unwilling to grant political pluralism he promised in 1990.
"The difference," said a European diplomat, "is that Belgians have been wrestling with Mobutu for a much longer time, and most people here believe that they truly want to get rid of him. The Americans, on other hand, aren't seen as credible; they're afraid to say publicly what they privately believe, that Mobutu must go."
This view has come at a time when the American presence has nearly vanished. Since the State Department warned of danger in Zaire in September, thousands of Americans have left. The few who remain -- fewer than 100 by some estimates -- say they are targets of strident criticism. Many fear that the tension could easily turn violent.
"It is not the virulence of the stories appearing in the local press that concern me," said Melissa Wells, the United States Ambassador, "but it is the comments of longtime American residents, including many missionaries, who say they have never known this extent of anti-American sentiment. And I hear such comments day after day after day."
The anger increased a few weeks ago when Herman J. Cohen, Assistant Secretary of State for African Affairs, met briefly with President Mobutu. Mr. Cohen declined to discuss the meeting, and his silence seems to have released a torrent of pent-up anger and fed conspiracy theories involving the United States.
Mr. Cohen, though critical of President Mobutu, has said he should stay in office during a transitional period leading to multi-party elections.

HEADLINE: Washington at Work;
Cloak and Dagger Retired, Ex-Chief Of C.I.A. Remains Hard to Predict

BYLINE: By ELAINE SCIOLINO, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
Which is the real William Egan Colby, "the traditional grey man, so inconspicuous that he can never catch the waiter's eye in a restaurant," as the former spymaster described himself in his 1978 memoir, "Honorable Men: My Life in the C.I.A.", or the romantic who drives a red Fiat Pininfarina around Washington as if he were in vehicular combat in Rome?
Is he the onetime secret agent who for years assiduously cultivated a manner as colorless as his translucent eyeglasses, or the free spirit who tangoes with his wife in the living room of their Georgetown town house? 
 Mr. Colby, once an unrepentant cold warrior branded a war criminal on antiwar posters in the Vietnam era, is now promoting a 50 percent cut in the military budget. Once the mastermind of the Central Intelligence Agency's clandestine operations in Indochina in the 1960's, he is now advising the heads of Eastern European intelligence services how to make their agencies compatible with democracy.
Is the 72-year-old former Director of Central Intelligence a born-again liberal atoning for past misdeeds? Is he, as his wife, Sally Shelton, insists, misunderstood -- a lifelong populist who is driven to tears by the plight of the world when he watches the nightly news, so "low key," she says, "that he sometimes lets people walk all over him?"
 
A Covert Career
 Mr. Colby himself, now a practicing lawyer and a consultant to a handful of multinational corporations who is earning considerably more than he did at the C.I.A., dismisses any seeming contradictions in his history. "The career explains the whole thing," he said in a recent interview. "For years I was a covert operator, and in the intelligence business you're not supposed to be terribly open."
Though Mr. Colby had been speaking out on disarmament for years, it was the overlit, 30-second television commercial that began in Washington in January that portrayed the image of a man whose world has turned upside down.
"I'm William Colby, and I was head of the C.I.A.," he says in the commercial. "The job of intelligence is to warn us of dangers to our national security. During the cold war, those dangers were military. The cold war is over, and the military threat is now far less. It's time to cut our military budget by 50 percent and to invest that money in our schools, our health care and our economy."
Since then, Mr. Colby has expanded on his proposal before Congress, traveled around the country giving speeches and drafted a long paper with Paul C. Warnke, the former director of the Arms Control and Disarmament Agency for the Coalition for Democratic Values, the organization that sponsored the ads.
Their plan would halve the military budget in five years, freeing $345 billion to help the country's sagging economy. This is considerably more than President Bush's plan to pare $50 billion from the military budget, a plan that Mr. Colby says "reflects a desire to continue business as usual."
Mr. Colby's is not, of course, a universe of one. Ray S. Cline, for example, who as Deputy Director of Central Intelligence first informed the Kennedy White House of the Cuban missile crisis in 1962, stood beard-to-beard with Fidel Castro at a conference in Havana last January, joking about the C.I.A.'s unsuccessful efforts to assassinate him.
But Mr. Colby said he has been waiting in the wings for the new world for more than a decade. Indeed, as long ago as 1975, his friends say, he was made an outcast by the Old Guard in the intelligence agencies for violating the unspoken rule of C.I.A. secrecy, telling Congress about "the family jewels" -- the nickname for the agency's illegal activities and dark secrets, from spying on American citizens in the United States to operating a project to develop supersecret weapons like poisons, biological weapons and dart guns.
Mr. Colby said his candor to journalists, and in interminable Senate hearings on the excesses of C.I.A. covert operations, cost him his job but gained him the respect of Congress and helped establish the process of Congressional supervision over intelligence.
Shortly after he left the agency Mr. Colby began to speak out on military affairs, quickly becoming the darling of the disarmament set.
 
Panel Discussion Recalled
 "It was amazing," recalls Jeremy Stone, the president of the American Federation of Scientists, who says he discovered the soft side of Mr. Colby at a panel discussion on disarmament 15 years ago. "I thought I'd be the only dove among the hawks, but I found myself saying that Colby had said everything I believed in on disarmament and more eloquently than I did. Then, to my surprise, I started finding Colby on panels everywhere -- traveling at his own expense."
In 1983 the man who had years before acknowledged to Congress that the C.I.A. had regularly used overseas missionaries to gather intelligence, endeared himself to the Roman Catholic bishops when he joined their cause, calling himself an "unabashed supporter of the nuclear freeze."
Mr. Colby's critics say that he was always a bit of a misfit as a C.I.A. bureaucrat, that his cooperation with the press and the Congress made "the process of demolition" of the C.I.A. "irreversible," as Archie Roosevelt, the veteran C.I.A. official, said in his memoirs.
Those close to him say he is nothing if not a pragmatist.
In an essay written in 1986, for example, Mr. Colby in effect called for a military coup in Iran, arguing that the United States should "actively encourage the appearance" of a new Shah, "preferably out of the army rather than the clergy."
 
A Sense of What's Practical
 "I don't think he has an ideological bone in his body," says his wife, Ms. Shelton, a former ambassador in the Carter Administration who runs an independent consulting firm for multinational corporations. "He just wants to know what's practical, what's fair and what makes sense."
Mr. Colby says that he still believes in old-fashioned covert action, but only when it works. "If you can stir up a little antagonism between rival drug lords, if you can support covertly some more responsible people than some wild anti-American groups, if you could give some assurances privately to a few people in Cuba, North Korea, Burma that if they change their government and orientation, yes, the United States would be responsive, then why not?" he asks.
When asked why he does not favor assassination plots, he does not plunge into Jesuitical arguments about morality, but says simply, "If they're exposed, it's usually counterproductive."
In the case of Panama, he said, an invasion that "killed several hundred Panamanians and in which we lost 40 of our own" was better than a covert plot to eliminate Gen. Manuel Antonio Noriega.
"If he had been assassinated you'd still be hearing about it," Mr. Colby said. "But military action fit so naturally into America's longtime relationship with Panama, it causes no great stir."
 
Praise and Criticism
 As for his current stand on military spending, some independent experts praise the effort but criticize its lack of specificity. "What's missing on paper is a sense of roles and missions," says Gordon Adams, director of the Defense Budget Project, a nonpartisan research group that works on military-spending issues. "If I were a college professor I'd give him a C-plus, or better, maybe an incomplete. But it's not off the wall. No way."
Mr. Colby says he does not understand all the fuss about his position on defense. A member of the American Civil Liberties Union before he joined the C.I.A., he spent a summer organizing service-station operators when he was an undergraduate at Princeton, parachuted behind enemy lines into France and Norway in World War II, campaigned door-to-door for President Harry S. Truman in 1948 and joined the C.I.A. because, he wrote in his memoir "Honorable Men" (Simon & Schuster) , it was "the most effective place for a non-Communist liberal to do battle against the Communist menace."
In "Lost Victory," his book on Vietnam (Contemporary Books, 1989), he argues that one of the main reasons the war was lost is that the C.I.A. had to turn it over to the American military.
He also refuses to distance himself from the C.I.A.'s Phoenix program, a secret operation that he supervised and that was designed to "neutralize" the Vietcong in South Vietnam. More than 20,000 "suspects" were killed, and although he vehemently denies charges that it was a program of systematic assassination and torture, he acknowledged to a House committee that there had been some "excesses."
But Vietnam was "a liberal action," Mr. Colby says, adding, "I think we had the war won in 1972."
Daniel Ellsberg, the Defense Department analyst in the 1960's who became a prominent opponent of the war, still insists that the Phoenix program was wrong. But he adds, "I admire Colby for being so open, and I wish there were more like him."

HEADLINE: An On-Time Budget;
An Early Start and Angry Voters Help Cure Albany's Tardy Ways

BYLINE: By KEVIN SACK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ALBANY, March 29

 BODY:
In trying to find a silver lining in the cumulus of New York's budget crisis, state leaders consoled themselves with the notion that hard times would allow them to change government in fundamental ways that were politically unfeasible in brighter days.
The tentative budget deal reached on Saturday night by Gov. Mario M. Cuomo and the state's legislative leaders showed the premise to be true on one important count but false on others. 
 It was the hard political times -- marked by a deep sentiment against incumbents at all levels of government -- that provided the imperative to produce an on-time budget for the first year since 1984. If the agreement reached by Mr. Cuomo and the legislative leaders wins approval in tonight's party caucuses in both the Senate and Assembly, it is expected that budget bills can be passed by Wednesday, the start of the fiscal year.
 
Hard Economic Times
 But the hard economic times, which forced the state to close a significant budget gap for the third consecutive year, did not provide the imperative to revise government in the way that Mr. Cuomo and some lawmakers had envisioned.
With the exception of the major savings squeezed from the Medicaid program, much of which came from increased taxes on hospitals, nursing homes and private insurance companies, there is apparently little in the budget that qualifies as fundamental reform.
As in past years, the Legislature did not agree to create more prison space by loosening sentencing laws, to eliminate inequities in the formula that determines state aid to schools or to relieve local governments of hundreds of costly state regulations.
"The biggest disappointment is that the Legislature will not restructure across the board as I asked them to," Mr. Cuomo said last week, as budget negotiations were entering their final phase. "They will do only one thing -- poor people -- and call it restructuring."
In the context of state politics, passing a budget by April 1 is no small achievement. For the last seven years, the budget deadline has been imaginary, often treated with little respect even at the beginning of each year's budget talks.
State officials did not even heed the warning from Wall Street in 1990, when the state's credit rating was lowered at least partly because that year's deadline was missed.
Although voters expressed exasperation about the succession of late budgets, the majorities in both houses -- the Democrats in the Assembly and the Republicans in the Senate -- expanded in that November's election.
The next year, the state shattered its previous record for tardiness by approving a budget that was nine weeks late.
But this year, the politicians in Albany could sense that the relentless recession in the Northeast had given rise to a growing anger.
In that environment, legislators saw no reason to once again hand voters a tangible symbol of their impotence, particularly in a reapportionment year when they would be introducing themselves to strangers in newly drawn districts.
"This is really a first step back to re-establishing our credibility, both with the voters and the raters," said Assembly Speaker Saul Weprin, a Queens Democrat.
 
Negotiations Began Early
 There were other factors that prodded the process as well. Above all, budget negotiations got started early. After discovering in November that New York had a gap of nearly $900 million in this year's budget, state leaders spent two months of trying to produce an agreement on how to close that gap or on the possibility of forging a 15-month budget that would close the combined $4.8 billion gap for this year and next year.
While those talks failed, they produced a great deal of progress toward this year's deal. Essentially, the leaders spent the same amount of time negotiating the 1992-93 budget as they did in the last two years. But this year, they started earlier, and they had the unpleasant memory of last spring to guide them.
"One of the things that was different about this year was last year," Mr. Cuomo said today.
In addition, the Assembly had a new leader, Mr. Weprin, who joined Mr. Cuomo in placing a high premium on timeliness. Some Assembly members have said they believed this was how Mr. Weprin hoped to make his mark.
Privately, some New York City officials have faulted Mr. Weprin for being so eager to be on time that he did not mount a strong defense of the city's interests. But Mr. Weprin said today that the city would have been worse off if the budget was late because he would have lost negotiating leverage with the Senate, particularly on Medicaid and welfare.
Ralph J. Marino, the leader of the Republican-controlled State Senate, said he agreed to several last-minute compromises, including a measure that would increase the rates paid by insurance companies to hospitals, because he had won deep cuts in Medicaid and had staved off cuts in school aid.
"I think basically we got what we strove for," he said. "This is why we could agree on a timely budget."

HEADLINE: METRO DIGEST

 BODY:

NEW YORK CITY
 
CRASH MAY FORCE NEW ICE RULES
If the National Transportation Safety Board determines that ice on the wing caused the crash of USAir Flight 405 last week, it could prompt fundamental changes in how aircraft are inspected before takeoff, how they are de-iced and even how they are dispatched to the runway. Despite the potential danger of even thin sheets of ice, there is no universal rule on how it should be detected and removed. B3.
 
FLIGHTS TO LOCAL AIRPORTS IN DECLINE
The total number of travelers who passed through the region's three main airports declined sharply last year, a decrease caused by the recession and the economic effects of the Persian Gulf war, according to the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey. The overall number of passengers who used the three airports -- Kennedy International, La Guardia and Newark International -- fell 8.7 percent, from 74.8 million in 1990 to 68.3 million last year. B5.
 
FIRE RIPS THROUGH TRANSIENT HOTEL 
 Nineteen people were injured, 13 of them firefighters, and more than 150 people left homeless in a five-alarm fire at a transient hotel in midtown Manhattan, fire officials said. The firefighters sustained burns and smoke inhalation while fighting the two-hour fire at the Hotel Mansfield Hall, near the theater district. B3
 
GIRL KILLED IN BROOKLYN BLAZE
A 2-year-old girl was killed when fire engulfed her bed in a Brooklyn home. The cause of the blaze is under investigation, but neighbors said the victim's sister was playing with a cigarette lighter and accidentally started the fire. B3.
 
GOTTI TRIAL NEARS DELIBERATIONS
With final summations to end today, the racketeering-murder trial of John Gotti is entering its last phase in Federal District Court in Brooklyn. The closely guarded jury will probably begin deliberations tomorrow. B2.

A wind-swept fire roared through hundreds of acres of open marshland near the Meadowlands Sports Complex in Carlstadt, N.J., backing up traffic 15 miles on the northern stretches of the turnpike. Billowing black smoke and flaming 8-foot marsh reeds on both sides of the turnpike caused the authorities to close the turnpike's 10-mile western spur for about five hours. B5.

The nation's map fraternity is astir over the fate of General Drafting Company, one of the nation's premier mapmakers, now housed in an ersatz 16th-century mansion in Morris County, N.J. The company was recently sold, and its new owners say that the maps -- though readable, exact and beautiful -- simply do not turn enough profit. Convent Station Journal. B5.

Voters do not dispute whether development should take place. The questions are whether the development proposed is the right kind, for the right reasons. B5.

HEADLINE: From Trenton to Montauk, Local Officials Are Buying Local

BYLINE: By THOMAS J. LUECK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MINEOLA, L.I., March 27

 BODY:
In a mini-trade war, New York City, New Jersey and Long Island are engaged in a bitter exchange of purchasing policies that favor local suppliers for government contracts.
The policies are intended to protect jobs and buttress local economies, all mired in recession. They give preference to resident businesses, even if their bids are as much as 10 percent higher than competing proposals from other, nonresident companies. 
 But as they spread from Trenton to Montauk, the incentives, known as "local preferences," are encountering a groundswell of skepticism. The critics say they waste tax money and squash competition, and are fraught with many of the same conflicts that make trade protectionism a recurrent theme of global debate.
"Any advantage to a specific group of contractors, even if it sounds good politically, leads to higher prices," said Carla S. Lallatin, a financial consultant in Brooklyn and a member of New York City's Procurement Policy Board, created under the 1988 revision of the City Charter to oversee its purchasing procedures. The board wants all local preferences eliminated.
 
'Not a Foreign Power'
 So do many business people.
"It is amazing to me that with all the financial problems around us, they would not go with the low bidder," said Conrad Noble, president of the White Plains Electrical Supply Company, whose $173,200 bid to supply light bulbs to New York City was rejected last year. The contract went to a supplier in the city that bid $5,700 more.
"It's not as though I don't spend my own money in New York City," Mr. Noble said. "I am not a foreign power."
New York City started the battle in 1984 when it began favoring local companies in contracts for products ranging from police cars to software. City companies can win city business as long as their bids are no more than 5 percent higher than competing bids from outsiders.
The favoritism has always rankled business executives outside the city, but the first counterattack did not come until October, when New Jersey's Division of Purchase and Property adopted what it called a "Golden Rule."
The New Jersey policy retaliates against 18 states and 7 cities across the nation that impose various forms of local preference, but is aimed most squarely at New York City. Under the rule, all out-of-state bidders for New Jersey business must contend with the same penalties faced by New Jersey companies that compete for contracts in their states or cities.
Now, in what it calls a plan to create jobs, Nassau County is proposing a preferential policy of its own, which would allow local bidders to charge up to 10 percent more than companies from outside the area. The preference would be extended to companies in New York City and Suffolk County, as well as in Nassau.
Unlike New York City's law, the Nassau law would cover those providing services as well as products.
"Local tax dollars should be spent locally," said Thomas S. Gulotta, the Nassau County Executive and author of the proposal, which is scheduled for a hearing by the county's Board of Supervisors on April 6.
Over the last three years, officials here said, $109 million in county construction contracts and 25 percent of the purchase orders for Nassau have been awarded to companies outside Long Island and New York City.
 
'Time to Protect Our Own'
 "It has always galled our members when local funds are spent on businesses based outside the region," said Anthony Russo, business manager for Local 138 of the Engineers Union in Nassau County, which supports Mr. Gulotta's plan. "It's time to protect out own."
But while support for such policies appears to be growing, some business people say the preferences undermine competition and waste taxes.
And the laws have not escaped the notice of officials in Washington, and even abroad. American negotiators, at work in Uruguay on revisions of the international treaty governing trade among more than 100 nations, have asked all states to forgo preferential policies that would handicap foreign bidders.
"The Europeans in particular are aware of, and are very much opposed to, the so-called 'buy local' initiatives," said Beverly Fuortes, an adviser to Carla Hill, the United States Trade Representative.
And while the movement for local preferences seems particularly strong in the New York metropolitan region, other cities and states have also been enacting or considering such rules.
"There has been a frenzy since November," said Les Jackson, director of research of the National Institute of Governmental Purchasing, a nonprofit group in Falls Church, Va. He said a dozen cities and states are now considering policies either to initiate local preferences or to retaliate against other areas.
The new interest in local preferences comes as cities and states have adopted a host of other business incentives, many of them for minority-owned companies. A more recent surge involves "Buy American" programs in which goods made anywhere in this country are favored over those produced abroad.
But Mr. Jackson's group, whose members include government procurement officials from across the United States and Canada, said the recent flurry of interest in local initiatives may be more deeply rooted in the recession than the other programs, as cities and states respond to calls for help from their financially troubled business constituents.
 
How Much Competition
 The group opposes the local preferences, saying the officials who advocate them often overlook the precision with which suppliers can estimate how much others will bid for contracts. In many cases, it contends, a local company that is given a 5 percent or 10 percent price advantage in bidding will automotically increase its bid by just enough to be assured of winning the work.
Still, some proponents of local preferences say they leave ample room for competition. In Nassau, Mr. Gulotta and other officials said they wanted to offer their incentives to New York City and Suffolk County because many residents here work outside the county, and because a bigger pot of potential bidders will keep costs low.
The impact of New York City's seven-year experience with local initiatives is being studied by the Procurement Policy Review Board. Although the board has repeatedly criticized the incentives, it does not have the authority to eliminate them.
In its most recent report, covering city purchases for 12 months ending in September 1991, the board found that city procurement officials had rejected nonresident suppliers in 93 contracts, in each case awarding the work to city businesses that submitted higher bids.
 
Costing the City More
 It said the city spent $199,400 more for the contracts from its hometown businesses than if it had accepted the competing out-of-town bids. More than half of the rejected low bids came from elsewhere in New York State, and 20 percent came from companies in New Jersey, it said.
In previous years the preference policies cost the city even more, according to the board's records. But because the city's policy is discretionary, it said, the Dinkins administration has more frequently chosen to use out-of-town bidders to cut costs in the face of the city's budget crunch.
Amid the broadening array of local preferences, some business people say their cities and states have no choice but economic retaliation.
"What is good for the goose is good for the gander," said Donald Warnock, owner of Warnock Chrysler Dodge in Hanover, N.J., who in 1989 was rejected on a $21.8 million bid to supply police cars to New York City. The contract was awarded to a dealer in the city that bid $1 million more.
Mr. Warnock was a leader in the lobbying effort for New Jersey's new preference rule. But he said he would prefer that none of the local preferences existed anywhere. "The people that pay in the end are the taxpayers," he said. "What we'd prefer is a level playing field."
