HEADLINE: Opposition Develops to Proposal For Building-Permit Extensions

BYLINE: By IVER PETERSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TRENTON, March 29

 BODY:
A move by New Jersey developers to gain blanket extensions of building permits for projects that were delayed by the recession has run into opposition from environmentalists and municipalities in the state.
At hearings of the Assembly Community Affairs committee on Monday, representatives of the New Jersey League of Municipalities are planning to argue that the automatic extensions being sought by the builders usurp local powers and reward only a small group. 
 Environmentalists also oppose the proposed legislation, which would extend any construction or development permit that expired after Jan. 1, 1989, through 1994. Many developers had stopped or slowed down the construction of projects for which they had obtained the required dozen or more state and municipal permits. Many of those permits, which are typically good for three years, are expiring.
Granting automatic extensions for projects that have already passed municipal and environmental muster would put thousands of unemployed construction workers back to work, said Patrick O'Keefe, director of the New Jersey Builders Association. Requiring developers to go back to the authorities for extensions or renewals, he said, would add to delays and costs that could kill the projects.
 
'Dead in the Water'
 "We're talking about projects that have already demonstrated to the satisfaction of everyone that they are appropriate," Mr. O'Keefe said in an interview last week. "But because of the economic slowdown, they have been built out at a pace slower than originally anticipated. Because of the credit crunch and the expenses involved in reapplying for the necessary permits, there is little likelihood that they will ever be resurrected, and they will be dead in the water."
That would only delay a revival of the state's stagnant construction industry, he said. The industry has lost approximately 50,000 jobs in the state. "We're better off just extending these permits and get the projects moving, put people back to work and get on with the business of the day," Mr. O'Keefe said.
Mr. O'Keefe said he did not know how many such permits have expired or are about to do so. The measure has been heard in the Senate Community Affairs committee and may be voted on in that chamber in mid-April, he said.
But while the builders say the extensions would promote jobs and economic development, the municipalities are gathering forces to head off what they see as an attempt to circumvent local land-use prerogatives.
William G. Dressel Jr., Assistant Executive Director of the New Jersey State League of Municipalities, called the proposal "misdirected" in a letter to legislators and league members. "It is intended to benefit a small segment of the state, namely, land developers, at the expense of prohibiting governmental officials from protecting the citizens."
 
State Land-Use Plan Coming
 Many of New Jersey's counties, townships and boroughs are reviewing their land-use plans in anticipation of a state land-use plan that is scheduled to be published this summer by the State Planning Commission. Local officials, as well as some environmentalists, maintain that the builders are trying to preserve the viability of their plans within a fluid planning environment.
"Local plans are not standing still," said Steve Howard, a lobbyist with the New Jersey Conservation Fund. The municipalities, he said, "can be revising their master plans or changing their zoning, and all of a sudden they are overridden by the Legislature? That would be irksome in the extreme to the people in a municipality."

HEADLINE: Big Gains for European Stocks, but Not in Dollars

BYLINE: By SETH FAISON Jr.

 BODY:
Hefty gains in European stock markets in the first quarter were virtually wiped out, for American investors, by a corresponding rally in the dollar.
Since Jan. 1, several markets in Europe have outpaced the United States market and appeared to offer global investors an attractive alternative to domestic stocks. But an investor happily calculating impressive earnings in a foreign denomination saw them evaporate when converted back into dollars. 
 German and French markets advanced 8.82 and 9.35 percent, for example, while American markets dropped 2.95 percent, according to the Financial Times index of world markets. But in dollar amounts, those gains were watered down to only 0.42 percent for Germany and 1.61 percent for France.
Markets in Sweden and Austria saw nice advances of 12.52 percent and 13.45 percent, but currency differentials reduced the gains to 4.42 percent for Sweden and 3.86 percent for Austria.
Analysts expected the dollar to rise slightly this year, but anticipation of a recovery in the American economy has pushed it higher than many predicted. The dollar closed at 1.64 German marks on Friday, up from 1.52 on Jan. 1.
An essentially flat market in Britain, down 1.22 percent, became an 8.22 percent loss once the dollar differential was factored in. Britain's market reached an all-time high in the fall but has since been listless.
Over all, Mexico's bourse again led the world's markets in the quarter, with now-predictable gains. Capping an advance inspired by an economic policy that has lasted four years, it rose 28.42 percent in the local currency, and 26.42 percent in dollars.
In Asia, Hong Kong's stocks surged nearly as sharply as Japan's fell. The Hong Kong market advanced 17.58 percent, and with Hong Kong dollars closely pegged to the American currency, stock gains are protected from currency fluctuations.
The benchmark Hang Seng index closed Friday at 5,007.58, and analysts predict another spurt of growth in the coming months. Known for its sensitivity to political events in Beijing, Hong Kong's market has surged in recent weeks after a wave of political clues indicating that the economic reformers affiliated with Deng Xiaoping, the senior Chinese leader, had gained the upper hand in a power struggle with conservatives.
 
Hong Kong Hopeful About China
 Mr. Deng has supported nascent securities markets in Shanghai and Shenzhen, although it will probably be at least a few years before foreigners can own more than a nominal amount of stock. But progress in China is opportunity in Hong Kong, and the market reflects fresh confidence.
"We're partly playing catchup," Howard Gorges, managing director of South China Brokerage Ltd., said from Hong Kong. "The market here was suppressed in 1989 because of the Tiananmen crackdown, and last year because of the delay in getting approval for the airport project. Now it's very strong, and there are some real opportunities."
Mr. Gorges said good fundamentals and better-than-expected 1991 earnings had also fueled the boom, which he expects to continue for the rest of the year.
Elsewhere in Asia, the Malaysian market grew 6.97 percent in local currency. But in a rare instance in which the local currency rose more than the dollar, the growth in stocks was worth 12.47 percent in dollars.
Singapore's market, on the other hand, fell by 4.67 percent in the local currency, and by 7.25 percent when translated into dollars.
At the very bottom of the Financial Times listing of 24 markets was Japan, with a decline of 17.07 percent, measured in yen, and a 22.17 percent drop in dollars.

HEADLINE: Budget Office Retreats on Work Health

BYLINE: By ADAM CLYMER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
Under pressure from Congress, the Administration's budget office retreated last week from its demand that new occupational-health regulations be delayed to see if their cost would impoverish workers, thereby making them sicker rather than healthier.
Instead of sending the regulations back to the Labor Department for an analysis of that issue, the Office of Management and Budget said it would be satisfied if the department asked for public comment on the question. 
That step would allow the draft regulation to be published, an essential step before it can be put into effect. But even so, several senators of both parties said they were suspicious that the new issue would serve to delay implementation of the proposed regulations, which set limits on exposure to lead and a wide range of toxic chemicals.
The issue arose on March 10, when James B. MacRae Jr., acting administrator of O.M.B.'s Office of Information and Regulatory Affairs, announced that he was suspending review of regulations affecting the construction, agriculture and maritime industries, a move that would have left the regulations in limbo. He said he was doing so because the Occupational Safety and Health Administration's review of the regulations failed to examine the effect on workers of the costs of compliance.
 
Trying to See a Link
 When regulations impose higher costs on employers, he said, employers sometimes cut wages or reduce employment. That makes workers poorer, he said, and consequently less healthy, because "richer workers on average buy more leisure time, more nutritious food, more preventive health care and smoke and drink less than poorer workers."
In the hearing, Senator James M. Jeffords, Republican of Vermont, said he feared that even inviting public comment would "greatly extend" the time it takes ultimately to get the regulations issued.
At a hearing of the Senate Labor and Human Resources Committee on Wednesday, Mr. Jeffords said the budget office approach had little support among Republicans. He said he could not repeat the expletives used when the issue was discussed in the Republican caucus the previous week.
Senator Edward M. Kennedy, chairman of the committee, charged that the entire effort was part of the Bush Administration's "blatant election-year move to cater to business."
 
'A Bit Off the Wall'
 "While this unnecessary controversy goes on, more workers will get sick, and some will die," the Massachusetts Democrat said. "With this Administration's encouragement, businesses will continue to pocket the funds they should be spending to eliminate dangerous conditions in the workplace."
Senator David Durenberger, Republican of Minnesota, said during the hearing of the Labor and Human Resources Committee that the whole idea was "a bit off the wall."
A Labor Department official told the committee that she expected to reach agreement with O.M.B. by the end of last week on how to frame the question for public comment. By the weekend, however, the question had not been settled.
Witnesses at Mr. Kennedy's hearing had argued that the analysis could take years to complete.
 
No Defenders Found
 Mr. MacRae said there were studies suggesting that "each $7.5 million in additional regulatory expenditures may result in one additional death from lowered incomes." Since the costs to employers would come to $163 million annually, he said, that could cause 22 additional deaths -- or more than the 8 to 13 deaths a year OSHA believed the new rules would prevent.
The complaints about the budget office approach, which had no defenders at the hearing, fell into three categories. Mr. Kennedy called it an attempt to revive a weighing of costs and benefits that the Supreme Court had outlawed in 1980 on the grounds that the Occupational Safety and Health Act allowed only consideration of impacts on health.
That argument, in muffled terms, was offered by Nancy Risque Rohrbach, Assistant Secretary of Labor for policy, in her reply to Mr. MacRae on Monday. Mr. Kennedy also put into the record an earlier draft of her letter in which she said Mr. MacRae's approach contradicted the Court decision and Congressional intent.
A second argument was that the approach was based on "speculative hypotheses set forth in a small number of obscure academic publications," as Margaret Seminario, director of occupational safety and health for the A.F.L.-C.I.O., put it.
 
'Cold and Uncaring'?
 She said the O.M.B. reasoning about cause and effect was so badly flawed that it could not form a basis for serious policy making. While some individuals facing a loss of income might smoke less, others might eat less meat, and still others would cancel a vacation. "Neither O.M.B. nor anyone else knows the effect of income reductions on individuals or what the public health consequences will be," she said.
Dr. Anthony Robbins of Boston University, a past president of the American Public Health Association, called the proposals "cold and uncaring."
"I would like to close down O.M.B. for six months so that the economists who daily manipulate data and revel in mischievous speculation could be sent to work on ships, constructing buildings and harvesting crops," he said.
Senator Howard M. Metzenbaum, Democrat of Ohio, assailed the budget office as too powerful and out of touch with the people. He complained about the "absurd and twisted thinking that less protection saves lives."
A clear purpose of the Congressional effort was to encourage the Labor Department to stand firm against Mr. MacRae and O.M.B. Mr. Jeffords offered the department his "condolences." Mr. Kennedy said, "We want to give the Labor Department all the support it really deserves."

HEADLINE: Machine Tool Orders Up 6.4% in February

BYLINE: By SETH FAISON Jr.

 BODY:
Orders for American-made machine tools rose 6.4 percent last month from January's level, in another sign of renewed economic growth, according to a report to be released today by an industry trade group.
The trade group, the Association for Manufacturing Technology, calculated February's orders at $231.4 million, up from $217.4 million in January. The amount of February orders was down 5.6 percent, however, from the $245.2 million of February 1991. 
 Together with January's figures, year-to-date orders for machine tools totaled $448.8 million, up nine-tenths of a percent from $445.0 million in the first two months of last year.
Of the $231.4 million in total orders, $200.6 million were placed by American companies and $30.8 million were sold for export.
"Domestic orders are off to a good start," Albert W. Moore, president of the association, said in a statement. He noted that domestic machine tool sales had topped $200 million for two consecutive months, while in 1991 domestic sales topped $200 million only three times all year.
The association's statistics combine figures for two kinds of machinery: metal-cutting and metal-forming machines. Each are used to produce a significant number of manufactured goods, from refrigerators and cookware to automobiles and aircraft, and are regarded by economists as one of many indicators of the nation's economic condition.
With exports depressed in recent months, Mr. Moore expressed determination to press ahead with efforts to gain foreign market share, despite concerns that foreign markets may recover more slowly than domestic industries.
"The slow- or no-growth condition in much of Asia and Europe does not mean we should pull back," Mr. Moore said. "Instead, it is precisely the time to invest more in export promotion."
At $30.8 million, export orders of machine tools were more than double the $13.2 million of January, but down sharply from the $80.1 million of February 1991.
"Japan and Germany have always found economic downturns in foreign markets a good time to intensify their efforts and gain foreign market share," Mr. Moore said. "The machine tool industry is now global, and the U.S. industry must continue the successful strategy of diversifying our markets."
Actual shipments of machine tools in February grew faster than orders, soaring 27.2 percent, to $203.2 million, from $159.8 million in January.
The individual statistic that changed most sharply in February from the previous month was export orders for metal-cutting machinery, rising to $19.6 million, from $5.2 million in January. The February total was small, however, compared with the $72.5 million posted in the same category a year earlier.

HEADLINE: Peter Sammartino, 87, Is Dead; Was Fairleigh Dickinson Founder

BYLINE: By BRUCE LAMBERT

 BODY:
Peter Sammartino, the founder of Fairleigh Dickinson University and an early leader in the restoration of Ellis Island, was found shot to death yesterday with his wife, Sylvia, at their home in Rutherford, N.J., in what the police said was a murder-suicide. He was 87 years old and his wife was 88.
The couple had been in failing health. The Bergen County Prosecutor, John J. Fahy, said Mrs. Sammartino had Alzheimer's disease and Dr. Sammartino had undergone surgery to remove a kidney. 
 No note was found. But Mr. Fahy said that Dr. Sammartino had been recently getting his affairs in order and had hinted to friends that he might die soon.
The university Dr. Sammartino founded in the old Ivison Castle in Rutherford is to celebrate its 50th anniversary later this year. He began with 60 students. The initial decision to start a two-year junior college was made four days before the bombing of Pearl Harbor. The next morning Dr. Sammartino and a handful of colleagues reconsidered and, by one vote, decided to proceed.
 
Enrollment of 12,000
 Today Fairleigh Dickinson is the largest private university in New Jersey. It has an enrollment of 12,000 and campuses in Teaneck-Hackensack, Rutherford and Florham Park-Madison, plus a branch in Wroxton, England. Its programs include liberal arts, business administration, dentistry, engineering and education.
Mrs. Sammartino, the former Sylvia (Sally) Scaramelli, served without pay for 14 years as its first director of admissions and retired as dean of admissions. The university's initial financing was $15,000 from Dr. Sammartino, $15,000 from his father-in-law and $30,000 from Col. Fairleigh S. Dickinson, the head of the Becton, Dickinson surgical appliance company, for whom the school was named. The original building cost $21,600. Years later, individual contributions rose into the millions of dollars.
An innovative educator, Dr. Sammartino insisted that teachers visit students in their homes, sent the social sciences department to visit Asia and Africa, dispatched students to the Bowery, coal mines and migrant camps, and required some students to buy a share of stock in any company to study its reports.
When new buildings were planned, the contractors' bids were opened by students at assemblies, and architects were on stage to answer questions. The students voted their recommendations to the trustees. When money ran out on one project, the students decided to complete the work themselves. Dr. Sammartino and the faculty donned overalls to join them.
To impress local business with the college's economic importance, he once paid the staff and faculty in silver dollars, and the merchants quickly took notice. He also a promoted health food, buying chemical-free beef and providing free brewer's yeast and cheap vitamins for students. He was an outspoken critic of water fluoridation.
Dr. Sammartino served as president until he retired in 1967, but remained active at the university and in other causes.
 
Active in Civic Affairs
 He was the president of the New York Cultural Center, in the former Gallery of Modern Art building at Columbus Circle, for five years. The gallery was donated to the university by Huntington Hartford, heir to the A& P fortune. Mr. Sammartino was also on the board of the State Opera Society. He was the founder and chairman of the Restore Ellis Island Committee, but he retired from the effort in 1978. He also founded the International Association of University Professors and served as its president.
He was chairman of an eight-town economic development commission and vice president of the New Jersey Constitutional Convention and the National Italian-Americans Foundation. He also participated in the President's Commission on Higher Education, the White House Conference on Education, the Peace Corps advisory board and the National Board of Foreign Scholarships. He worked to improve services for the hearing-impaired, of which he was one.
Dr. Sammartino was born in New York City. He graduated from City College in 1924 at New York University earned a master's degree in education in 1928 and a doctorate in 1931. The idea for Fairleigh Dickinson grew out of his work for 10 years at the New College, an experimental school at Columbia University's Teachers College. He wrote more than a dozen books on education.
Mrs. Sammartino was born in Boston. She graduated from Smith College and earned a master's degree in history from Columbia. She was chairwoman of the State Commission on Women and president of the Garden State Ballet Foundation.
A university spokeswoman said she did not know of any immediate survivors.
A maid found the Sammartinos bodies in the bedroom of their home in Rutherford early yesterday afternoon, after she suspected something was amiss and forced open the locked door.
The police recovered a .38-caliber revolver. Mrs. Sammartino was shot in the temple and Dr. Sammartino was shot in the mouth, Mr. Fahy said. The last time the couple were seen alive was 4:30 P.M. Saturday, when the maid left the house for the day.

HEADLINE: In Hoboken, Debate Over Waterfront Plan Heats Up as Vote Looms

BYLINE: By EVELYN NIEVES, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: HOBOKEN, N.J., March 29

 BODY:
This city is in the throes of a pre-election blitz. Campaigners are meeting commuters at the bus, train and ferry depots. Pamphleteers are canvassing the rows of tightly packed houses. There are posters and buttons, fund-raisers and rallies, and plenty of debates.
At issue here for the second time in less than two years is the future of Hoboken's historic waterfront. On Tuesday, voters in the mile-square city of 33,400 will decide whether to enter a waterfront redevelopment agreement with the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey. The previous referendum on the question was narrowly defeated in July 1990. This time, those on both sides say the contest is a dead heat. 
 The debate is not whether the moldering Hudson River port should be developed. Most here believe it should. Voters are grappling with whether the development proposed is the right kind, for the right reasons and with the right partnership.
 
Looking for Jobs
 To proponents, led by a recently formed City Hall contingent called the People for Hoboken's Future, the agreement with the authority means 4,500 new jobs, new recreation areas for the city's children and new revenues to stabilize the city's property tax rate, one of the highest in the state.
Opponents, led by a citizens' group called the Coalition for a Better Waterfront, say that while there is no guarantee the plan will bring benefits, it is certain to give too much away to the Port Authority, wreak havoc on the environment and ruin Hoboken's small scale and charm.
And to some other residents, the plan and the issues swirling around it have been too complex and confusing to follow.
"I don't understand what any of them are talking about," said Luis Rivera, 21, who moved to the northwest end of Hoboken two years ago.
The city's plan is a scaled-back version of the one defeated in 1990. It proposes a 20-story office tower and nine-story hotel on a 5.5-acre pier near the city's transportation terminal and two residential buildings, 21 and 17 stories, just west of the pier. The plan also calls for a new athletic field and for expanding the city's Little League baseball field. The rest of the milelong stretch of waterfront would be developed with the advice of a citizens advisory committee.
 
Accusations Fly
 Those favoring the plan have accused its opponents of being interested only in whether development blocks their view of the river. Those against it say the promise of jobs is a pipe dream -- that city officials are most interested in a political victory and short-term payments to fix a hole in Hoboken's budget.
The thorniest issue for the city is the lease agreement with the Port Authority. Under the agreement, the Port Authority would lease the pier and the parcels next to it for 55 years. In exchange, the agency would pay the city $3.25 million as soon as the referendum is passed, part of $20 million in fixed lease payments.
The Port Authority would also prepare the site for development, build the waterfront park and expand the Little League field. It would sublease the property to developers, giving the city 20 percent of the gross revenues for the first 35 years and 60 percent for the remaining 20 years.
Opponents say the plan cedes controls of the city's most valuable asset for peanuts. The Port Authority would have a $10.25 lien on the property -- the $3.25 million it would hand out once the referendum passes, plus $7 million it handed the city in 1989, before the first plan was defeated.
But proponents say the lien means only that the Port Authority would get its money back if Hoboken entered into an agreement with a developer and without the Port Authority. And they say the waterfront, which lost its last link to the busy heyday immortalized in Elia Kazan's "On the Waterfront" when the Maxwell House plant closed less than two weeks ago, could lie dormant for years if the plan is not approved.
Tom Newman, a City Councilman who is the leading spokesman for the plan, said: "Three years ago, when the private developers of the world were beating down the doors to get a shot at the waterfront, I argued that we didn't need the Port Authority to prime the pump for us in order to attract investors. But now, we're in a recession. There's nobody out there with $100 million ready to invest in Hoboken."
But Ron Hine, a leading member of the Coalition for a Better Waterfront, said he believes that just as the recession halted the legions of potential investors, the coming recovery would bring enough of them back to enable Hoboken to make choices.
"That's another of the city's big lies -- that the Port Authority is the only investor we could find," said Mr. Hine, whose group has proposed an alternative development plan for the entire length of the waterfront. The plan calls for a milelong esplanade, 19 blocks of mixed residential and commercial development and and more than 20 acres of new parks.
But Mr. Newman said the city needs a plan now. "We have to deal with dire realities," he said. "The plan we have before the voters may not be my ideal plan but it's one I can live with."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Voters in Hoboken, N.J., are to decide tomorrow whether to enter an agreement with the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey to redevelop the Hoboken waterfront, above. The debate is not about whether to redevelop but whether the plan is the right one with the right people; The Coalition for a Better Waterfront, a citizens' group opposed to the Port Authority plan, says it would give too much away to the Port Authority. HEADLINE: Market Place;
Phone Company Growing Rapidly

BYLINE: By Anthony Ramirez

 BODY:
IN Monroe, La., is a little-known company worth more than $1 billion -- Century Telephone Enterprises Inc., a rarity in the telephone business. Last year, Century's profits rose 20 percent, in an industry where earnings gains in the single digits are considered a good performance. Some analysts expect the company's annual earnings growth to exceed 25 percent for the rest of the decade.
"This is a growth company disguised as a telephone company," said John P. Reddan, a media and communications analyst at Moran & Associates in Greenwich, Conn. Century has consistently performed better than analysts' estimates, Mr. Reddan noted. 
 Century Telephone has a large cellular telephone business that it has been investing in for several years. The operation is about to break even, analysts say, probably in the quarter ending this month or the quarter ending on June 30. Proponents of the stock say investors can essentially get its cellular properties for free.
Rather than buy expensive cellular telephone properties in big cities, Century Telephone has bought cheaper rural properties next door. It has reaped benefits when big-city subscribers paid "roamer fees" to use Century's network when they made calls during trips or commuting home. Century's mainstay conventional telephone network has grown largely through acquisition and internal growth, again away from the big cities.
While analysts think highly of the company, they are divided on whether to buy its stock. Some recommend purchase, but others say it is substantially overpriced at 29 times earnings.
Century Telephone closed Friday at $34.875 a share on the New York Stock Exchange, down 37.5 cents and close to its 52-week high of $37.25. With 31.3 million shares outstanding, Century Telephone's market capitalization is nearly $1.1 billion.
Analysts expect earnings to be in the range of $1.45 a share for 1992 and $2.10 for 1993. But part of the hesitancy among some analysts about the stock is its relatively rich price compared with annual cash flow, about $7 for every $1 of estimated 1992 cash flow, compared with $6 or less for other telephone companies.
Most huge telephone companies, like the GTE Corporation and the regional Bell operating companies, have been struggling in the last year or so. With nearly 80 percent of the market for local telephone service, they have steady revenue and guaranteed earnings as near-monopolies but often must confront openly hostile regulators who want to keep rates low for consumers. The recession has hurt the big companies' revenue at a time when labor costs, often the majority of their operating costs, remain high.
Like others among the nearly 1,300 telephone companies that make up the other 20 percent of the local phone market, Century Telephone has benefited from the lower operating and maintenance costs found in rural areas. And analysts laud it for being one of the best managed of the smaller phone companies, with an emphasis on holding down costs without sacrificing performance.
In the states where Century has most of its conventional telephone operations -- Louisiana, Michigan, Wisconsin and Arkansas -- regulators have not been as hostile to it as other states' regulators have been to phone companies. In Texas, for example, carriers sometimes find it difficult to recover costs, analysts say.
What most excites analysts is Century's cellular properties. Mr. Reddan of Moran & Associates, values Century's conventional telephone service at $1.3 billion, or $42 a share. In valuing the cellular business, he says that Century has 3.7 million potential subscribers, or "pops," in urban areas, at $195 a pop, and another 1.9 million potential subscribers in rural areas, at about $100 a pop. That means, he concludes, Century's cellular properties are worth about $29 a share.
Alexander Alma, an analyst at Martin Simpson & Company, projects that by 1993 the cellular business will be at least 25 percent of Century's annual revenue, or more than double the proportion of 1991 and 1992. Earnings will be more than 25 percent cellular, Mr. Alma estimates, compared with today's earnings, which are all from conventional telephone service.
Analysts agree that the cellular properties will perform well, compared with the nearly $5 million loss in 1991, but are divided about how well. Joel D. Gross, an analyst with Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette Inc., for example, expects cellular and a small operation in electronic pagers to be profitable this quarter, with their combined operating income estimated at $4.4 million in 1992, and 1993 income estimated at $13.2 million.
Mr. Reddan of Moran & Associates, on the other hand, estimated that 1992 operating income would be 60 percent more, or $7 million, and about 5 percent more in 1993, or $13.8 million.
Mr. Gross of Donaldson, Lufkin gets queasy, however, when his computer says Century Telephone is fully priced at about $35 a share. He said that he was neutral on short-term purchase but that the stock was fine for long-term accumulation.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Some Predict Era of Low Inflation

BYLINE: By SYLVIA NASAR

 BODY:
Although last week's report on gross domestic product showed that inflation has been extraordinarily low lately, most economists expect prices to revive along with the economy.
Earlier this month, for example, the Blue Chip consensus of 50 forecasters predicted that the nation's price level would rise about 4 percent a year -- twice the rate of the last nine months -- for the rest of the decade. 
 But some contrariant Federal Reserve officials and private economists think that inflation is likely to stay as low as it did during the 1960's, when it ran around 2 percent. They are trying to convert bond investors to this view.
"Largely due to actions which took place before I joined the Fed, we have a good chance to achieve effective price stability in America by mid-decade," Lawrence B. Lindsay, a newly appointed Fed governor, told members of the Government Bond Club of New England in a speech two weeks ago.
 
Unexpected Consequences
 If Mr. Lindsay is right, some of the consequences for individuals and the economy could be quite unexpected. Most startling, lower inflation would have the same impact on the economy as a supply-side tax cut aimed at raising savings and investment.
"There is a widespread consensus in the economics profession that higher rates of saving and capital accumulation would be beneficial to the U.S. economy," said Mr. Lindsay, once the most prominent supply-side economist in the Bush Administration and a former professor of public finance at Harvard University.
"Numerous schemes have been advanced to achieve this end through the tax system," he said. "The point is that disinflation would produce much the same result."
Few members of Mr. Lindsay's under-40 generation of public finance experts would disagree. "We have a tax system that is not indexed for inflation," said James Poterba at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology. "Inflation raises the tax burden on capital."
Lawrence A. Kudlow, chief economist at Bear, Stearns & Company and a budget official in the Reagan Administration, agreed. "Very few people consider the tax implications of the inflation rate."
 
Raising After-Tax Returns
 A permanent drop in inflation raises the after-tax returns of savings and investment. The reason: When Congress revised the tax code in the mid-1980's to eliminate "bracket creep," which forced taxpayers to pay higher income taxes merely because inflation had swelled nominal incomes, the tax-writing body did not do the same for most kinds of investment income. Owners of bonds or real estate, for example, must pay taxes on nominal gains, which merely reflect inflation.
Consider how lower inflation would slash capital gains taxes. On the books, the capital gains tax rate is 28 percent. In real terms, however, with 4 percent inflation, the effective tax on the real gains from selling an asset held for three or four years is more than twice as high.
"The net effect of our tax system, coupled with current levels of inflation, is to make the effective tax rate on real capital gains well over 50 percent," Mr. Lindsay said.
Mr. Kudlow of Bear, Stearns estimated that a drop in inflation of two percentage points, from 4 percent to 2 percent, would slash the effective capital gains tax rate by about 20 percent.
 
Benefits for Business
 For businesses that buy new equipment, lower inflation amounts to an investment tax credit, Mr. Lindsay argued. A two-percentage-point decline in inflation is roughly equivalent to a 2 percent investment tax credit, he figured.
The reason is that businesses are allowed to deduct capital outlays from their incomes over a period of years. Income -- or in this case, a depreciation allowance -- a few years off is worth more or less depending on how much it must be discounted to account for the level of interest rates. Lower inflation makes depreciation more valuable because interest rates typically come down with inflation.
Lower inflation would also bolster the payoff to savers, just as a cut in the top income tax rate would.
It is just a matter of arithmetic. If bond yields are 8 percent and savers pay 30 percent of their interest income in Federal taxes, that means a bondholder's nominal after-tax return is 5.6 percent. With 4 percent inflation, their real return shrinks to 1.6 percent.
Now consider what happens when inflation remains at 2 percent. Interest rates may decline to 6 percent, but the after-tax return still manages to go up, to 4.2 percent. After taking inflation into account, the return is 2.2 percent, or nearly 40 percent higher than if inflation were 4 percent.
"Even though market rates are lower, the real rate of return is higher," Mr. Kudlow said. "The dollar buys more."
 
Other Factors Necessary
 To be sure, skeptics would say that higher returns alone would not necessarily produce more savings and investment. Real returns rose sharply when inflation fell from double digits to less than 5 percent in the early 1980's, but personal saving actually declined and business investment was about average. But most economists agree with Mr. Lindsay that higher real returns can only help.
As with any tax cut, there are winners and losers when inflation falls.
In the long run, Mr. Lindsay argues, lower inflation could make income distribution more equal.
For one thing, some of the measured gain in the incomes of the well-to-do has been caused by sharply higher interest income. But that income, Mr. Lindsay said, is partly compensation for the erosion of financial wealth from inflation and would decline with inflation. For another, the fact that housing would become more affordable should make the distribution of wealth more equal by spreading homeownership more widely.
What Mr. Lindsay does not say is that in the short run, lower-than-expected inflation would hurt borrowers and benefit lenders until interest rates adjusted downward, much as they did in similar episodes at the beginning of the 1980's. In the 1970's, of course, the tables were turned when inflation ended up much higher than expected.

HEADLINE: Travel Slows at Local Airports

BYLINE: By JACQUES STEINBERG

 BODY:
Constrained by the recession and the economic effects of the Persian Gulf war, the total number of travelers who passed through the region's three main airports declined sharply last year, according to a report released yesterday by the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey.
The overall number of passengers who used the three airports -- Kennedy International, La Guardia and Newark International -- fell 8.7 percent, from 74.8 million in 1990 to 68.3 million last year, the Port Authority said. The decline erased the slight increase of 0.5 percent that the airports had posted in 1990, compared with 1989. 
 The total decline came despite slight gains at Newark International, where passenger traffic climbed from 22.25 million in 1990 to 22.28 million last year.
Prof. Mitchell L. Moss, director of the New York University Urban Research Center, said the passenger figures reflected the economic fragility of the region, which, he said, lost 400,000 jobs last year.
"We had fewer people employed here," Professor Moss said. "And the firms were not only laying off people but were cutting back on their travel budgets."
 
Gulf War Cuts Travel
 In addition to the recession, Richard C. Leone, chairman of the Port Authority, cited the Gulf war as a factor. He said travelers were deterred from flying in early 1991 by fears of terrorism and by higher ticket prices, which rose in response to increased jet fuel costs.
"The good news is that we saw what may be the beginning of an upturn late in the year," Mr. Leone said in a statement.
Of the three airports, La Guardia had the worst year, with passenger traffic falling 13.5 percent, from 22.8 million in 1990 to 19.7 million last year, the authority said. The number of passengers at Kennedy dropped 11.6 percent, from 29.8 million in 1990 to 26.3 million last year.
The narrow increase at Newark, Mr. Leone said, was fueled in part by an 8.6 percent rise in international traffic.

HEADLINE: Transit Vote Shows Split In Union

BYLINE: By ALAN FINDER

 BODY:
Lou Corradi thinks of himself as a loyal union man, the son of a transit worker and a longtime supporter of Sonny Hall, the president of the transit workers' union. But Mr. Corradi could not even wait to bring his D train to a complete halt at the end of its run in Coney Island the other day before beginning a broad denunciation of his union and the tentative three-year contract that union leaders had negotiated with management.
"The workers are disgusted," Mr. Corradi said. "This really struck a chord." 
 Mr. Corradi was among thousands of New York City subway and bus workers who voted earlier this month, nearly 2 to 1, to reject the contract endorsed by the leaders of Local 100 of the Transport Workers Union. The union has now asked the Metropolitan Transportation Authority to reopen negotiations. Because transit officials say they have no more money to offer, the renewed bargaining is not likely to last long, and the dispute appears bound for arbitration.
The rejection of the contract was the first time in memory that the union's members had turned down a pact supported by the leadership. And while the vote clearly hinged on basic bread-and-butter issues -- including concessions on salary and health care -- it also exposed publicly some internal tensions that have been dividing the once monolithic local.
 
An Unusual Rejection
 To New Yorkers familiar with the iron-fisted reigns of colorful transit union leaders like Michael J. Quill and John E. Lawe, the sight of their successor, Sonny Hall, being rebuffed by young union members concerned about participatory democracy and women's rights was disconcerting, if not revolutionary.
Among other things, the rejection of the contract, by a vote of 13,110 to 7,220, reflected the rise of New Directions, a faction within the union that began challenging Mr. Hall's leadership four years ago over dissatisfaction with two contracts negotiated in the 1980's.
New Directions organized much of the campaign against the contract, distributing literature, putting together demonstrations and generally making its critique of the proposal known throughout the city's vast mass-transit system.
"They showed more concern about the membership than the union leadership did," said Mr. Corradi, although he added that he was skeptical of the dissident faction and was uncertain whether he would vote for their slate in the next union election, in three years.
Michael Clark, another train operator who was taking a break between runs in Coney Island, said that New Directions had done a better job explaining its position. "They came out here and they talked with us," he said. "We only see the other guys when it's election time."
The vote also reflected generational conflicts and changes in the composition of the transit work force, which in recent years has come to include many more women and more black and Hispanic workers. And it underscored a longstanding split within divisions of the transit system.
 
Women's Issues Mattered
 Most of the support for the contract came from bus drivers and other employees of the bus division in Manhattan and the Bronx, the traditional stronghold of the union's leaders, including Mr. Hall. Much of the opposition came from subway workers. Train operators, for example, rejected the pact by a margin of nearly 20 to 1.
"It was not so much this particular contract that upset us," Mr. Clark said. "It's been building up, an accumulation of issues."
Many women who work on the subways as conductors, train operators and car cleaners said that they had been angered because the union's leaders did not press issues important to them, including guaranteeing light duties for pregnant workers and more generous leaves for mothers and fathers of newborns.
"That was very important to me," said Denise, a car cleaner in Coney Island who declined to give her last name. "If I have a baby, I have no benefits."
Corine Scott, a train operator who is a member of New Directions, said that many matters of concern to women were simply ignored by union leaders in their discussions with management. "Sonny never took these seriously," Ms. Scott said.
 
'Money Is the Issue'
 It is hard to measure whether race, too, has played a role in the dissension within the union, many black and Hispanic workers said.
The slates offered by New Directions in the last two union elections, in 1988 and again last year, contained many black and Hispanic candidates. But within Mr. Hall's circle, the union establishment, there are also a number of minority-group members, reflecting perhaps that the proportion of black and Hispanic workers within the transit system has grown enormously in recent decades. Mr. Hall is white.
Mr. Clark, who is black, said he thought there might be some racial aspects to the union insurgency, but he offered no specific examples. Another black transit worker, a car inspector who spoke on the condition of anonymity, said he thought race played no part in the contract's defeat. "Money is the issue, not color," he said.
New Directions is an informal group, with no officers and a core of several hundred active members. The total union has about 32,000 members.
New Directions' candidate for local president, Tim Schermerhorn, won about one-quarter of the vote in the 1988 union election, said Steve Downs, a train operator who is a member.
Mr. Schermerhorn, who is also a train operator, won about a third of the vote last year and New Directions won nine seats on the union's 39-seat executive board, said Mr. Downs, who was one of the successful board candidates. The group wants the union to work more openly with its membership, consulting them more before they negotiate with management. It also wants more union jobs to be elective and not appointive, he said.
"In our view, there was a decided lack of democracy in the union," Mr. Downs said. "There was a feeling that the leadership was out of touch."
But Mr. Downs also said that the defeat of the contract reflected broad dissatisfaction with its provisions. It would be a mistake, he said, to view the rejection as simply the result of an internal power struggle.
"I think it is an important breakthrough, but it was not just New Direction's doing that the contract was voted down," Mr. Downs said. "There was widespread displeasure over the contract, and so we don't take credit for it."
Mr. Hall was equally candid in his own assessment of the vote, saying that he was surprised by the strength of the opposition and chastened by the outcome.
He had argued for the last two months that the tentative agreement with the M.T.A. was the best that could be obtained during the recession. It called for a 4.5 percent raise this year, a 2 percent raise next year and a $1,000 payment to cover inflation since last May, when the old contract expired.
Mr. Hall said he still believed that he had wrung from transit officials all that he could, but that he had clearly failed to convince his members of it. "I think there is a lot of merit to the fact that we have failed to spend the amount of time that we should out in the field," Mr. Hall said. "We've made a major mistake."
He said he believed the proposed contract was superior to any now being negotiated by unions representing other city or state workers, but that his members have made it clear that they want more. "I think the vast majority of the members were telling us, 'You've got to go back and get us more,' " Mr. Hall said.
"It's a tough one for me to handle," said Mr. Hall, who was re-elected president last November. "But I really believe that what's happened is that the internal factions, the people who want to be leaders of the union, were able to capture their minds."
The only way to wring additional money and concessions, he said, would be to convince the state to increase its operating subsidies to the transit agency, and that will be no easy undertaking, given the state's fiscal problems. "If we can't, then I don't know how we can resolve the contract in a peaceful way," Mr. Hall said.

HEADLINE: Editorial Notebook;
Jerry Brown, Peronist

BYLINE: By DAVID C. UNGER

 BODY:
It's June 1973, the year before Jerry Brown was first elected Governor of California. Argentina's repackaged populist legend, Juan Peron, returns to Buenos Aires in triumph. As his plane approaches, more than a million impassioned supporters line the expressway. They constitute an impossibly broad coalition: countercultural revolutionaries, blue-collar workers, Catholic mystics, hard-core fascists.
Just how impossibly broad seemed clear minutes later when gunfire erupted and Peronists, united only by their hero's stirring oratory, started shooting Peronists. Even so, the coalition endured; a few months later, Peron was elected President. 
 America is not Argentina. And Jerry Brown remains a long shot for the Democratic nomination, let alone for President. Still, there is an instructive parallel. Playing fast and loose with powerful political emotions, he offers himself, like Peron, as the leader of an impossibly broad coalition. Recklessly, he courts different constituencies with different, even contradictory positions. Which would guide him as President? If his candidacy is for real, there has to come a moment of truth.
Mr. Brown has come this far largely on the strength of what urban teen-agers call attitude -- a stance that only angry outsiders can be trusted not to sell out. He reaches out like a snarling Oliver Stone hero or rap star to audiences turned off by conventional politics.
He does democracy a huge favor when he exhorts alienated voters to re-enter politics. But so far he has offered his followers only the rush of protest. He's quick with scattershot debating points. Translating them into policies could leave even his most enthusiastic supporters feeling betrayed. Having spent eight years as a competent governor of the largest state, he knows government inside out. He's too smart for his inconsistencies to arise from ignorance. They smack, rather, of a renegade's bid to tap the politics of anger.
Jerry Brown, of the pilgrimages to Calcutta, Africa and Japan, is a convinced internationalist. As a Californian, he well understands the growing integration of the U.S. and Mexican economies. As Governor, he outspokenly favored the trend. Just last month, he impatiently reminded a debate questioner that aiding Russia creates jobs and markets for Americans.
But this month in Michigan, he played to auto workers' fears, indicting free trade for letting good jobs slip south of the border. Now, back in trade-dependent New York, Mr. Brown does a double reverse, denying that he's a protectionist and recalling that his home state's economy also depends on trade.
Consider the issue of abortion. Jerry Brown, graying alumnus of the California 1960's, believes in full reproductive rights for women. But he also agrees with Mother Teresa that abortion is "the killing of the unborn."
Consider the Middle East. Last week he told Ted Koppel on ABC that as President, he would do all in his power to stop Israeli settlements in the West Bank. But, he added, he wouldn't withhold U.S. loan guarantees over the issue. All in his power?
Jerry Brown is fast on his feet. He argues that his diverse positions can be reconciled. Maybe so, metaphysically. Rather than programmatic consistency, he offers attitudinal consistency: a sharp-edged anger that resonates among a variety of Americans. If he wants the rest of America to pay attention, he'll need to start translating attitude into plausible proposals.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Editorial

HEADLINE: Icy Words on Global Warming

 BODY:
Words matter, words wound. Asked last week whether he planned to attend the "Earth Summit" in Rio de Janeiro in June, Mr. Bush replied that "running for the Presidency" might keep him at home. Asked whether he would sign a treaty limiting the emission of gases that contribute to the threat of global warming, the President said not if it threatens American jobs.
It's true that stricter controls on fossil fuels could affect economic growth. But Mr. Bush's comments conveyed a parochial indifference and dismayed a universe of friends -- developing countries seeking Western assistance, European allies willing to take risks for a cleaner environment and the 105 heads of state who will attend the conference. 
 As the President surely knows, those leaders confront the same tough choices he does. The Rio meeting has one central theme: "sustainable development." That means finding ways to build the economies of rich and poor nations alike without wrecking the environment.
No easy task, especially for the poor. For example, the conference seeks to slow the wanton destruction of the world's rain forests. Poor countries, however, see the forests as sources of jobs and revenue. Thus the U.S. is not the only country which fears that environmental restrictions will limit growth. Yet the U.S. has been uniquely sluggish in the pre-Rio negotiations. Why? The main problem is a proposed binding treaty on global warming. Several European countries and Japan have urged that by the year 2000 all nations cap emissions of carbon dioxide -- the chief gas implicated in trapping heat in the atmosphere -- at 1990 levels. That scares White House officials, who fear that restricting fuels that produce carbon dioxide, like oil and coal, would be too costly.
Yet if the U.S., the world's largest emitter of carbon dioxide, doesn't move, what incentive is there for poor countries, whose development depends on fossil fuels, to make sacrifices?
It should not be impossible for the U.S. to sign the treaty. Data available to Mr. Bush suggest that even if the U.S. did nothing new, greenhouse gases would increase by only 5 percent by the year 2000. A few additional measures -- wider use of clean fuels like natural gas or further conservation incentives for utilities -- might do the trick with little pain.
If Mr. Bush still refuses to accept the cap, the language of diplomacy might help. The treaty could simply call for measures that aim at, but do not guarantee, carbon dioxide stabilization by the turn of the century.
In any case, there's no longer much doubt that global warming is a worrisome prospect. What is in doubt is America's resolve. Mr. Bush should go to the conference, carrying a commitment to the well-being of the earth.

TYPE: Editorial

HEADLINE: Essay;
China's Silent Upheaval

BYLINE: By WILLIAM SAFIRE

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
"The word is racing gleefully through Beijing intellectual circles," reported The New York Times's Nicholas Kristof early this year, "that an impeccably reliable American political forecaster, renowned for his clairvoyance, has declared that there will be immense changes in China in 1992."
This mysterious Nostradamus (a prophet without honor in his own country) has already been proven partly right. 
 Chinese Communist hard-liners led by the ideologue Chen Yun, 86, saw the disintegration of the Soviet Union as proof that reform was heresy, and that decentralization was "economic warlordism." They viewed the Tiananmen crackdown as sensible precaution, and sneered at "peaceful evolution" as a sellout to capitalism.
That brought the old anti-Maoist economic reformer, Deng Xiaoping, 87, rolling out of bed for his last hurrah. "Some comrades inside the party remain muddleheaded," he charged. "Had it not been for our reform over the past 10 years, our situation could have been worse than the U.S.S.R.'s."
China-watchers tell of the cultural struggle between the rigid north and the freewheeling south; between the inward-looking Yellow-River mindset of the interior and the "blue-water" mindset of the ports of Shanghai and Guangzhou, formerly Canton. Mao and Chen Yun were Yellow-River; Zhou Enlai and Deng were blue-water.
Although Deng maintains "we cannot do without dictatorship," he sees the need for the sort of capitalism now burgeoning in the blue-water provinces. To dramatize his case, the old man went on a final campaign tour of China's southern models of free-ish enterprise.
With control of the party to be decided this fall at the 14th Congress, the battle was joined. "Whoever is opposed to reform," Deng told the Chen faction, "must leave office." That was a warning to Prime Minister Li Peng, disliked by reformers and hated by intellectuals, who tries to straddle the fence between hard-line leftists and capitalist-roaders.
Editors put in power by the crackdown crew after the Tiananmen massacre blacked out Deng's tour. We are only now getting accounts of Deng's divisive speeches (a good argument for starting Radio Free Asia). But his strange message is getting through: Communism can succeed only through an infusion of capitalism. "The main danger in thought inside the party comes from some people's refusal to admit that Marxism needs continuous development and revision."
For the paramount Communist leader to call for theoretical "revision" is a very big thing. Revisionism is counterrevolutionary, a sin ascribed by diehards to Gorbachev.
Deng is not talking about political freedom. He thinks the only way to hold together the 50 ethnic groups that make up China, not to mention Taiwan and Tibet, is to buy stability through prosperity. Deng talks of "emancipating the mind," but he means that China is more easily ruled if the people have full bellies rather than free minds.
I think he's wrong about that: political and economic freedom are ultimately one and inseparable. But friends of mine in China who were distressed by the 1989 crackdown are now encouraged by Deng's 1992 counterattack. They say that the Soviet mistake was in putting glasnost before perestroika. They prefer a more orderly upheaval, believing that if China first puts capitalism to work, political freedom will follow.
President Bush accepts Deng's half a loaf, and refuses to apply economic pressure to win human-rights gains. (Last week, he applied such pressure to get China's vote to isolate Libya, negating what little principle undergirded his policy of placating Beijing.)
We should be publicizing Deng's campaign, reaching out to his disciple, Zhu Rongji, while freezing out Chun's followers.
As we hail most of Deng's "four modernizations," the U.S. should remind Beijing of what the dissenter Wei Jingsheng, still in jail after 13 years, called the fifth modernization -- democracy. Hunger has different forms; freedom to think can be as nourishing as freedom to eat.
Will my off-the-wall soothsaying of "China convulses, goes democratic" come true? The timing may be optimistic, but rub the crystal ball: see how the suppressed story of Deng's tour has brought creative instability to this Year of the Monkey.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: How to Turn Our Guns Into Their Butter

 BODY:
To the Editor:
In "How to Lose the Cold War" (Op-Ed, March 10), Daniel Schorr cites Richard M. Nixon's warning of a "new despotism" in the former Soviet Union that would spread rapidly. What better way to save the "cold war victory" than for President Bush and Congress to start a new Marshall Plan for the former Soviet republics and Eastern Europe and to meet United Nations peacekeeping costs.
To underwrite these bold initiatives for true national security, $10 billion can be transferred from the proposed House of Representatives defense budget of $287.2 billion for fiscal year 1993, to be combined with equal contributions by Japan and the European Community. Such a program would generate exports, lift the world economy out of recession and create lasting jobs for our defense workers and the troops who would come home. 
 Starting in 1947, the United States could afford $12 billion for the original Marshall Plan by cutting military spending 90 percent within three years after World War II. In contrast President Bush proposes a minuscule 6 percent cut by 1997.
The House provides that the cut of $9.2 billion to date is to be used for defense worker retraining, deficit reduction and public investment. The additional $10 billion can be taken from programs obviously not needed with the end of the cold war including all nuclear testing, production of B-2 bombers, antiballistic missiles, almost all "Star Wars" and a great many of the 395 United States military bases on foreign soil.
An even larger total cut of $25 billion has been urged by many members of Congress and more than 100 national organizations.
The President and Congress must be told to find in the defense budget the emergency funds that President Nixon calls for to insure the "victory of freedom."
 
RICHARD H. MATTOX
Delmar, N.Y., March 16, 1992

HEADLINE: How to Turn Our Guns Into Their Butter;
Habit of Belligerence

 BODY:
To the Editor:
In "America Only" (editorial, March 10), you ask: "why on earth does the Administration allow the Defense Department alone to define the terms of the national debate on U.S. strategy after the cold war?" And, further, why don't the State Department and the National Security Council also weigh in?
A large part of the answer is that all three institutions operate as a well-oiled foreign policy machine that emphasizes military power over economic and cultural influence. As a career diplomat and economist member of the Secretary of State's policy planning and coordination staff during the false detente of the early 1970's, I raised that issue to fellow diplomats. Young diplomats sick of rationalizing Vietnam to disbelieving industrial democracies were then more worried about losing Europe than Indochina. 
 President Nixon and Henry Kissinger had come up with a plan to normalize economic and cultural relations with the Communist world. However, the State Department was staffed and organized to wage economic and ideological war to contain Communism. Its deeply imbedded world view was that of a policeman, rather than a merchant. A departmental purge would have been necessary to implement a new policy, followed by a reorganization along the lines of modern European foreign ministries that stress economics and culture over war.
The Department of State was not made over but further militarized during the stand-tall Reagan-Bush era. It is naive today to expect that Secretary of State James A. Baker 3d or his senior Foreign Service deputies will change the institution that serves their careers so well. More likely would be their recommendation for a well-orchestrated attack on Libya or Iraq to contain terrorism, instead of Communism, and which could extend their collective reign for at least another four years.
Our deepening economic and cultural crisis will someday sweep in a President and Congress determined to fix our broken foreign policy apparatus.
 
CORD D. HANSEN-STURM
New York, March 10, 1992

HEADLINE: How to Turn Our Guns Into Their Butter;
Seeking New Enemies

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Re "U.S. Strategy Plan Calls for Insuring No Rivals Develop" (front page, March 8):
Dwight Eisenhower, a military man, left us a warning against the military-industrial complex, which exploits world tensions for its own ends. Now the demise of the Russian bear has diminished tensions, so scenarios are invented to sustain them. 
John Foster Dulles, Eisenhower's first Secretary of State, told how:
"In order to bring a nation to support the burdens of maintaining great military establishments, it is necessary to create an emotional state akin to war psychology. There must be a portrayal of external menace. This involves . . . arousing the population to a sense of sacrifice. Once these exist, we have gone a long way on the path of war."
Those who profit from turmoil, fearing for their power as peace breaks out, are trying to resurrect the open channel between the arms makers and the United States Treasury. This combination went on a spending spree under Ronald Reagan, leaving us with rusting weapons and a staggering deficit. Now they offer us the gallant image of America against the world when there has been a billionfold increase in the power and delivery of weapons.
The recession has made citizens skeptical of the cover stories that explain why the infrastructure deteriorated while the military-industrial complex became bloated. They are asking why the peace dividend is being appropriated to the same end.
It is not insignificant that the text of the Pentagon's bellicose plan was not sent to Congress, the representatives of the people.
 
NANCY REEVES
San Diego, March 9, 1992

HEADLINE: Foreign Affairs;
The Russian Sinkhole

BYLINE: By LESLIE H. GELB

 BODY:
As the U.S. foreign policy establishment stampedes toward a megabillion-dollar comprehensive "aid" program for the ex-Soviet Union, consider the following facts:
* These states have 44 nuclear power plants, including 15 of the Chernobyl style, many of which are in terrible shape and could spring radioactive leaks. One near St. Petersburg leaked two weeks ago. 
 * Ukraine and Kazakhstan now threaten to take control of intercontinental-range missiles on their territory. If they do, they would instantly become the third- and fourth-largest nuclear powers after the U.S. and Russia.
* Russia's debt is running at 25 percent of total Government expenditures for the first quarter of this year. There is massive underpayment of taxes by business enterprises and local governments.
* Wages have dropped so low relative to freed prices that some 90 percent of Russians now live below subsistence levels. Subsistence is about 1,500 rubles monthly, while per capita income now totals about 900 rubles monthly or $8.25. Life will get much worse if, as expected, production in key sectors falls an additional 50 percent this year.
* The ex-Soviet states are now meeting only 30 percent of their interest payments (and almost no principal) on debts to the West of $70 billion. Most of this is owed to or guaranteed by Western governments.
* Various forms of Western aid to the ex-Soviet states totaled about $50 billion in the last 20 months, and the money has virtually disappeared without a trace or a dent on the economic picture. Tens of billions in hard currency controlled by Communist Party officials also vanished in the last two years.
These sad facts -- plus civil and ethnic wars and the lack of cooperation among the ex-Soviet republics -- suggest that the new states are nearing military, economic and political anarchy.
Such considerations should cause American political leaders to think again, and hard, about rescue plans. The danger is that we will waste billions and, even more tragically, fail to zero in on what can be realistically accomplished by outside aid.
My particular concern is Richard Nixon's recent proposal for a comprehensive aid plan. He argued that anything less would ignite a "Who lost Russia?" debate. He accused President Bush of playing "a penny-ante game" where the stakes were geopolitical survival. He called for tens of billions of dollars to stabilize the ruble and more tens of billions in other help. He made more modest and practical proposals as well. But the practical ideas have gotten lost in the political scramble he triggered.
Mr. Bush, stung by the Nixon charges, is now under the gun to present a bigger-bucks plan than he would otherwise have done. Governor Clinton, the leading Democratic Presidential contender, could try to outbid him in a major world affairs address Wednesday. Congressional leaders and foreign policy gurus are also jumping on the now fashionable big-bucks, big-plan bandwagon.
The West should be working on plans for big aid programs -- currency stabilization, import and export credits and the like. But these plans have to be for later, when and if the threat of anarchy subsides and some stability returns. Any cash given now would simply vanish down a vast sinkhole.
For now, Western governments should not throw away their aid on saving governments that can only save themselves. Instead, they should focus on specific, pressing and solvable problems and on seeding the future of democracy.
First priority must go to the nukes. This means immediate help to repair or shut down unsafe nuclear reactors. It means talking very tough with Ukraine and others about their nuclear weapons aspirations and putting experts on the ground now to help them dismantle weapons.
The second priority should be agriculture and food aid. Without bread, the return of dictatorships is inevitable.
The third priority has to be sending experts and technical aid directly to groups committed to running businesses and making democracy work.
The stakes could not be higher. All the more reason for substantial, practical and immediate aid -- not for grand illusions.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 2; Metropolitan Desk

LENGTH: 1664 words

HEADLINE: Their Wages Low, Single Mothers Get Little Help

BYLINE: By CELIA W. DUGGER

 BODY:
Milagros Reyes took her baby boy to the hospital for a hernia operation, but had to rush away a few hours after surgery to get back to work. Her 6-month-old son, bandaged and scared, was crying as she left, but she feared that a missed day might get her fired from her $7.79-an-hour factory job.
The same anxiety has also driven Ms. Reyes to ignore the advice of her baby's pediatrician who says she should quit her job. Her son, Juan Carlos, cared for by a series of poorly paid sitters, is severely underweight, the doctor told her. But Ms. Reyes feels she cannot afford to stop working. 
 "I have to pay the rent," she explained with a helpless shrug.
As do a growing number of single women raising children alone, Ms. Reyes faces an agonizing choice between the dead-end certainty of poverty on welfare and the grinding struggle to survive and get ahead in a low-paying job.
The number of single working mothers has soared from 2.1 million in 1971, to 4.4 million in 1981, to 5.8 million in 1991, according to the U.S. Bureau of Labor Statistics. Two million had incomes of only $10,000 to $20,000 in 1990.
These women often cannot afford housing, health insurance or quality day care, and most of them get no child support from their children's fathers, say researchers.
"The system is totally stacked against single working moms," said David T. Ellwood, a professor of public policy at the John F. Kennedy School of Government at Harvard University. "It's very difficult for them to survive, and the only alternative is welfare. The current welfare system only helps women when they've failed and reduces the support for them as they move forward in the workplace."
Ms. Reyes feels that, despite the difficulties she has had finding decent child care for her son, welfare would lead her nowhere. "People take the money and don't do anything," she said. She came to New York from the Dominican Republic when she was 14 and has worked full time in the same garment factory in the 19 years since.
This year, the Federal Government has modestly expanded a program to help low-income working families. Parents who make up to $21,000 will get as much as $2,020 back in an earned income tax credit. Some mothers interviewed recently said they were unaware that they were entitled to the credit. Others said it had slightly eased their pinched circumstances.
Working mothers living in New York City on the cusp of poverty -- just above a sharp line drawn by the Government that starts at $9,388 for a woman and one child -- say their path is especially treacherous because the high cost of housing often forces them to live in poor neighborhoods where predatory drug dealers and violent crime endanger their children.
And they resent their status as invisible women, going unnoticed in neighborhoods most often portrayed as havens for crime, drugs and welfare dependency, rarely for workaday striving.
"They can tell you about the black man who raped and killed someone," said Deborah Barton, a teacher's assistant who supports her two daughters on $12,000 a year, "but you don't hear about the single black mothers doing positive things to survive."
Ms. Barton's 10-year-old daughter, Serina, influenced by a barrage of negative images of blacks, at one point wished she was white. Her mother was appalled. "That day, I almost lost my mind." Serina recalled, "I wanted long hair and money and a Ford Taurus."
 
A Bitter Arithmetic
 For many of these women, working does not pay great dividends, at least in the short run. Ms. Barton has have about the same amount of money to spend as she would have if she were on welfare -- and even less when her expenses for transportation, clothing and child care are deducted. After taxes, she takes home about $10,000 a year. A family of three on welfare would get about about $9,300 in grants and food stamps, as well as free medical care through Medicaid.
Ms. Barton says working is worth it anyway. She loves her job. And she knows she must work and go to school on weekends to achieve her goal of becoming a full-fledged teacher.
Often, single mothers have had spells on welfare when their children were young. There is a pattern in their ability to resist the undertow of welfare dependency. They rely on their own determination, as well as supportive extended families, and often government housing programs. Usually, those who make it without welfare find ways to avoid some of the costs of housing or day care.
To save on rent, Wanda Boyd, a secretary who earns $19,499 a year, lives with her mother in Bedford-Stuyvesant, Brooklyn, and shares a bedroom with her two daughters. She works for the city's Department of Housing Preservation and Development, but she makes too much money to qualify for apartments it renovates for the very poor.
"I'm 32 years old with two children," she said. "I'd like to be on my own. But every apartment I look at, I can't afford."
Belkis Jimenez is able to support her two children on a bank clerk's $14,500-a-year salary, because she lives in a public housing project in Bushwick, Brooklyn, and pays only $250 a month for rent and utilities. Often the elevator is broken and Ms. Jimenez, who has asthma, must climb 18 flights of stairs with her baby in her arms. Even so, she counts herself lucky. About 200,000 families are waiting for a spot in the projects.
Maria Guadalupe, a $12,000-a-year secretary, can only afford to work because her mother lives two blocks away from her in the South Bronx and looks after her son Christopher, 4 years old. A friend she calls "Mama," who lives in her apartment building, keeps an eye on her two older sons, Carlos, 9, and Jorge, 7, and does not charge her anything. Ms. Guadalupe forbids the boys to play outside without supervision, because drug dealers work the sidewalk across the street.
"The big one complains a lot. He wants to go outside," she said. "I tell him, 'One day I'll hit the Lotto and buy a nice house.' "
Many working women attend school at night or on weekends. Education, they say, is the only path to a bigger income.. The price they pay is even less time for their children. Zianna Bennett leaves for work as a home health-care worker at about 7 A.M. and gets home from night school at 10 P.M. She's earning a college degree in social work.
After school, her sons, 7-year-old Neville and 4-year-old Michael, go to their grandmother's house. Each afternoon, Neville plugs in a tape his mother recorded the night before and hears her voice telling him to read a this book or do his homework.
For now, Ms. Bennett, who earns $14,000 a year, can only afford to live in a run-down, drug-infested apartment building in the South Bronx. The rent is $500 a month. She said she is the only woman in the apartment house with a job.
"I live there, but I'm not part of that culture," she said. "I am there as a necessity. The only way out is through education. There is no other legal way."
 
No Faith in Public Schools
 Deborah Barton also dreams of a better life for her daughters. She spends $3,000 of her $12,000 income as a teacher's assistant on tuition so Serina and her younger sister, Stephanie, 7, can attend the private school where she works, St. Paul Community Christian School, in Brooklyn.
The school's fence is topped by concertina wire in an effort to keep the violence of East New York at bay, but inside its classrooms her daughters are learning and thriving. Serina wants to go to Spelman College, in Atlanta.
The family lives in a public housing project in Brownsville, Brooklyn, where the rent is only $285 a month. The elevator reeks of urine, and gunfire erupts most nights. Ms. Barton has pulled the couch away from the windows so the girls will not get shot in the back of the head while they study or watch television. "Bullets don't have eyes," she said.
To survive on her pay, Ms. Barton accepts hand-me-downs with grace. And she has become a canny bargain-hunter who punctuates every household item she mentions with a price tag. She buys economy packs of chicken legs, $5.99. She worries when the girls get runs in their tights, $1.99. Ms. Barton has no winter coat, just a raincoat she bought three years ago at Strawberry, $79.
They live so close to the edge that Ms. Barton cannot afford to go to the doctor, even though she has health insurance. Under the rules of her policy, she must have at least $100 in charges to file a claim, but she cannot afford to pay the money out of pocket and wait to be reimbursed.
"When I'm sick, I doctor myself," she said.
While Ms. Barton has found a way to keep her children near her at all times, the challenge is more difficult for women with very young children. Good day care that is affordable to poorly paid working mothers is hard to come by, say researchers and city officials.
 
Employer's Cold Shoulder
 Milagros Reyes, who earns about $12,000 a year as a clerical worker in a garment factory, says she has had bad luck finding reliable sitters for Juan Carlos, now 19 months old. She has hired about half a dozen women, paying each just $40 a week. Some have quit for better jobs. Others have gone back to the Dominican Republic. She fired one woman after learning Carlito had been left with a stranger.
The two times she missed work to care for her son when he was sick, she received harsh warnings from her employer.
"You were absent 17.50 hours, or 12.50 percent of your maximum regular time," one warning said. The company "finds your absenteeism unacceptable. The company expects you to immediately correct this problem."
So Ms. Reyes goes to work even when Carlito is sick. Recently, she hired yet another sitter and raised the amount she pays to $50 a week. She hopes the new woman will do better. And, always, she watches nervously for signs that her spry, affectionate toddler -- smaller than 95 percent of children his age -- is filling out, growing taller, catching up with the other boys and girls.
