HEADLINE: BASKETBALL: FINAL FOUR PREVIEW;
At Cincinnati, Happy Days Are Here Again

BYLINE: By THOMAS GEORGE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CINCINNATI, March 30

 BODY:
Every way that Anthony Buford turned on the University of Cincinnati campus this afternoon, there was a handshake, a pat on the back, a good word. Buford, Cincinnati's versatile, play-making guard, was standing in the campus bookstore when another well-wisher offered his thoughts on what Cincinnati's inclusion among the Final Four meant to him and to others.
"I've got $500 seats, right on the floor," the fan and fellow student said. "I won't miss a minute of this. You guys have put Cincinnati basketball back on the map. This is going to be one big party in Minneapolis -- and here at home."
 
'Something Special' for Collection
 Buford smiled. 
 Later he explained: "No one can know just what this means to us. All of our lives, as kids playing basketball, you dream of playing in the Final Four. I've taped all of the recent ones. Now I'll have something special to add to the collection.
"All of my teammates and me know what it's like to have the eyes of the world fixed on you for all the wrong reasons. Just two years ago, I was transferring from Akron to here and I was getting hate mail from the Akron fans. I had to sit out all of last year yet keep my mind focused on basketball. I almost gave up. It seemed my chance would never come. And then to have all of this happen in my last year of college ball? We are living the dream."
Cincinnati's 29-4 season, which includes an 88-57 trouncing of Memphis State in the Midwest Regional Final on Sunday in Kansas City, Mo., earned the Bearcats their Final Four berth against Michigan this Saturday. It all serves as a throwback here to the glory years when players like Oscar Robertson helped Cincinnati almost 30 years ago make Final Four trips routine.
The long drought in between included a three-year probationary period from the N.C.A.A. and one-year elimination (in 1988) from post-season play for the school. Many of the Cincinnati players, however, have experienced their own sort of personal drought. Ten of the 11 players on the Bearcats' roster are transfer students, eight from junior colleges and two others from other Division I schools. There has long been an undefinable stigma placed on junior college players:
*Too dumb to make the big time.
*Not good enough to play with the big boys.
Cincinnati and its coach, Bob Huggins, may change much of that perception this week in Minneapolis.
"We recruit people, good people, whether they are junior college kids or not," Huggins said. "We get people who want to do the right things and want to go to class and make something of themselves. We fit their need and they fit ours. They know that we care about them first as people.
"Here, even in our own hometown, we're just beginning to educate people. We've got a guy who grew 6 inches while in junior college and worked hard to become the type of player he is today. We've got guys who transferred because of a change in coaches and in their old school's playing style. There are often myriad factors why a guy starts out in junior college, and they all don't have to be negative factors. What we're talking about here is opportunity."
The university thinks of itself in much the same manner. Its brouchures read: "A comprehensive university with a personal touch."
The university is situated three miles north of downtown Cincinnati and has an enrollment of about 36,000. The institution is noted for its engineering and music programs and for its distinction as the first in America (in 1906) to offer cooperative education for its students, the idea of classwork combined with practical on-the-job experience.
The players say that the university and the city are not too big or not too small but just right. The city's diverse economic base offers job opportunities after basketball, they said. And their coach, in his third season here, has offered the right touch. "He knew what it took," said Buford, "to get us where we are right now."
It took Huggins and the players fitting all the different pieces together to form a common bond. It took diving for loose balls -- "that's not optional," Huggins said -- and a relentless full-court pressure defense that kept producing turnovers. It took the humor of junior forward Terry Nelson, a standup comedian, to keep everyone loose and players like Nelson who do the dirty, inglorious work under the basket.
Huggins has worn the same, lucky brown suit for the last 10 games, all victories. He said he will later offer it for charity -- after the Final Four, of course.
Huggins arrived here in 1989 after five seasons as head coach at Akron and three more at Walsh College. In his 11 collegiate seasons as a head coach, his teams have won 20 or more games eight times. He said from the start that Cincinnati would eventually make the Final Four.
"I had 15 or so friends in the coaching business call me and tell me I was out of my mind, that I was putting too much pressure on myself," Huggins said. "What happens too often is that people are afraid to dream and are afraid of being disappointed.
"I'm honest and direct and sometimes I don't give the answers that people are looking for. I don't say to my players to do it because I said so. I say do it and here's why. They listen. I listen. My father was a coach and I grew up in a house always full of coaches. I learned there are so many different ways to skin a cat."
There are also many ways to make a "cat" lean and mean and full of desire and once again the apple of a city's eye. That is what Cincinnati's Bearcats are today.
"And no one can take that away from us," Buford said. "That is what our college experience has meant."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Bearcat Coach Bob Huggins giving bear hug to Anthony Buford after routing Memphis State. (Associated Press)

SECTION: Section C; Page 13; Column 5; Cultural Desk

LENGTH: 2024 words

HEADLINE: A Day to Demonstrate Affection for the Stars And Some Dismay

BYLINE: By BERNARD WEINRAUB, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: HOLLYWOOD, March 30

 BODY:
By 10 P.M., the movie stars and moguls had left the Academy Award ceremonies outside the Los Angeles Music Center to noisy demonstrators protesting Hollywood's depiction of homosexuals in a number of films.
But the demonstrators, who had threatened to disrupt the Oscar ceremonies, failed to do so. And members of the glamorous audience expressed relief mingled with sympathy. 
 As Academy Award winners trooped backstage to chat with reporters, several said that although the protests fizzled, they would not criticize the demonstrators.
"I think protest is good," said Jodie Foster, who won the award for best actress for her performance in "The Silence of the Lambs." "Protest is American. It's not against the law. Criticism is also good. Anything other than that falls into the category of undignified. But criticism is very important."
Bill Lauch, a New York architect who accepted the late Howard Ashman's award for best song, "Beauty and the Beast," said: "Howard and I shared a home and a life together, but this is so bittersweet. It is the first Academy Award won by a person with AIDS." He also expressed sympathy towards the protesters.
 
A Secular Holiday
 The protests by organizations like Queer Nation were the only somber note in a giddy day in Hollywood. By 2 P.M. the movie studios were empty, the nominees were all dressed up and drinking champagne, and the streets around the Los Angeles Music Center resembled a barricaded war zone in which the police, the paparazzi and demonstrators engaged in running skirmishes. By late afternoon, the freeways were gridlocked in pre-holiday-style traffic as everyone rushed home to turn on the television set.
"For most of my life I saw the Oscars as an occasion to stay home, laugh at the outfits, boo and applaud the winners, and groan at everyone stumbling through their speeches," said Ted Tally, who won the Oscar for best adapted screenplay for "The Silence of the Lambs." "Now, I'm one of them."
Callie Khouri, who won for best original screenplay for "Thelma and Louise," began drinking champagne with her family and friends in her Santa Monica home before she climbed into a limousine paid for by MGM. "You spend your whole life getting to the point where you can express yourself as a writer and try to deal with some truths and thinking of yourself as a kind of serious person," she said. "But the one thing I kept worrying about, obsessing about, is what shoes I'll wear under my Oscar dress."
The movie industry was virtually shut down this afternoon in preparation for the 64th annual Academy Awards. "It's sort of a holiday, the only religious rite we have in Hollywood," one studio executive said. "You have to observe it. It's like getting dressed up for church or temple -- except this is strictly a secular experience and Oscar is our golden idol."
By early evening, after an opening monologue by the master of ceremonies, Billy Crystal, that kidded everyone from Vice President Dan Quayle to the director Oliver Stone, the winners, gripping their golden idols, began trooping backstage to talk to reporters.
Jack Palance, who was named best supporting actor for "City Slickers," plainly indicated that the 39-year interval between his last nomination for "Shane" and his current award had left him in a bleak mood. His Hollywood career, he implied, has been turbulent.
"My future plans -- I don't have any future plans," he said tartly. Had the 68-year-old actor won the Oscar earlier in his career, he said, the award would have had an impact. Now, Mr. Palance implied, the award's effect on his career would be marginal.
On the other hand, an exuberant Mercedes Ruehl, who was named best supporting actress for "The Fisher King," discussed "the pleasure and validation" of the Oscar. Ms. Ruehl said she was planning to start rehearsing "Anthony and Cleopatra" at the Actors Theater of Louisville, and then repeat her Tony Award-winning role in the film of Neil Simon's "Lost in Yonkers."
Anthony Hopkins, who won the award for best actor for his portrayal of the cannibalistic psychiatrist Hannibal Lecter in "The Silence of the Lambs," was asked what he would be having for dinner. He broke into a laugh. "I'm going to the Governor's Ball, and I'll eat everyone's dinner," he said.
Jonathan Demme, who received the Oscar for best director for the film, said: "This is all so unbelievable. It's very hard to digest. No pun intended."
 
'It's All So Bizarre'
 The gray, drizzly day followed a single pattern, Hollywood style. Nominees worked a bit in the morning, went to barbers and hairdressers in the early afternoon, put on their outfit of outfits, met friends at home for champagne, piled into limousines and went to the Academy Awards at the Dorothy Chandler Pavilion. Later, many of them were to attend the academy's official party, the Governor's Ball, in a tent on the Music Center plaza, and then, with the night still young, make their way around town to numerous parties.
At midday the scene downtown at the Los Angeles Music Center was chaotic. Hundreds of police and security officers and firefighters surrounded the music center all day, partly because of demonstrations by gay protesters against what they termed Hollywood's unfair treatment of homosexuals. The demonstrators singled out films including "J. F. K." and "The Silence of the Lambs," two nominees for best picture, as well as "Basic Instinct," which opened this month, as films that dealt crudely and unfairly with gay characters.
About a dozen people, blowing whistles and carrying signs reading "Hollywood Stop Censoring Our True Queer Lives," demonstrated noisily outside the ceremonies. Several of them embraced and kissed, and others pasted small signs that said "Fag" on an enormous replica of the Oscar. Security officers quickly removed the tags as the crowd waiting for the movie stars to enter applauded and booed the demonstrators. Sizable numbers of Los Angeles police officers patrolled the area on horseback to drive back the demonstrators and arrested several of them.
The fans, many of whom arrived with sleeping bags on Sunday, surged forward, shrieking "Barbra!" "Kevin!" "Warren!" "Julia!" as the stars, some of whom wore red bows on their lapels symbolizing AIDS awareness, climbed out of limousines in mid-afternoon, waved like royalty and walked past the photographers and the screaming crowd into the marble-and-black-glass Dorothy Chandler Pavilion, the hall where the Academy of Motion Picture Arts and Sciences often holds its annual awards ceremony.
 
Metaphors and Money
 Some of the participants looked beyond the ceremony to more bankable matters, pointing out that winning an Oscar -- or even a nomination -- represents a considerable financial gain to a film, especially in overseas markets, as well as to the performers or technicians who triumph.
"It increases the fortunes of everyone involved," said Lynda Obst, one of the producers of "The Fisher King," which was nominated for five Oscars. "You're able to somehow break through the wall." Beyond the money factor, she said, the Oscars serve as a metaphor for Hollywood.
"There's the nature of this place," she said. "Sociopathically competitive, status-conscious, hierarchal, flavor-of-the-month-oriented and glamour-struck. Somehow, this is the brass ring at the end of the rainbow. You become crazy."
Robert Bookman, an agent at the Creative Artists Agency, put it another way. "Unless you're a nominee, when it's the most important night of you life, Oscar is work; it's not fun," he said. "It's not even glamorous. It's business. It has an enormous effect on people's careers in terms of what they're offered, what their price will be and how they're perceived by their peers. It's really a bottom-line evening."
 
A Little Less Flamboyance
 The most famous party, and probably the hottest ticket, was the one given annually by the agent Irving (Swifty) Lazar, and his wife, Mary, at which some of the biggest movie stars in town would be crammed, like rush-hour subway riders, into Spago, a restaurant above Sunset Strip in West Hollywood. The guest list included Elizabeth Taylor, Tom Cruise, Anjelica Huston, Barbra Streisand, Warren Beatty, Annette Bening, Faye Dunaway, Kevin Costner and just about everyone else who has ever appeared on the cover of People magazine.
"The party began 30 years ago in my home, and it just grew to what it is today," said Mr. Lazar, who has just turned 85. "We invite people because we like them, not because they're important. But, believe me, some people try to take advantage."
Mr. Lazar said a very well-known agent tried to have an equally well-known producer invited several years ago, but the Lazars refused. "Why? Because the guy is a bore," Mr. Lazar said. "The agent said the producer would give $50,000 to charity. I said no. He said the fellow will give $100,000 to charity. I said no. Can you believe it?"
Movie executives spent the weekend and today wining and dining the creators and stars of their Oscar-nominated films at parties in their homes in Bel Air and Beverly Hills. Mark Canton, the chairman of Columbia Pictures, said he was convinced that even in Hollywood, which takes its parties very seriously, the nation's sluggish economy had led to a more low-key Oscar event than usual. Mr. Canton had a party on Saturday night at his home in Holmby Hills for, among others, Barbra Streisand, Nick Nolte and Kate Nelligan of "The Prince of Tides," as well as John Singleton, who was nominated for best director and best screenplay for "Boyz N the Hood."
Despite the glamour and glitz today, Mr. Canton observed, "It's a sobering time now. There's a little bit less flamboyance. People are a little less concerned about the parties."
But many in Hollywood, like elsewhere, simply stayed home and watched the show -- with a difference.
"It's such a company town, such a tight-knit little world, and this is the one really officially sanctioned event," said Robert Cort, the president of Interscope Communications, a film and television production company. Mr. Cort had arranged to watch the show at his Bel Air home with his wife, Rosalie Swedlin, a producer, and several friends. "You tend to know a lot of people who are sitting in the audience, and it's kind of like seeing old friends -- or enemies."
"It's sort of like the end of a long political campaign," he added. "And yet it's Hollywood. You have the advantage here of being part of something -- and yet you know it matters to the rest of the world, too. Everyone cares about the Oscars out there. So it's a pretty heady mixture for everyone in Hollywood, where narcissism runs amok anyway."

HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
Prepare for Lockout In Spring of '93, Fehr Is Warning Players

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SCOTTSDALE, Ariz., March 30

 BODY:
For four weeks, Donald Fehr has been traveling through Florida and Arizona telling the members of the union he heads to expect their employers to employ a two-stage strategy in the next 12 months:
*Reopen the collective bargaining agreement in December. 
 *Lock out the players from spring training next February.
"It's almost a here-we-go-again thing," Fehr said after one of his recent spring meetings with players. "You don't take it as a certainty because a lot can happen between now and then, but at the moment, I assume they will reopen and I assume they will lock out. They have clearly left the impression with the players that they intend to lock out. If they do, it'll be a long one so you have to prepare for it. You plan for the worst-case scenario."
In planning for that scenario, the Major League Baseball Players Association is in the process of accumulating a fund that a year from now is expected to total between $125 million and $140 million. The money, which would be used in the event of an extended work stoppage, comes from licensing fees that the union is holding instead of distributing to the players.
 
'Reopening Is an Option'
 But concern about a labor confrontation is premature, owners say. "We're a long way from making any of those decisions," said Bud Selig of the Milwaukee Brewers, chairman of the board of directors of the Player Relations Committee, the owners' labor body.
The labor agreement negotiated during a spring lockout in 1990 runs to the end of 1993, but either side can reopen it by giving the other side notice by Dec. 11, 1992. If the contract is reopened, either side would be free to "engage in concerted action."
"Obviously, reopening is an option we have and we'd be silly not to consider the option," said Jerry Reinsdorf of the Chicago White Sox, a member of the Player Relations Committee board.
Reinsdorf, whom the players view as one of the more militant owners, also acknowledged that if the owners did reopen and the two sides could not reach agreement, a lockout could loom.
 
Not Ruling Out Lockout
 "To me," Reinsdorf said, "all a lockout means is there's a work stoppage and the owners have decided the timing. A strike is where the players decide the timing. To me, if we can't reach agreement and we reach an impasse, a lockout is something that would have to be considered."
The owners used that strategy in 1990, and the dispute, primarily over eligibility for salary arbitration, wasn't resolved until March 18. Commissioner Fay Vincent was instrumental in the resolution, but some owners didn't appreciate his involvement, saying that he was too conciliatory. Owners involved in labor relations have made it clear that Richard Ravitch, new president of the Player Relations Committee and a former New York City mayoral candidate, will be management's negotiator.
"The commissioner is not a member of the board of directors of the P.R.C.," said Reinsdorf. "In 1981, Bowie Kuhn did not participate. In '85 and '90, the commissioners did. Technically they probably shouldn't have."
Two years ago, Selig said, "there were more participants, including myself, than there should have been."
"Dick Ravitch is going to do the negotiations for us," Selig said.
 
Ravitch Is the Negotiator
 Asked about the view that Vincent was too involved in 1990, Selig said: "I don't want to comment on that. Everybody is comfortable that Dick is the negotiator."
Everybody perhaps except the players. They viewed Vincent's involvement as a positive step in the quagmire that passes for baseball's labor relations and would like to have his input again in the next negotiations.
The commissioner said he was aware of the Player Relations Committee's position on negotiations and said Ravitch should do the job he was hired to do.
"I didn't think it was my role to be involved in the last negotiation," Vincent said. "The only reason I got involved was nothing was being done."
Whether or not the Player Relations Committee approves, the commissioner could step into negotiations under the "best interests of baseball" clause in the major league agreement.
 
Keeping Commissioner Out?
 There is also the perception in some baseball circles that the joint economic study committee created in the last negotiations is another example of how the owners want to keep the commissioner away from player relations.
The study committee, composed of Fehr, Selig and two outside professionals selected by each side, met here for more than 20 hours Friday, Saturday and Sunday. Vincent had been here visiting spring training camps but was not asked to attend any of the sessions.
"It means nothing, zero," Selig said of Vincent's absence.
Vincent said, "I don't think my not going is a big deal."
Fehr, meanwhile, prepared to wrap up his spring sessions, having two teams left after meeting with Seattle players today. His message remained the same: Be prepared for a reopener and a lockout.
"We hear references to a conflagration coming, about Armageddon on the horizon," he said in an interview. "But the rumor mill seems to have slowed down since I first made comments in Florida, which is another reason I feel I'm right."

HEADLINE: Soil Degradation Study: There Goes the Farm

 BODY:
MORE than three billion acres of land, amounting to 11 percent of the world's vegetated surface area, have become seriously degraded since World War II, an environmental research group has reported.
The soils, mostly in Asia and Africa, have lost much of their capacity to produce crops or lumber or support livestock, the group, the World Resources Institute in Washington, said in a report last week. In total, they represent an area the size of China and India together. 
 "Soil that you can't produce crops on anymore is real serious business," said Robert Livernash, senior editor of the report. "This is an alarm bell."
 
Survey of Scientists
 The study by the World Resources Institute drew on data from more than 250 soil scientists about levels of erosion, salinization, and physical destruction in the world's soils. Major causes of damage include farming, overgrazing and deforestation, the study said.
More than a third of the degraded area is in Asia, where severe water erosion has destroyed soils in western India, the Himalayas, Southeast Asia and China, and wind erosion threatens western China and Mongolia.
Nearly half of Asia's damaged soils were degraded by deforestation and a quarter by overgrazing and most of the rest by agriculture.
When fields become unproductive, farmers often abandon them for newly cleared lands, making soil degradation a major contributor to loss of forests worldwide.
"I rank this as the No. 1 environmental problem in the world," said Dr. David Pimentel, professor of entomology and agricultural sciences at Cornell University.
"Ninety-eight percent of the world's food for people comes from the land as opposed to the sea or rivers or all the rest," he said. "When we degrade this land we get deforestation. We get losses in biodiversity. We get global warming."
 
Very Slow Process
 Dr. Pimentel said the problem of soil degradation received too little attention. "Even farmers don't appreciate the degradation that is occurring because it occurs so slowly," he said.
A farmer can lose one twenty-fifth of an inch of soil in one good rain storm at night, which is more than five tons of topsoil on a single acre.
"The next morning a farmer wouldn't notice that," Dr. Pimentel said. "But about 20 years after this you'd have lost one inch of topsoil, which takes 500 years to reform."
Though soil quality has degraded since 1945, agricultural productivity has risen because of better crop varieties, irrigation, fertilizers and pesticides. But productivity could have been higher had soil quality been maintained.
For instance, farms in the hilly Palouse area of eastern Washington State are estimated to have lost more than 500 pounds of winter wheat per acre because heavy erosion has impoverished their soils.
 
No Hope for Some Lands
 Most of the three billion damaged acres can be restored to full productivity only with major and often costly improvements. More than 22 million of those acres have had their biotic functions fully destroyed so that no amount of effort will restore them.
While many farmers own the land they work and are committed to harvesting from it for the long term, it is often not in their economic interest to invest in conservation or restoration.
In fact, on very fertile lands, an individual farmer may not benefit at all from conservation practices. The World Resources Institute report suggests that it is therefore up to governments to tackle the issue.
"We already know a lot of different ways to solve these problems," Mr. Livernash said, "but it's really a question of having the money and the political will to do it. There are lots of techniques to restore degraded lands or to reduce or prevent soil erosion."
Study results were reported in "World Resources 1992-93," published by the World Resources Institute, P.O. Box 4852, Hampden Station, Baltimore, Md. 21211.

HEADLINE: Novel Strategy Puts People At Heart of Texas Preserve

BYLINE: By WILLIAM K. STEVENS

DATELINE: SAN ANTONIO

 BODY:
THE ecological heart of Texas consists not of desert and dust, but of deep, spectacular wooded canyons where eagles soar and rare songbirds nest, and thousands of crystalline springs bubble from the forested hillsides. Beneath the surface lie vast, honeycombed formations of limestone where strange creatures whose kind have not seen the light of day for millennia live in subterranean rivers, caves and flooded crevices.
It is the Texas hill country, and a grand experiment in what might be called the New Conservation is under way to save its distinctive assemblages of flora and fauna. 
 In an area almost twice as large as New Jersey where 2.5 million Texans live, including the cities of San Antonio and Austin, the Nature Conservancy is brokering a pioneering effort involving dozens of public agencies and private interests. The goal of the Conservancy, a nonprofit organization that operates the world's largest system of private nature preserves, is to help the economy and ecology of the hill country flourish together.
The principle, a sharp departure from traditional conservation, is to focus on the entire inhabited landscape, not just individual species or even habitats. That requires humans, too, to be treated as part of the ecosystem.
Conservationists have historically ignored and excluded humans as somehow separate from and antagonistic to the rest of nature. They have tried to set aside nature preserves, keep people out and let nature take its course, reckoning that this was enough. The results have been less than satisfactory. Natural ecosystems have become increasingly fragmented, and even in the preserves native species have often been pushed toward extinction by plants and animals introduced from elsewhere.
"For too long we have behaved as if conservation could be achieved merely by separating nature from people, with detrimental effects on both," said Dr. Robert E. Jenkins Jr., the Conservancy's vice president for science.
Consequently, conservationists increasingly seek to integrate human activity with natural ecosystems. The plan in the case of the Texas hill country is to stitch together a quilt of healthy natural habitat and settled areas, saving as many species as possible while preserving economic vitality.
In attempting to execute the plan, the Conservancy is working with local, state and Federal authorities, environmentalists and landowners in a three-pronged strategy:
*Setting aside a few highly protected and carefully managed "core" reserves for native fauna and flora, in addition to existing park lands.
*Surrounding the core reserves with ecologically friendly buffer zones. These would consist, for instance, of ranches that maintain wild habitat for tourists as well as undeveloped areas of military installations.
*Creating ecologically healthy islands of wildlife habitat on the outskirts of cities while allowing development to proceed around them.
The strategy assumes that when conservationists can plan for an entire ecological region, preserving the most essential tracts of land, it becomes less necessary to protect every scrap of habitat. The hope is that fewer knock-down fights will develop between environmentalists and economic interests, and that fewer wild species will wind up on the endangered list.
The Nature Conservancy and its allies here are not insisting that "every little-bitty bug, every little-bitty tree" be protected, said Beyrl Armstrong, the manager of a 9,300-acre guest ranch west of here where an extensive habitat protection program is under way. This moderate path, he said, opens a welcome middle road between "the screamers on the far right, who say this is my damn land and I can do anything I want with it, and the screamers on the far left, who chain themselves around every tree they come up on."
Experimenters in the New Conservation are still feeling their way, both here and in other parts of the country, and how their efforts will turn out remains to be seen. Not least in Texas, with its highly developed sense of bow-necked, don't-tread-on-me landowner independence, the potential for conflict remains high in the best of circumstances. The fate of some aspects of the hill country project is touch and go.
But a general shift in philosophy is clearly taking hold among conservationists, and in the hill country the challenge is more than matched by the grandeur of the opportunity.
 
Stair Steps
The Ecosystem And the Threat
 The hill country's most distinctive feature is the Balcones Escarpment, a 200-mile-long crescent of limestone stair steps cross-cut by rivers and streams that have carved out the region's characteristic ravines and canyons. Rainwater cascading off the stair steps runs into millions of tiny openings in the limestone and collects underground in a reservoir, one of the biggest and purest in the country.
In the crevices of the aquifer dwell many species of insects, crustaceans, salamanders and small fish, many of them unpigmented and blind. The aquifer, the region's sole source of drinking water, depends for its quality on the integrity of the natural ecosystem above, which buffers it from pollution.
 
158 Species of Birds
 Oak and Ashe juniper, locally called cedar, set the vegetational tone of the region. Its green hills, canyons and tablelands are a biological crossroads for species from grasslands, deciduous forests and deserts. The hill country is host to 17 major plant communities, each with its special assemblage of animals and microorganisms.
One measure of the region's biological variety is the 158 species of birds that have been sighted on the Prade Ranch, managed by Mr. Armstrong, in the heart of the region. On a day earlier this month, three golden eagles wheeled in the sky above a mist-shrouded ridge, while an endangered golden-cheeked warbler, newly arrived after migrating from Central America, appeared in response to Mr. Armstrong's call. "My enchilada from Guatemala," Mr. Armstrong called the bird.
Both the warbler, which nests exclusively in Texas, and the black-capped vireo, also endangered, depend on the hill country habitat.
Over all, some 70 species of hill country plants and animals have been identified as rare or threatened. They include plants like Hellers' false gromwell, the broadpod rushpea and the Comal snakewood, and creatures like the Blanco blind salamander, the Bee Creek Cave harvestman, and the ghost-faced bat.
So far, the hill country still supports most of the species that lived there before Europeans settled the country, and only a tiny fraction of the total are under threat. While the raising of sheep and goats has changed the landscape, it has been spared intensive development.
Until now. The population of the San Antonio and Austin areas is booming, and the beauty of the hill country is an increasingly strong magnet for urban expansion, retirees and tourists. The population of the 26 counties that make up the conservation project grew by 27 percent from 1980 to 1990.
 
Devil's River
Core Preserves And Buffers
 In the hill country, as in 11 other large, diverse geographical areas in the United States and Latin America, the Nature Conservancy has delineated a landscape-wide "bioreserve" planned to include as many of the region's species as possible.
The 18,650-square-mile Texas bioreserve is to contain four to six core preserves. The first of which, through which the Devil's River runs, was purchased by the Conservancy last fall. In the last 40 years, the Conservancy has bought more than 5.5 million acres of ecologically important land in the United States and abroad, reselling 60 percent of its holdings to governments. It operates some 1,300 private nature preserves in the United States and abroad.
The Devil's River core preserve is a spectacular 19,000-acre tract in the relatively dry western part of the hill country. The river's source is the clear springs that flow from towering limestone formations streaked in colors of gray and rust. The centerpiece of the reserve is a lush but ecologically delicate oasis with a cataract called Dolan Falls. Cormorants and great blue herons fly above. Sycamores and oaks line the banks. Killdeer run along the flats. Spotted squirrels batten on acorns. Tiny rare fish swim in the crystal water.
 
Ranches as Buffer Zones
 "This is so fragile," said Jeff Weigel, the Texas Nature Conservancy's director of stewardship. "This is what takes the highest level of protection. It couldn't take a lot of human use."
But neither the Conservancy nor any other group will ever be able to buy up entire landscapes. So the group is trying to work with landowners to promote land use that both turns a profit and is compatible with the ecosystem's health. These tracts would serve as buffers for core preserves. The prospects appear good in the hill country, since much of it is taken up by ranches rich in habitat, many of whose owners have long been concerned with preserving wildlife.
The Conservancy has identified several ranches to serve as examples that other landowners might be persuaded to follow. One is the Prade Ranch, whose 9,300 acres are a biological cornucopia. There, Mr. Armstrong tends a going enterprise based on limited cattle ranching; hunting, which prevents an exploding deer population from wreaking ecological havoc; the hosting of conferences and meetings, and nature study, especially bird watching.
 
Binocular-Toting Tourists
 "When I first came here I looked around and said to myself, 'I could keep this place full of binocular-toting people from New York all day long,' " he said. "People will pay big money to come and see golden-cheeked warblers. I got 'em, and I got more than anybody else, and I know where they are, and I know how to talk to 'em."
Making a go of the venture requires Mr. Armstrong to preserve intact the ecosystem on which it depends, and to manage it carefully. Marrying economics and conservation "is going to have to happen more and more and more," he said, because it is becoming evident that traditional ranching activities are not enough to sustain the high costs of maintaining a ranch.
But there is some distance to go in convincing ranchers to embrace conservation values. Some, Mr. Armstrong said, "were raised on the idea that the Lord gave you dominion over all, and that it was not only your right but your responsibility to go out there and make America safe for domesticated animals and to eradicate anything wild."
 
Austin
Wildlife Habitat In an Urban Area
 The struggle between landowners' prerogatives and conservation values erupted with a vengeance four years ago in the Austin area, and the result has been the birth of a pioneering conservation program for the burgeoning metropolitan area whose progress is being closely watched around the country.
This is the front line of the hill country project, the place where urban development and nature meet head on. The issue was forced in 1988 when developers came into conflict with the Endangered Species Act, under which the golden-cheeked warbler, the black-capped vireo and five species of endangered bugs living in the Austin area are protected. Under the law, developers must comply with Federal regulations protecting the species.
Fearing legal chaos and economic paralysis if each landowner negotiated separately with the Government, developers, environmentalists and local agencies invited the Nature Conservancy to mediate in an attempt to come up with a coordinated, county-wide plan that would allow development to proceed while protecting habitat. Such consolidations are permitted under the Endangered Species Act.
 
60,000 Acres Near Austin
 The result is a conservation plan for the Austin area, developed over the last three years by a committee composed of parties on all sides of the issue. The plan aims to carve out 60,000 acres of the least fragmented habitat within the metropolitan area and establish it as a system of preserves. "Incidental takings" of habitat by developers are to be allowed, said David Braun, the director of the Texas Nature Conservancy. But the most important chunks of habitat, many of them contiguous, will be set aside as "a viable preserve system around which development can occur," he said.
The land is being assembled in a variety of ways. The Federal Government is acquiring half the required acreage. The Conservancy has been taking options on other parcels. Some of the land will be paid for by local governments. Some is being purchased and donated by developers with the understanding that they will be allowed to pursue, uncontested, developments they plan elsewhere in the local area.
The locally financed portion of the plan is poised to go into effect if a bond issue to finance the City of Austin's share of the land acquisition, tentatively set for this August, passes. But there is much contention. While there is considerable support for the plan, some taxpayers balk at putting up the money. Some landowners argue that if anyone should pick up the tab, the public should. The general mood in Austin, a liberal university town, favors the plan, "but the details could kill it" said the Mayor, Bruce Todd, who is also chairman of the project's executive committee. "It can pass, but not without some work."
As for the overall hill country bioreserve project, "they've got a good idea," said Dan Byfield, a lobbyist for the Texas Farm Bureau, which opposes what it sees as the Endangered Species Act's infringement of property rights. The Conservancy "got off on the wrong foot" with landowners by announcing the project without without consulting ranchers, he said. And "once you put that line on a map," he said, "you have inadvertently put somebody in harm's way of the Endangered Species Act. You've said that land is environmentally sensitive."
Nevertheless, he said, "what the Nature Conservancy is doing is probably a concept we've all got to turn toward."

Chart: "An Ecosystem Viewed as a Whole"
A new approach seeks to protect the Texas hill country as an entire inhabited landscape, rather than focusing on individual species and habitats. It combines three elements in an attempt to integrate human activity with nature.
 
1. The wilderness
 Four to six core reserves are to be established to protect and manage natural habitat. The first, north of Del Rio, was bought last fall by the Nature Conservancy.
 
2. Eco-ranching
 Ranchers with vast acreage are being encouraged to bring ecological perspectives to their money-making plans. Some cater to nature study, bird watching and similar activities.
 
3. Habitats near cities
 Governments, environmentalists and landowners in the Austin area are attempting to cobble together healthy islands of wildlife habitat while allowing development to proceed around them.

HEADLINE: Q&A

BYLINE: By C. CLAIBORNE RAY

 BODY:

The Biggest Seed
 Q. Which plant has the largest seed?
A. A tall palm tree called Lodoicea maldivicia, which grows only in the Seychelles in the Indian Ocean, produces the largest single seed known in the plant kingdom, said Dr. Andrew Henderson, a botanist and research associate at the Institute of Economic Botany at the New York Botanical Garden in the Bronx. 
 The two-lobed seed can be 20 inches long. It grows in huge fruits that look something like two coconuts joined together in an almost heart-shaped arrangement, Dr. Henderson said.
To the European sailors who first saw the seeds floating in the ocean, the shape was suggestive of female buttocks. To the tourists who eventually followed, they looked like wonderful souvenirs, and the trees are threatened in the wild because local residents collect the seeds to sell, Dr. Henderson said.
The seeds are also much sought after by amateur palm growers, but they are not easy to cultivate, he said. They have a very slow germination time of several years. Eventually a shoot grows out of the seed and then goes underground.
 
Blood Trouble
 Q. What is vasculitis and what causes it?
A. Vasculitis is a general term for inflammation of blood vessels, according to the American Medical Association. The inflammation usually damages the lining of the vessels, so that they become constricted or blocked. This, in turn, leads to damage and sometimes destruction of the tissues that are normally supplied with oxygen carried by the blood.
In most cases, vasculitis is thought to be caused by bodies circulating in the blood called immune complexes. These are clumps of foreign materials called antigens that are bound to antibodies formed in response to the antigens. Normally the clumps are destroyed by white blood cells called phagocytes, but sometimes they settle in the walls of blood vessels and cause severe inflammation.
Vasculitis is the basic problem in a number of ailments with other names. It can sometimes arise as a secondary problem from infections like hepatitis B, streptococcus or tuberculosis and can also result from oversensitivity to injections of an antiserum of animal origin, like the antirabies serum obtained from horses. However, in many cases the cause is unknown.

HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
Mets Urged To Break Silence

BYLINE: By JOE SEXTON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PORT ST. LUCIE, Fla., March 30

 BODY:
Silence continues to reign in the Mets' clubhouse. Patience, though, may be wearing thin in the front office.
Al Harazin, the team's general manager, said today that he had been working behind the scenes over the last 24 hours to achieve an end to a boycott of the news media being enforced by the players. 
 "Everybody is trying to do what they can, all trying to deal with a sensitive situation," Harazin said. "It would be inappropriate for me to comment further."
Harazin's announcement came against the backdrop of the final week of spring training. The Mets, apparently able to divorce themselves from the disturbing distractions that have plagued them for weeks, have played remarkable baseball recently.
Today, Howard Johnson slammed a pair of home runs, Dwight Gooden pitched another healthy six innings and the Mets won their 12th exhibition game in the last 14. The heart of the lineup has produced dozens of runs, the defense has tightened, and the pitching has Mel Stottlemyre talking about it being superior to the 1988 staff.
It would thus seem to make for a perfect prelude to the regular season for the Mets. They spent millions of dollars and countless hours in the off season overhauling the club, and appear prepared to make good.
But Harazin said that tickets sales for 1992 have been only marginally better than last season. And the fact that the players are not talking is not helping matters.
"Under ordinary circumstances, I'd be more disappointed, but given the nature of the economy in New York, I feel kind of good about it," Harazin said of the ticket sales. "We're not any more insulated from the economy than anyone else."
Harazin confirmed that the Mets and the commissioner's office have been talking about the players' news media boycott, and that the talk would go on. "We're hopeful we can make progress this week," Harazin said.
 
Six Shutout Innings
 The progress on the field for the Mets continued to be accelerated and even exhilarating. Gooden, in his third official start of the spring, worked six shutout innings, his longest, most impressive stint of the spring. Struggling once more for complete control of his repertory, Gooden struck out five, with high fastballs and one 3-2 curve.
"It's amazing when you look up and see the numbers on the scoreboard," said Manager Jeff Torborg. "And when he today reached back, he had pop."
Gooden, of course, had nothing to say afterward. Johnson, who has now driven in 21 runs this spring, wouldn't speak. Junior Noboa, the utility player who was smiling at his strong spring days ago, got another pair of hits, but was prohibited from speaking about what Torborg has termed "the surprise of the spring."
 
INSIDE PITCH
 The Mets, convinced that TERRY BROSS would never be a factor in their plans, Monday traded the right-handed reliever to the Padres for CRAIG BULLOCK, a 20-year-old third-base prospect. "I feel an obligation to try and move a young player once it's clear they aren't going to be with us at the major league level," GERRY HUNSICKER, the vice president for baseball operations for the Mets, said of Bross. "Terry, I believe, can pitch in the major leagues, but it wasn't going to be with us." Bullock, playing in Class A last season, hit .234 with 8 homers and 50 runs batted in. "He's a big, strapping kid, another prospect to add to our organization," said Hunsicker.

GRAPHIC: Photo: The Mets' Howard Johnson, who had two home runs and five runs batted in, being congratulated in dugout. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: U.S. to Delay Rerouting Of Flights

BYLINE: By JOSEPH F. SULLIVAN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BERNARDSVILLE, N.J., March 30

 BODY:
Residents of northern New Jersey and Staten Island who have been fighting to change air-traffic patterns near the region's airports to reduce noise will not see any route changes for at least a year, the Federal Aviation Administration says.
Norbert A. Owens, the agency's Deputy Associate Administrator for Air Traffic, said at a hearing of a Congressional subcommittee today that an environmental impact study scheduled for completion in May 1991 would not be finished until the end of 1992. 
 And that study, even if it supports moving takeoff and landing patterns over the ocean, will not be the final step. Mr. Owens said further environmental studies would probably be required to assess the impact of new routes on areas beneath them.
"That's the most important thing I've heard today," said Representative Richard Zimmer, a Republican of Delaware Township, who conducted the hearing. He said the F.A.A. had failed to conduct an environmental study before it changed all the routes in the Northeast in 1987. Now, Mr. Zimmer said, the agency appears to be using the need for such studies to delay making changes that would respond to noise complaints by residents of New York and New Jersey.
Mr. Zimmer asked Mr. Owens how the agency could have failed to anticipate the angry reaction from people living below the new flight patterns. The Federal official said the agency was required to assess noise problems only when planes are at 3,000 feet or below, when they are close to airports. He said the agency made some adjustments in 1989, "albeit after the fact," in response to complaints from people living farther from the airports.
Mr. Owens agreed that the agency "did not assess the level of noise complaints correctly," and added, "but we are listening now." That statement brought groans from many of the 100 people who crowded into a small meeting room at the Bernardsville Municipal Building.
Mr. Zimmer said the 1989 changes simply shifted the noise problems from the air over Cranford to Scotch Plains. "The agency has been basically unresponsive," he said. "For a long time, agency officials took the position that the noise problems were simply figments of residents' imaginations."
Mr. Zimmer is sponsoring legislation to force the Federal agency to use ocean routes where feasible within 18 months. In cases where these routes could not be used, the agency would have to return to routes in use before 1987. Mr. Owens said the 1987 changes were intended to meet a rapid increase in air traffic in the region and that without them, the economies in the area would have suffered.
 
U.S. Threat to Port Authority
 The hearing panel, which included Representatives Susan Molinari, Republican of Staten Island, and Dean A. Gallo, Republican of Parsippany, also questioned the aviation administration about its threat to withold approval of a plan by the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey to assess a $3 passenger tax on airline travelers to help pay for a $2 billion plan to build rail links to Newark, Kennedy and La Guardia Airports.
The F.A.A. opposes a plan by the Port Authority to accelerate the Federal agency's schedule for eliminating noisy aircraft. The Federal Airport Noise and Capacity Act of 1990 requires airlines to phase out or upgrade their noisiest aircraft over the next 10 years. The Port Authority is scheduled to vote this summer on the plan to move up that schedule.
Louise E. Maillet, director of the Office of Environment and Energy for the F.A.A., said the Port Authority had failed to provide a detailed cost and benefits analysis of its plan. She said a computer model of the entire national air traffic system indicated that the authority's plan could cost an additional $1.2 billion across the system.
Both Mr. Zimmer and Jerry Fitzgerald, Deputy Director of Aviation for the Port Authority, said they doubted the Federal agency's cost estimate. "We'd like to get a look at their computer model," Mr. Fitzgerald said.
Guy V. Molinari, the Staten Island Borough President, played a tape of an outdoor speech he made that was drowned out by aircraft noise. "I sympathize with the people in New Jersey," he said. "But in all honesty, our aircraft noise problems are much worse."

HEADLINE: Census Shows Connecticut Is Richest State

BYLINE: By ANDREW L. YARROW, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: STAMFORD, Conn., March 30

 BODY:
Connecticut became America's wealthiest state during the 1980's, as its residents' incomes soared and its poverty rate declined, according to newly released 1990 Census Bureau data.
But there were also signs of increasing economic duress, evident in the rising costs of housing and the number of manufacturing jobs lost, even though the information was collected before the current recession. 
 In many ways, the Census report offers a portrait of a prosperous state just as it was beginning to descend into the economic maelstrom of the last two years. Unemployment, for example, rose from 5.4 percent when the Census collected its data to 7.5 percent in January, and the Connecticut labor force has continued to contract.
The report, the third detailed state report to be released by the Census Bureau, is based on the long-form Census questionnaires sent to one in six households.
The Census also indicated that Connecticut had a fourfold increase in the number of foreign-born residents during the 1980's and that the ranks of working women continued their generation-long growth.
"Connecticut passed Alaska in the 1980's to become the state with the highest per capita income," said James Palma Jr., the planning analyst supervisor with the Connecticut State Data Center, which analyzes Census information for state and local governments. The Census first reported that Connecticut had the nation's highest per capita income in 1986, as the Alaskan oil boom was fading.
The state's median household income -- half the state's households are above this point and half below -- increased 24 percent after inflation, to $41,721 in 1989 from $33,649 in 1979. Per capita income went up even more sharply, to $20,189, from $14,264.
The number of Connecticut residents below the official poverty level dropped from 8.0 percent in 1979 to 6.8 percent in 1989. The poverty level, which varies by household size and other factors, currently is defined as an annual income of $5,947 for an individual or $9,981 for a family with two adults and one child. The percentage of people over 65 years old who were below the poverty level went down from 8.7 percent to 7.2 percent, and the poverty rate for children under 18 went from 11.4 percent to 10.4 percent.
The general picture of growing prosperity was dampened by sharp increases in housing costs. For homeowners with a mortgage, the median monthly cost rose 66.3 percent after inflation, to $1,096. Nearly one-fourth of homeowners spent at least 30 percent of their income on housing, compared with less than one-fifth spending that proportion in 1980. Among renters, 40.5 percent had monthly housing costs of at least 30 percent of their income.
 
Housing Costs 'a Flag'
 "When housing costs are more than 30 percent, that's a flag," Mr. Palma said. "It either means that more people are choosing to spend more of their money on housing or they have no choice. And it's a fair assumption that it's not by choice."
Since 1988, Connecticut real estate prices have fallen by about 20 percent, with half of that decline occurring since the Census, said Nicholas S. Perna, the chief economist for Connecticut National Bank.
He noted that one possible bright spot since the Census data were collected was that "affordability has improved, because house prices and interest rates are down, while incomes rose slightly in 1991."
Although the state's total population grew by 5.8 percent, to 3,287,116, Connecticut's labor force grew more than twice as much, increasing 15 percent, to 1,804,457. The percentage of women in the work force shot up from 53.6 percent in 1980 to 60.9 percent in 1990, while the percentage of men in the work force dipped from 78.3 percent to 77.8 percent.
The number of manufacturing jobs, until recently the backbone of the state's economy, declined to 346,551, from 458,816. By January of this year, the number was 318,200, according to Kathy Hurley, a spokeswoman for the State Labor Department.
 
A Growth of Immigrants
 Conversely, employment in retail trade grew from 215,873 to 260,829, and the number of workers in finance, insurance and real estate increased from 118,702 to 176,421.
In the last year, however, employment in retail trade dropped by 6 percent, Mr. Perna said, and the other fields also showed decreases.
The proportion of Federal, state and local government employees edged slightly downward to about one in eight Connecticut workers; the 1990 total was 217,327, compared with 207,129 a decade earlier.
"We've lost close to 10 percent of our jobs since the peak in late 1988 or early 1989," Mr. Perna said.
 
How the Data Are Gathered
 The Census Bureau's report on Connecticut is the third of the agency's reports on the 50 states and the District of Columbia to be issued. The first state report issued was on Vermont, and the second on Montana.
Reports on New York and New Jersey are "tentatively to be released the week of April 13," said Sharon Massie, an information assistant with the Census. Detailed information for the entire nation is to be released after all state reports are issued.
The data are based on the long-form questionnaire that was distributed in 1990 to one in six, or 17.7 million, housing units throughout the United States.
Among the topics included in the survey are population data on education, citizenship, ancestry, the language spoken at home, veteran status, disability, industry of employment, labor force participation, occupation, income and poverty status.

HEADLINE: POLITICAL MEMO;
Spotlight on Bronx: Heat's on Dinkins

BYLINE: By TODD S. PURDUM

 BODY:
Today, Mayor David N. Dinkins will move his office to that hoary national symbol of urban problems, the Bronx. But when the Democratic Presidential contenders debate there tonight with the eyes of the nation upon them, their host will be not the Mayor, but Borough President Fernando Ferrer.
After striving for months to spotlight urban problems -- and winning little public attention -- the Mayor found himself last week facing the magic media opportunity of the primary, but one-upped by Mr. Ferrer. The Borough President long ago arranged the televised debate at 7 P.M. at Herbert H. Lehman College, to be shown on WABC-TV and C-Span. 
 So the Mayor, whose weeklong sojourn to the Bronx is part of a continuing public relations campaign to be seen as more responsive to the concerns of ordinary New Yorkers, will end up going back home to ask the candidates about their urban policies at a hastily arranged forum with other mayors at Gracie Mansion this afternoon.
That dichotomy is a metaphor for Mr. Dinkins's political headache these days: How to make the case that Washington's neglect has contributed to New York's problems, and how to persuade his fellow New Yorkers that he can help solve those problems. In other words, how to think globally and act locally.
Of course, if Mr. Ferrer and the Mayor were getting along better, they might have worked out a joint event in the only New York borough on the mainland United States. But the two have been bickering for months, most recently over Mr. Ferrer's failure to include a Hispanic journalist on the panel for tonight's debate. It will be enough if they get along sharing office space in the Bronx County Building, the setting of "Bonfire of the Vanities."
"I am always a good host," Mr. Ferrer said yesterday. "Anyone who comes to my office gets a warm welcome and a cup of coffee. As we say, 'Mi casa es su casa.' "
But he could not resist adding, "Cordially yours," which is how he signed a terse letter to the Mayor last week after Mr. Dinkins complained that the Borough President had not included any Hispanic reporters on his panel of questioners tonight.
The friction spells trouble for the Mayor, who won office with nearly 70 percent of the Hispanic vote and needs to do well again in a borough that has half a million Hispanic residents, nearly half its population. There is also dissatisfaction elsewhere in the borough, and local officials view the Mayor's visit cynically.
 
'Meaningless,' Critic Says
 "It's meaningless," said Councilman Michael DeMarco, who represents the northeast corner of the borough, which went to Mr. Dinkins's Republican rival, Rudolph W. Giuliani. "There's only so much cosmetics you can put on something to make it look good, and unfortunately, we don't look good right now.
"He thinks that by coming to the Bronx and putting on all this schmaltzy stuff, he's going to make a difference. It's like a woman. How much lipstick can you put on her?"
As the Mayor did on his stay in Brooklyn last month, he will hold his daily news conferences in the Bronx, visit neighborhood schools, meet with local politicians, stop by a police precinct and take walking tours of shopping strips. He plans a week in each of the boroughs outside of Manhattan and in a Manhattan neighborhood away from City Hall.
The Bronx became a required stop on political itineraries after President Jimmy Carter swept Mayor Abraham D. Beame to the rubble-strewn remains of Charlotte Street in the South Bronx in 1977, vowing to rebuild. There has been some rebirth since, but Mr. Dinkins never tires of noting that Federal aid has shrunk from 19 percent of the city's budget a decade ago to under 10 percent now.
"I hope the fact that we're in the Bronx will not make the Bronx a presidential photo-op for candidates, but that they will take the opportunity to truly focus on the problems of urban America," Deputy Mayor Bill Lynch said last week. "We want to saturate the borough and highlight some of the positive things going on."
 
Unhappy Hispanic Voters
 But the Mayor will have to do more than that to repair his political coalition, especially his standing with some Hispanic voters who have been unhappy with his appointments and accused him of insensitivity to their concerns. Mr. Ferrer is sometimes rumored as a potential challenger -- or, more likely, a candidate for City Council President on a mayoral ticket with Mr. Dinkins's archrival, Andrew J. Stein -- though Mr. Ferrer said, "I can't conceive of the Mayor viewing me as a threat."
Still, Angelo Falcon, head of the Institute for Puerto Rican Policy, a nonprofit group, said Mr. Dinkins is "definitely in trouble in terms of the Latino community."
"We just did a poll of 200 Puerto Rican community activists, and found for the most part very negative feelings toward the Mayor," he said.
 
Divergent Views
 "Unfortunately, it's so close to re-election time that anything he does now is suspect, because people will just dismiss it," Mr. Falcon said.
One thing is clear: How one views these mayoral field trips depends on where one sits. After the Mayor's week in Brooklyn, the Park Slope Paper, a weekly in a liberal neighborhood Mr. Dinkins won last time, asked editorially: "Did we learn anything about the Mayor? Yes -- that he hasn't a clue as to who we are or what he's supposed to do to make things better."
But the Amsterdam News, the Harlem weekly, said, "The Mayor was given a tumultuous welcome in practically every ethnic neighborhood he visited."

HEADLINE: Fighting AIDS, and Fighting One Another;
As the Battleground Shifts, Old and Newer Groups Vie for Scarce Funds

BYLINE: By MIREYA NAVARRO

 BODY:
With its staff of one, Mothers of Children With AIDS operates out of a donated cubicle in a church office. Its brochure was the gift of an advertising agency and its incorporation papers were filed by lawyers working without charge.
Gay Men's Health Crisis also relies on donations and volunteers, to provide a wide range of services to people who suffer the symptoms of H.I.V., the virus that causes AIDS. But G.M.H.C., formed in 1982 by six gay men, has grown with the epidemic to enter its second decade with a staff of 200 and an annual budget of $20 million. 
 The two groups, despite vast differences in resources and constituencies, come under the same rubric: AIDS organizations. And both are after the same scarce dollars.
 
New AIDS Cases
 They now stand at the crux of a difficult question brought on by stagnant government budgets and hard economic times: whether established AIDS groups should lose some of their funds to new, smaller organizations rooted in black and Hispanic communities, where many of the new AIDS cases are found.
The issue has come to a head in New York State, where officials say the tentative budget of the Department of Health's AIDS Institute for the next fiscal year falls far short of what is needed. The institute has proposed reducing the grants of some traditional recipients, like Gay Men's Health Crisis, to expand existing AIDS programs and finance several new ones geared to minorities, like Mothers of Children With AIDS. The plan has led to open warfare among AIDS organizations, with Hispanic and black groups publicly denouncing G.M.H.C. and other large programs for resisting any cuts.
With the exception of G.M.H.C. -- which raises 85 percent of its funds from private sources -- both large and small AIDS programs depend on government financing for most of their budgets.
The rift has underscored the diversity of those involved in fighting AIDS and exposed the imbalance in resources and political clout among the groups. And the conflict goes beyond one needy group's being pitted against another; it is likely to determine what services are offered to those infected with H.I.V., and who gets help.
The needs of the H.I.V.-infected population -- the largest groups are gay men, intravenous drug users, and black and Hispanic women in poor communities, many of them mothers whose children are also infected -- can be vastly different. More important, experts say prevention and social services may be more effectively delivered by groups attuned to the communities being served. And AIDS Institute officials say that small organizations can sometimes fill that role better than larger groups, even those which already serve a large percentage of minority clients.
 
A National Problem
 AIDS Institute officials say they still hope more money will come out of last-minute budget negotiations, relieving them of the painful task of redistribution.
As of yesterday, institute officials said they expected to receive $55.6 million for the coming fiscal year, up from $51.9 million this year. A total of $371.9 million in state AIDS spending is budgeted for the coming year, mostly for Medicaid. But the increase is not keeping pace with the needs created by AIDS, and the same problem is appearing nationwide, Federal officials and AIDS groups say.
"It's going to be more and more difficult every year," said Gary West, assistant deputy director of the National Center for Prevention Services within the Federal Centers for Disease Control. The center experienced a budget cut this year and anticipates no increase next year.
"Even if we have level funding, level funding actually means reduced services because there is inflation and the demand for services continue to increase," he said.
 
Emphasizing Prevention
 Faced with a budget squeeze, New York State health officials are basing the distribution of funds on the changing pattern of the AIDS epidemic. Early on, reported cases stemming from homosexual and bisexual activity were a majority in New York State, but that proportion has declined to 42 percent. In the meantime, cases stemming from drug use rose to 42 percent, from 31 percent in 1986, and black and Hispanic residents now account for 63 percent of all cases in the state, health officials say.
The AIDS Institute says it will review the 400 programs it finances -- including primary care services in hospitals and education and prevention programs -- to insure a fair distribution.
AIDS organizations that serve different populations sometimes have conflicting interests. For instance, groups based in minority neighborhoods usually place far more emphasis on prevention. They say these programs are particularly crucial in their communities where there is still great ignorance about how to avoid H.I.V. infection.
"For the gay community, care is the central issue because there are so many infected," said Paul A. Kawata, executive director of the National Minority AIDS Council in Washington, D.C., which represents 300 organizations. "But in communities of color we still have the opportunity to prevent disease."
Groups that stand to gain from any redistribution of AIDS Institute money say they would run more prevention programs, including giving clean needles to intravenous drug users. They also say that minority-run programs will be more effective in minority communities.
 
Children With AIDS
 "It has a lot to do with acceptability, not expertise," said Paul Jellinek, vice president of the Robert Wood Johnson Foundation in Princeton, N.J., which three years ago gave $17 million to 54 groups for AIDS projects.
Some of the groups offer programs fine-tuned to the needs of the emerging AIDS population. For instance, Mothers of Children With AIDS, created by a woman who lost her daughter to AIDS last February, aims to help Hispanic women who care for someone with the disease, often coping with their own infection at the same time.
But some advocates worry that redistributing funds is a bad idea, simply because it suggests that a shift of money, not an increase in spending, will solve the problem.
"It lets the Federal government off the hook," said Dan Broff, executive director of the AIDS Action Council, a lobbying group in Washington that represents 550 organizations. "Our top priority is more money. Had we spent money early on in the epidemic, the needs wouldn't be as great now."
 
Oldest and Largest Group
 G.M.H.C. officials say any cut in public funds would hurt their clients. Already, they say, their public financing has not kept pace with the rising demand for services. The bulk of the agency's money comes from private donations and from big fund-raisers, like the Ringling Bros. and Barnum & Bailey "Circus for Life" held at Madison Square Garden last Friday.
The oldest and largest AIDS organization in the country, Gay Men's Health Crisis uses its $1.6 million AIDS Institute grant, one of the largest awarded, for services ranging from help with daily chores to legal services for its 3,300 clients, 78 percent of whom are gay.
David A. Hansell, the group's deputy executive director for policy, said part of the answer to the financing problem is to mobilize black and Hispanic communities, including their more affluent segments, to raise money privately, as gay groups did in the early part of the epidemic.
"Let's not assume that we've exhausted all the money there is for AIDS," he said.
But while lauding gay organizations for their pioneering efforts, some say that the gay response will be hard to emulate, if only because of the generally higher income and education levels in the gay community. Many minority groups also say they are competing for a slice of the pie at a huge disadvantage because the established organizations can pay lobbyists and enjoy the name recognition that make fund-raisers like G.M.H.C.'s successful. They also have a proven track record.
 
Reaching Out to Other Groups
 As the giant that dwarfs all others, however, Gay Men's Health Crisis has also felt a responsibility to help others. It gives 15 percent of the $4 million raised by the annual AIDS Walk to other AIDS groups and it shares its technical expertise, for instance.
The organization has also made an effort to reach clients who are not gay. While it does not accept active drug users, 8 percent of its current clients are women; 20 percent are former drug addicts, and 41 percent are black or Hispanic.
 
Difficult Task
 G.H.M.C. and 13 other AIDS groups, which represent about 4 percent of the applicants but receive 25 percent of the AIDS Institute appropriations, have urged legislators to finance other community groups, but not by taking money away from them. Meanwhile, minority organizations are lobbying for redistribution not only of state money but of AIDS-related grants by the Federal government and the city's Department of Health.
AIDS Institute officials say that the task of reviewing programs will not be easy.
"The H.I.V. virus continues to burn through disadvantaged minority communities while it continues to ravage the gay community," Dr. Nicholas A. Rango, director of the AIDS Institute, said. "The pain experienced by those of us making funding decisions is caused by the failure of every level of government to take prevention seriously and to fund adequately for services going to those already infected."

SECTION: Section A; Page 20; Column 1; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 432 words

HEADLINE: Albany Races Its Budget Deadline

 BODY:
Despite a last-minute furor over school aid, leaders in Albany might still make their deadline with a harsh but plausible New York State budget. Could they do better? Yes. But the draft agreement relies on fewer gimmicks than usual and avoids huge tax increases that would hurt during a recession. And it could come in under the wire for the first time since 1984.
Frantic efforts to make last-minute changes before tomorrow's deadline have created chaos in Albany. If more time would produce a better budget, a delay would surely be warranted. But there's no reason to believe that waiting would improve the substance of the budget. Though flawed, an on-time budget might give a weary public some confidence in the ability of public officials to do their jobs. And local governments and school districts wouldn't have to borrow, at high interest rates, to make up for missing state aid. 
 Still, the budget under negotiation has many shortcomings. It's clear that some of the cuts will be counterproductive, costing more in the long run than they save up front. It's also clear that, as usual, the state failed to make the kinds of fundamental changes that would result in more efficient government and permanent savings.
The deal does not, for instance, restructure the school aid formula that still favors more affluent districts over poorer areas. Nor does it provide for fundamental Medicaid reforms or revamp mass transit formulas to give cities the same shake they give the suburbs. Such changes won't emerge by tomorrow. They take time, leadership and commitment -- none of which are in large supply in Albany.
But state lawmakers can still restore cuts that, as Mayor David Dinkins of New York City urgently argues, are damaging to big-city residents, particularly the poor. And legislators can make needed corrections during the legislative process.
The budget defines the state's agenda for the year. But no budget is set in concrete. Some of New York City's education aid could be restored before the deadline, but how much? And a damaging cap on aid for foster care, still in the budget deal, could cost the city up to $44 million. The budget also requires the city to provide $25 million more than it originally anticipated to keep the transit fare at $1.25. And municipal hospitals will no doubt have to make up for cuts and limits in home nursing care and home relief.
The state's options are limited by the economy and a growing budget deficit. But easing some burdens on the cities, either now or later, could make a difficult budget season less painful.
