HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Candidate's Record;
Clinton Remakes Home State in Own Image

SERIES: Clinton as Governor: 12 Years at Arkansas' Helm -- First of five articles

BYLINE: By JOEL BRINKLEY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LITTLE ROCK, Ark., March 26

 BODY:
When he was running for his fifth term as Governor two years ago, Bill Clinton made a promise to the people of Arkansas: He was not going to run for President in 1992.
"It was almost a 'read my lips' kind of thing," one local reporter recalled. " 'I will not run.' " 
 A year and a half later, Mr. Clinton changed his mind. He went back to the people of Arkansas, confessed to his changed view and in town meetings across the state asked their permission to enter the race for President.
In Mr. Clinton's view, the answer he heard, in town after little town, was a loud yes.
That's not surprising because during his 12 years in office, in many ways Mr. Clinton has remade Arkansans in his own image: a people easily forgiving of past problems, proud of what has been accomplished, eager to move forward, even if what has been done so far is flawed or incomplete.
With Bill Clinton, as with Jimmy Carter in the spring of 1976, the Democratic Party is considering a Southern Governor as its nominee. The party appears ready to accept both the promise and problems that come with this choice, for in Arkansas, as in much of the South, the requirements for political success and the standards by which candidates are judged on the state level often are less severe than those familiar to the nation's broader electorate as the considers candidates for President.
Indeed, Mr. Clinton's record mirrors that of a modern Southern progressive. It blends several paradoxes, for example, a populist sympathy for the underprivileged along with a cozy, low-tax relationship with his state's economic powers; a commitment to racial justice along with a basic cultural conservatism; a belief in activist government along with fiscal conservatism.
A review of Mr. Clinton's record as Arkansas's longest-serving Governor turns on two pivotal conclusions.
Mr. Clinton can take justifiable pride in moving the state forward in a number of areas that had long been ignored before. Creating more jobs and improving the state's schools are the largest examples. And out of that he has also helped give residents of Arkansas, historically among the poorest in the nation, a warmer view of themselves.
"There's kind of a feeling of pride across the state now, that we've done something worthwhile, helping ourselves along," said John E. Miller, a member of the Arkansas House for the last 34 years.
But the other salient fact is that Mr. Clinton tried to reform one of the nation's weakest, most impoverished states, notably more depressed than even some of the poor Southern states surrounding it.
To overcome that, sometimes he took shortcuts. And that has given him a reputation for misleading legislators in ways that left some of them bitter and others admiring of his political dexterity.
Trying to move Arkansas out of its historic and endemic state of poverty, he grew close to the state's barons of industry, as Southern politicians often do, so that now he is forced to confront increasing environmental problems and accusations of conflicts of interest.
To improve the schools and create jobs, he furthered the imbalance in state taxes, financing substantial tax breaks for business with added sales taxes on low-income people.
Property taxes, a traditional source of money for schools and other local projects in other states, are so low in Arkansas that sales taxes, again, make up the difference. That, the state Government explains, is because county governments, dominated by large landowners in many areas, refuse to raise the property tax rates.
 
Modest Gains
Arkansans Admire Their Governor
 By many important measures, the state is little better off today than when Mr. Clinton first took office 12 years ago.
As an example, the per capita income of Arkansans, among the lowest in the nation as long as the statistics have been collected, has not risen. It has even fallen back a tiny bit, when compared with the other states.
Still, even with the policy failures and vocal enemies that every governor inevitably picks up after 12 years in office, the state's election results and recent public opinion surveys show that Arkansans like and admire their Governor.
They are impressed by his grasp of the issues that affect them; he displays that knowledge almost every time he speaks. Most people do not seem to care that his enemies call him "slick Willie" or that the national press continues to turn up accusations of impropriety and potential scandal.
As soon as Mr. Clinton was declared the winner in presidential primaries over the last few weeks, some Arkansans out on the streets raised their arms and hollered a cheer, as if the hometown basketball team had just won the big game.
In earlier years, before he was a Presidential contender, local political analysts say, his forays into national politics through the National Governor's Conference and other Democratic Party positions made many Arkansans feel proud, not abandoned -- the local boy made good.
And he has been a local boy. Even with his two years at Oxford and his Ivy League law degree, Mr. Clinton is an authentic Arkansan, and so are nearly all of the people he has chosen to work around him. Many of his advisers are friends or political allies; some are former opponents. But nearly all are from Arkansas. And in this state, that alone guarantees a measure of support.
 
Low Expectations
 Though the state has elected several progressive governors in its recent past, Arkansans are a people with particularly low expectations of government.
After all, "people settled here originally to get away from government and taxes and other people -- just to be left alone," said Cal Ledbetter Jr., a professor of political science at the University of Arkansas.
Most Arkansans were aware that Arkansas ranked last or near last in the nation by most measures of quality of life in 1980, when Mr. Clinton took office. They know the state remains there today.
But Mike Gauldin, a Clinton aide, says he is reciting a common piece of Arkansas philosophy when he says: "It took us 150 years to get in this spot, and it might take a good long time to get out of it."
When Mr. Clinton began his first term, he promised his people: "We can fashion a life that will be the envy of the nation." Today, by common consent, Arkansas still has a long way to go, but most people here give him a great deal of credit just for trying.
John Brummett, the editor of Arkansas Times magazine and an occasional Clinton critic, said, "There's a clear perception here that, under Clinton, we have outperformed other states."
Mr. Miller said: "We'd been at the bottom so long, and we really have come a long way. You listen in the coffee shops and talk to people in the streets, and you see a general feeling around the state that we're better off now than we were 10 years ago."
Still, according to the most recent listings by the Southern Regional Council in Atlanta, a private research group, Arkansas ranked 48th in the nation in 1990 when comparing unemployment rates, 50th in the employment of young people, 49th in average weekly wages, 46th when comparing how many people have health insurance and 49th in the overall climate for workers.
Mr. Miller said, "We've always said 'thank God for Mississippi,' " the one state that usually ranks lower than Arkansas, "and some people really took pride in that. But there is no pride when you really are at the bottom."
An examination by The New York Times of Mr. Clinton's record on several major areas that he would have to tackle if he were President found that his administration has made concerted efforts in several areas that had long been ignored.
But over the same period, the examination showed, other states made improvements, too. As a result, in many areas Mr. Clinton's efforts have simply insured that his state did not fall even farther behind.
 
Tall Obstacles
State Has History Of Limiting Power
 All of Arkansas's governors have had to overcome a host of formidable obstacles to effect change. Arkansas was founded 150 years ago as a haven from strong government, excessive taxation and "too many laws," as Mr. Ledbetter put it. Vestiges of that tradition remain.
In the decades before the state enacted its first constitution 118 years ago, Arkansas was governed by corrupt, feuding family hierarchies that plundered whatever wealth was available, provided no services, drove the citizenry deeper into poverty with every passing year and often chose their leaders by murder and duel.
So by the time state Government was formalized, "whatever benefits the average Arkansan had enjoyed from the society and the resources of the state he had gotten from his own efforts, from the local community or from the Federal Government -- and not from the state," a historian, Donald Stokes, wrote in a doctoral dissertation about Arkansas's political development.
With that background, Arkansas enacted a constitution in 1874 that strictly limited the Governor's powers.
 
Diluted Authority
 In her book "Arkansas Politics and Government," Diane D. Blair, a political scientist, noted that "a two-year term, a meager salary specified in the constitution itself, executive power divided between the Governor and other separately elected executives and a veto that could be overridden by a simple majority of the legislature" all combined to dilute the Governor's authority.
With few powers and even fewer expectations, state government did little to improve the people's lot during the next 90 years. In 1900 the Arkansas school year was just 69 days long, as against a national average of 132 days. By 1930 only 8.1 percent of the state's students went on to high school, and 53 percent of the state's farms were farmed by tenants.
Beginning with the election of Winthrop Rockefeller in 1966, Arkansans selected a series of progressive Governors who began trying to turn the state's fortunes around. But given the built-in obstacles, their records were spotty.
Bill Bowen, a retired banker who is Mr. Clinton's chief of staff, said he believed that Mr. Clinton came into office "a beneficiary" of that evolving tradition of office. "But on the downside," he added, "there were still a lot of things that needed to be done."
 
Uneasy Start
Reaching Too Far In His First Term
 By most accounts Mr. Clinton reached too far in his first term, beginning in 1979. He tried to do more than many Arkansans, still imbued with the state's leave-us-alone tradition, were willing to accept. And so he was defeated two years later.
"In his first term," Mr. Miller added, "the perception was out there that he was going to do what he wanted without consulting anybody."
John Lipton, speaker of the Arkansas House, said, "He had lot of maturing to do, and I've watched him do it."
His defeat "helped him toward accelerated maturity," Mr. Lipton added. And by the time he was re-elected two years later, the experience appeared to have altered his political personality.
To his political friends and allies, the change was that "he learned to listen," as Mr. Lipton put it. "He came to realize that to accomplish things, he had to have a cooperative spirit."
To his political foes, however, the Governor had turned into "slick Willie," a politician too eager to please.
"He became very casual about the truth," said Paul Greenberg, a newspaper columnist who has been one of Mr. Clinton's most persistent critics. He was one of the first to begin using the epithet "slick Willie." After Mr. Clinton's defeat in 1981, Mr. Greenberg said, the Governor acquired "a tendency to tell people whatever it is they wanted to hear."
 
'Obsession to Please'
 Mr. Brummett, the magazine editor, said, "I don't know if it's really slickness or indecisiveness or anguish. He tries not to say no to anyone. He has an obsession to please. For years, I've seen legislators from different sides of the same question come out of his office, all thinking he's on their side."
Mr. Lipton, one of those legislators and a supporter, dismissed that complaint, saying, "Any politician does a little of that."
Whether he is slick, indecisive or simply effective, Mr. Clinton has grown to be popular and relatively powerful, given the inherent limits of this office.
In the last few years, the Governor's salary has been increased, to $60,000 a year from $35,000, and the term in office lengthened from two to four years, removing two of the original constitutional restrictions on the Governor. But the two largest impediments to action remain. They are the state's enduring poverty, and the Governor's inability to raise income taxes to improve services without approval of two-thirds of the Legislature.
Under that stricture, Dr. Ledbetter notes, the income tax rate has been raised only once since 1928, and now the top rate is about 7 percent. With all that, Arkansas's governors generally exercise leadership largely through the force of personal persuasion, and Mr. Clinton has learned to excel at that.
Arkansas may still rank near last in the nation by most measures of quality of life. But even his critics believe the state might very well be in a position "where we'd really have to reach up to touch bottom," as one of them put it -- if not for the efforts of Mr. Clinton.
"In the environment of the 80's, many states made advances," Mr. Brummett said. "Given this state's history of anti-tax, anti-government populism, if not for what Clinton did, we'd be at the very bottom right now. No doubt. No doubt."

CORRECTION-DATE: April 1, 1992, Wednesday

 CORRECTION:
An article yesterday about Bill Clinton's record as governor of Arkansas misstated his salary. It is $35,000 a year. A proposal to increase it to $60,000, and to raise the salaries of other state officials has been approved by the state Legislature and the Governor. It will be put to the voters on a ballot in November.
The article also misstated the date that his first term in office began. It was 1979 not 1980.
